Dispute on Acknowledgment Explained as Price of Forbidden Items
Ruling on explanation as price of forbidden items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on whether an acknowledgment explained as from the price of wine is binding, and state the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
An explanation that the debt is from an invalid source may be a retraction — but does connectivity matter?

When a person acknowledges a debt but immediately explains it as from the price of wine or other prohibited items, does the acknowledgment stand? This lesson examines the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, focusing on the role of connectivity and the nature of the explanation as a retraction versus a clarification of intent.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify that Imam Abu Hanifa holds the acknowledgment binding regardless of whether the explanation is connected or disconnected. The explanation is rejected as a retraction.
This is the primary view presented in the text.
2
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that if the explanation is connected (muttasil), then nothing is binding; if disconnected (munfasil), the acknowledgment is binding like Abu Hanifa.
The key variable is connectivity.
3
Analyze the dalil of each side
Abu Hanifa argues that the price of forbidden items is not legally recognized, so the explanation contradicts the initial obligation and is a retraction. Sahibayn analogize to saying 'in sha' Allah' at the end, which negates firm intention.
The analogy to 'in sha' Allah' is critical for Sahibayn.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the muqir must pay; under Sahibayn, if the statement is continuous, the muqir owes nothing.
This affects actual court judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : كقوله : من ثمن خمر؛ وكذا من ثمن خنزير أو مال قمار، أو حرّ، أو ميتة، أو دم، فإنّه يلزمه الألف، ولم يقبل تفسيره عند الإمام، سواء كان موصولاً أو مفصولاً؛ لأنَّ هذا التفسير رجوع عن الإقرار؛ فإن ثمن الخمر أو الخنزير لا يكون واجباً، وأول كلامه وهو : له علي ألف درهم للوجوب. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد الله : إذا وصل لا يلزمه شيء؛ لأن المقر بين بآخر كلامه أنه لم يرد به الإيجاب، وصار كما إذا قال في آخر كلامه : إن شاء الله.
His statement: like his saying 'from the price of wine'; and similarly from the price of a pig, gambling money, a free person, carrion, or blood, then the thousand is binding upon him, and his explanation is not accepted according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), whether connected or disconnected; because this explanation is a retraction from the acknowledgment; indeed, the price of wine or pig is not obligatory, and his first statement 'I owe one thousand dirhams' indicates obligation. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: if he connects it, nothing is binding upon him; because the muqir clarified by his later statement that he did not intend obligation, and it becomes like his saying 'if Allah wills' at the end.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage extends the discussion to cases where the muqir explains the debt as from the price of something that is legally prohibited or valueless, such as wine (khamr), pig (khinzir), gambling money, a free person (cannot be sold), carrion, or blood. According to Abu Hanifa (Imam), regardless of whether the explanation is connected or disconnected, the acknowledgment of a thousand dirhams is binding, and the explanation is not accepted. The reason: these items cannot be sold for a valid price, so the explanation effectively retracts the acknowledgment. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if the explanation is connected (muttasil) to the acknowledgment, then nothing is binding because the muqir has clarified that he never intended to create an obligation; it is akin to saying 'in sha' Allah' at the end of a statement. The practical effect is that Abu Hanifa upholds the acknowledgment, while Sahibayn discharge the muqir if the exception is connected.

Key Terms
من ثمن خمر (min thaman khamr): An explanation that the acknowledged debt is from the price of wine, which is an invalid basis.
إن شاء الله (in shā’ Allāh): A phrase indicating lack of firm intention; used by Sahibayn as analogy for connected exceptions that negate obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a person says 'I owe you 1000 dirhams from the price of a pig' in a connected statement, what is the ruling?
A) The 1000 dirhams are binding.
B) Nothing is binding because the price of a pig is invalid.
C) It is like a conditional statement similar to 'in sha' Allah'.
D) The statement is invalid and no debt exists.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school disagrees on whether a connected explanation of an invalid price cancels the acknowledgment; the thamarah is the binding nature of the debt.

Exception in Confessions for Ghasp and Wadī'ah
Commentary on rulings of istithnā' in iqrār for ghasp and wadī'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exception in confessions for ghasp and wadī'ah, distinguish it from sale and loan, and apply the conditions of connection and excused disconnection.

Core Idea
In ghasp and wadī'ah, exception of quality (counterfeit) is valid even if disconnected; type exceptions (sattuqa) require connection.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi positions on istithnā' (exception) in iqrār (confession) when the confessed item is from ghasp or wadī'ah. The key is whether the exception changes the very type of property or merely describes a defect. The passage from 'Umadat ar-Ri'āyah clarifies the nuance and distinguishes these cases from sale and loan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Determine whether the confession is about a debt arising from ghasp, wadī'ah, sale, or loan. This determines the default quality of the dirhams.
Sale and loan default to good dirhams; ghasp and wadī'ah include all types.
2
Classify the exception
Is the exception about quality (e.g., counterfeit) or about type (e.g., sattuqa base metal)? Quality exceptions may be accepted even if disconnected; type exceptions require immediate connection.
Counterfeit is a defect; sattuqa is a different genus.
3
Apply the connection rule
For quality exceptions in ghasp/wadī'ah, the confessor is believed whether connected or separated. For type exceptions and for all exceptions in sale/loan, connection is required.
Excused disconnection (e.g., breathlessness) is accepted per Abu Yusuf.
4
Analyze the proof
The reasoning distinguishes between quantity and quality: quantity can be excepted because it is part of the subject; quality cannot be excepted because it is not included in the absolute term.
Quality is established by custom, not by the word 'dirham'.
5
Note the exception for excused disconnection
Abu Yusuf holds that if disconnection occurs due to unavoidable reasons (e.g., coughing), the exception remains valid. This is the mufta bihi position.
Avoids hardship; cited from al-Tabyīn.

And regarding: [the case of] ghasp (usurpation) or deposit – if one claims one of these, he is believed except when there is a separation in the last two [cases]: that is, if he said: 'I owe him a thousand [dirhams] from ghasp or deposit, except that they are counterfeit or worthless (nabaḥrajah),' he is believed whether he connects or separates [the exception]. And the sattuqa is [called dirham] metaphorically; because sattuqa are called dirhams metaphorically, but the absolute term dirham refers to good [coins] because people's transactions are normally with good coins. So his saying 'they are counterfeit' is a statement changing [the meaning] from this aspect, therefore connection is required, and it becomes like when he says 'except that they weigh five [dirhams]' while the currency of their town weighs seven – if connected he is believed, otherwise not. The answer: This is not a statement changing [the meaning] as they claimed; rather it is a retraction of the confession as previously, and the analogy has a distinction: his saying 'except that they weigh five' is not from our case because it can be a valid exception since it is a quantity, and exception of part of the quantity is valid because the first part of the speech includes the quantity, so the exception of the mentioned [part] is valid, unlike quality (jūdah), for it is a description, and exception of description is not valid because it is not included in the term dirham so that it could be excepted; rather, the quality of being good is established in the absolute contract by custom and habit. If you say: But description may be excepted as when he says 'I owe him a kor of wheat from the price of a slave except that it is inferior (radī'ah),' for inferiority is opposite of quality, so both are descriptions that follow upon one subject. We say: We do not accept that inferiority is a defect; rather it is a type, for a defect is what is lacking from the original creation, and wheat may be inferior by original nature; so it is in the sense of clarifying the type, and the absolute contract does not necessitate one type over another; therefore the purchase of wheat is not valid unless one specifies whether it is good, medium, or inferior. Do you not see that if he said 'I sold you this wheat' and pointed to it, and the buyer had seen it and found it inferior but did not know it [before], he does not have the option of sight due to defect (khiyār ar-ru'yah bil-'ayb); but if he said 'I sold you [something] with these dirhams' and pointed to them while they were counterfeit and the seller was unaware, he [the buyer] is entitled to their like, good ones without counterfeitness. So it is known that counterfeitness is a defect, and inferiority is a type, so they differ.

Book of Iqrār, Chapter of Exception and Iqrār of the Sick: And he is believed in: 'I usurped a garment' and he comes with a defective one, and in: 'I owe him a thousand dirhams except that it lacks such and such' if connected, and if separated, no. And if he said: 'I took from you a thousand as deposit and it perished' and he said: 'it was sattuqa or lead', then if connected he is believed, if separated no. The difference between sale and loan and between ghasp and deposit is that the first two [sale/loan] are concluded with good [coins], so if he interprets the dirhams as other than good, it is a retraction; while ghasp and deposit occur with all that [i.e., any type], and sattuqa and lead are not of the genus of dirhams, but are called dirhams metaphorically, so it is a statement changing [the meaning] – if connected he is believed, if separated no. (And he is believed in: 'I usurped a garment' and comes with a defective one, and in: 'I owe him a thousand dirhams except that it lacks such and such' if connected, if separated no, because exception is valid connected not separated. (And if he said: 'I took from you a thousand deposit and it perished' and he said: 'it was sattuqa or lead' – [he is believed] because ghasp and deposit occur with all that, as previously, so it is not a retraction but rather a clarification of the type, so he is believed absolutely. [1] His statement: 'and if separated no' – i.e., he is not believed because sattuqa and lead are not of the genus of dirhams, except that the name dirham includes them metaphorically, so it is a changing statement, and it is only accepted if connected, so connection is necessary. [2] His statement: 'he is believed... etc.' – i.e., if he said: 'I usurped a garment' and came with a defective garment, the confessor is believed with an oath if the claimant does not prove its soundness, because it has been mentioned many times that ghasp is not restricted to sound [items], like deposit. (3) His statement: 'and in: I owe him... etc.' – i.e., if he said: 'I owe him a thousand dirhams except that it lacks a hundred dirhams' for example, he is believed if connected, otherwise the thousand is binding because exception is permitted only connected, not separated. And if the separation was due to interruption of breath or due to a fit of coughing, it is related from Abu Yusuf that it is valid if he connects it to it, and upon this is the fatwa, because a person needs to speak many words and mention the exception at the end, and he cannot speak all of that in one breath, and if an excuse is not made, it would cause hardship, and upon this is the fatwa. Thus in al-Tabyīn by al-ʿAllāmah al-Zaylaʿī. [4] His statement: 'I took from you... etc.' – i.e., if the confessor said to the claimant: 'I took from you a thousand dirhams...'
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the rulings of exception (istithnā') in confessions (iqrār), specifically concerning cases of usurpation (ghasp) and deposit (wadī'ah). The author first states the general rule: when a person confesses to a debt arising from ghasp or wadī'ah but claims the dirhams are counterfeit or worthless, he is believed regardless of whether he connects the exception immediately (waṣl) or separates it (faṣl). However, for the last two categories (ghasp and wadī'ah) he qualifies: he is believed unless there is a separation, meaning if he confesses to a specific type (e.g., from ghasp) and then later adds the exception, if disconnected, he is not believed. The reasoning is that the term 'dirham' in absolute terms is understood as good coins (jiyād) due to customary usage in transactions. Therefore, claiming they are counterfeit is a change in the nature of the confession, and such a change is only accepted if connected to the confession. The author then draws an analogy with an exception regarding weight (e.g., 'except that they weigh five' when the standard is seven). In that case, also connection is required. However, the opponent argues that both are similar, but the commentator distinguishes: weight is a quantity that is included in the first statement, while quality is a description that is not. He then addresses an objection about wheat: if someone confesses to owing a specific quantity of wheat and excepts inferior quality, that is valid because inferiority is considered a type, not a defect. In contrast, counterfeitness of dirhams is a defect. The passage then moves to other rulings: if a person confesses 'I usurped a garment' and brings a defective one, he is believed; similarly, 'I owe him a thousand dirhams except that it lacks such and such' is accepted if connected. The key distinction is made between sale/loan and ghasp/wadī'ah: in sale/loan, the default is good coins, so interpreting them otherwise constitutes a retraction, whereas in ghasp/wadī'ah, all types are possible. Finally, the commentator notes a fatwa from Abu Yusuf that if disconnection occurs due to inability to speak in one breath (e.g., coughing), the exception is still valid, and this is the relied-upon position to avoid hardship.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): An exception made by a person making a confession, excluding part of what was confessed.
الغصب (al-ghasp): Wrongful usurpation of property, creating a liability for restitution.
الوديعة (al-wadī'ah): Deposit made for safekeeping; the depositary may return the same item or its value.
متصلاً (muttaṣilan): Connected immediately in speech, without a significant pause.
منفصلاً (munfaṣilan): Separated by a pause or later speech, invalidating the exception.
الستوقة (as-sattuqah): Base metal coins that are not silver, considered dirhams metaphorically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a confession of a debt from ghasp, if the confessor says 'I owe you 1000 dirhams from ghasp, except they are counterfeit' and he disconnects the exception (pauses before 'except'), what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The exception is accepted and he owes only counterfeit dirhams.
B) The exception is not accepted and he owes good dirhams.
C) The exception is accepted only if proven by clear evidence.
D) The exception is accepted only if the creditor agrees.

Key Takeaway
In confessions for ghasp and wadī'ah, the default includes all qualities, so exception of quality is valid even if disconnected; but for type changes (sattuqa) and in sale/loan, connection is required. Excused disconnection is accepted per Abu Yusuf.

Connected and Disconnected Exceptions in Confessions
Ruling on connected and disconnected exceptions in confessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for connected vs disconnected exceptions in confessions and apply it to cases involving excuses such as coughing.

Core Idea
In confessions, only a connected exception is accepted; a disconnected one is a retraction, unless excused by necessity.

This passage from the Chapter on Confessions clarifies when an exception (istithnā’) following a confession is valid. The general rule is that the exception must be connected (muttaṣil) to the confession; if disconnected (munfaṣil), it is treated as a retraction. However, there is an important facilitation: if the disconnection is due to a valid excuse like catching breath or coughing, Abū Yūsuf’s view—that it is still valid—is the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its components
Recognize that the act is a confession (iqrār) followed by an exception (istithnā’). The exception qualifies the confession, e.g., ‘I owe you a thousand dirhams except that it is deficient by a hundred.’
2
State the general rule for connection
The exception must be connected (muttaṣil) to the confession without any break. A connected exception is accepted; a disconnected exception invalidates the exception and the full confession stands.
This is based on the principle that a disconnected exception is seen as a retraction or new statement.
3
Analyze the examples given
Study the examples: (1) confessing to ghaṣb and bringing a defective cloth; (2) confessing a debt of a thousand dirhams and immediately stating a deficiency; (3) confessing a deposit of a thousand dirhams and then explaining they were broken or lead. In each, if the exception is connected, the confessor is believed; if disconnected, he is not.
The distinction between sale/loan and ghaṣb/deposit is key: in the former, explaining poor quality is a retraction; in the latter, it is a clarification of type.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on excuses
Note the general rule requires immediate connection. However, Abū Yūsuf allows a disconnect if it is due to a valid excuse such as needing to catch a breath or coughing. The fatwa follows Abū Yūsuf’s view to avoid hardship.
This is an istiḥsān (juristic preference) to ease the burden on the confessor.
5
Apply the rule to a practical scenario
Given a scenario where a confessor says ‘I owe you 500 dirhams’, then coughs, then says ‘except that 100 are broken’, the exception is valid because of the excuse, following the muftā bihī position.
The confessor is believed with an oath unless the opponent proves otherwise.

And he is believed if he says: I grabbed a cloth and brought it defective, and if he says: I owe him a thousand dirhams except that it is deficient by so much connectedly, but if disconnectedly no. And if he says: I took from you a thousand as a deposit and it perished, and if he says: broken coins or lead, if connected he is believed, if disconnected no. The difference between sale and loan and between ghasb and deposit is that the first two occur on good quality, so if he explains the dirhams as other than good it is a retraction, while ghasb and deposit occur on all that, and broken coins and lead are not of the genus of dirhams, they are called dirhams metaphorically, so it is a clarifying change: if connected he is believed, if disconnected no. And he is believed in: I grabbed a cloth and brought it defective, and in: I owe a thousand dirhams except it is deficient by so much connectedly, but if disconnectedly no, because connected exception is valid, disconnected is not. And if he said: I took from you a thousand as a deposit and it perished, and the deposit likewise, because a person grabs what he finds and deposits what he owns, as previously, so it is not a retraction but a clarification of type, so he is believed absolutely. [1] His saying: and if disconnected no, meaning he is not believed, because broken coins and lead are not of the genus of dirhams, but the name dirhams includes them metaphorically, so it is a modifying explanation which is only accepted if connected. [2] His saying: he is believed... etc., meaning if he says: I grabbed a cloth and brings a defective cloth, the confessor is believed with an oath unless the opponent proves its soundness, because as mentioned repeatedly, ghasb is not specific to sound goods like deposit. (3) His saying: and in: I owe him... etc., meaning if he says: I owe a thousand dirhams except it is deficient by a hundred dirhams for example, he is believed if connected, otherwise the thousand are binding, because exception is valid connectedly not disconnectedly. And if the disconnection was due to catching breath or coughing, according to Abu Yusuf he is believed if he connects it, and the fatwa is on this, because a person needs to speak many words and mention the exception at the end, and cannot speak all of that in one breath, and if not made an excuse it would be hardship, and the fatwa is on it. As in al-Tabyin by al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Iqrar deals with exceptions in confessions. The ruling is that a connected exception (istithna muttasil) is accepted, meaning the confessor's qualification is believed, but a disconnected exception (istithna munfasil) is not. The text illustrates this with several examples. First, if someone confesses to grabbing a cloth but brings a defective cloth, he is believed if the exception is connected. Similarly, if he confesses to owing a thousand dirhams but immediately says it is deficient by a certain amount, he is believed; but if he pauses, he is not. However, an exception disconnected due to a legitimate excuse like coughing is accepted according to Abu Yusuf, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. Second, if someone says he took a thousand dirhams as a deposit and it perished, then explains that the dirhams were broken or lead, he is believed if the explanation is connected, because broken and lead dirhams are not really dirhams but are called dirhams metaphorically. The distinction between sale/loan and ghasb/deposit is that in the former, the contract is assumed to be on good quality goods, so explaining them as defective is a retraction; but in ghasb and deposit, the possession can be of any quality, so the explanation is a clarification of type, not a retraction. The commentary elaborates on each point, emphasizing that the exception must be connected to be valid.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): An exception or qualification made immediately after a confession, modifying its scope.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Wrongful taking of another's property without permission.
الوديعة (al-wadi'a): A deposit; property entrusted to someone for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says 'I owe you a thousand dirhams' and then, after a pause due to coughing, says 'except that it is deficient by one hundred dirhams', according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, which of the following is correct?
A) He is not believed because the exception is disconnected.
B) He is believed because the disconnection was due to a valid excuse.
C) He is believed only if he swears an oath.
D) He is not believed because the exception is not connected to the confession.

Key Takeaway
A connected exception in a confession is valid; a disconnected one is a retraction unless excused, and the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf's concession for necessity.

Conflicting Claims: Ghasb vs. Wadi'a
Ruling on conflicting claims in ghasb vs. deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish two scenarios of conflicting claims in Hanafi fiqh and determine liability based on whether the confessor admitted taking the property.

Core Idea
Liability turns on whether the confessor admits taking—if he does, he must prove permission; if not, the claimant must prove ghasb.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a nuanced ruling on conflicting claims involving deposit (wadi'a) and wrongful taking (ghasb). The key is not what the parties call the transaction, but what the confessor admits about the act of taking. We will walk through two cases and the reasoning behind their different outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, the confessor says 'I took a thousand as a deposit' and the claimant says 'no, it was ghasb'. Second, the confessor says 'you gave me a thousand as a deposit' and the claimant says 'no, I grabbed it as ghasb'. Note the difference in the confessor's admission.
2
Analyze the admission in each case
In the first case, the confessor admits taking (al-akhdh), which is the cause of liability. In the second, he admits only that the claimant gave (al-i'ta'), which is an act of the claimant, not an admission of taking by the confessor.
The text explains that giving may be by takhliya (vacating) without actual grasp.
3
Apply the burden of proof principle
In the first case, because the confessor admits the cause of liability, he must prove immunity (permission). Since the claimant denies permission, the confessor's claim is unproven, so he is liable. In the second, the confessor denies the cause of liability, so the claimant must prove ghasb.
The rule: al-qawl li-man yunkir (the statement is of the denier) applies.
4
Understand the distinction from the loan scenario
The text contrasts with a case where the claimant says 'you took it as a loan'. There, both agree on permission, so the confessor is not automatically liable; the claimant must prove the contract of loan, which imposes liability. This reinforces that liability stems from the cause of taking, not just any admission.
5
Address the objection about giving implying taking
One might argue that admission of giving necessarily implies taking. The text refutes this: giving can be by takhliya, which does not require the recipient's physical taking. Even if it did, the implication is limited by necessity and does not extend to creating liability without evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الآخر : بل غصباً، ضَمِنَ ، وفي : أعطيتنيه وديعة، وقال الآخر : غصبتنيه لا : والفرق أن في الأَوَّلِ أقرَّ بوجوب الضمان ، وهو الأخذ، وفي الثاني : لم يُقِرَّ بذلك، بل الآخر يدعى عليه الغصب، وهو ينكره ، فالقول له. وديعة، فهلكت تلك الدراهم في يدي من غير تعد، وقال المقر له : لا بل أخذتها مني حال كونها غصباً، ضَمِنَ المقر ما أقرَّ بأخذه له ؛ لأنه أقرَّ بسبب الضمان وهوا ثمّ إنّه ادعى ما يوجب البراءة وهو الإذن بالأخذ ، والآخر ينكر، فالقول قوله مع يمينه. بخلاف ما إذا قال له المقر له : بل أخذتها قرضاً، حيث يكون القول للمقر له ؛ لأنهما تصادقا على أنّ الأخذ حصل بإذنه ، وهذا لا يوجب الضمان على الآخذ إلا بسبب الأخذ، باعتبار عقدِ الضمان ، فالمالك يدعي عليه العقد، وذلك ينكر، فالقول قول المنكر). [١] قوله : وفي أعطيتنيه وديعة...الخ ؛ يعني لو قال المقر : أعطيتني ألف درهم وديعة فهلكت، وقال الآخر : لا ، غصبت ذلك الألف منّي لا يضمن المقر؛ لأنه لم يقر الضمان، بل أقر بالإعطاء ، وهو فعلُ المقر له، فلا يكونُ مُقِرِّاً على نفسه بسبب الضمان، والمقر له يدعي عليه بسبب الضمان ، وهو ينكر، فكان القول قول المنكر. فإن قيل : إعطاؤه والدفعُ لا يكون إلا بقبضه ، فوجب أن يكون إقراراً بالقبض. أجيب : بأن الدفع قد يكون بالتخلية والوضع بين يديه، ولـو اقتـضـى ذلـك فالمقتضي لا عموم له ؛ لأنه ثابت ضرورة، فيتقدَّرُ بقدر الضرورة، فلا يظهر في حق انعقادِهِ سبباً للضمان ؛ لأنه عدم في حق غيره (۳). [٢] قوله : والفرق...الخ؛ حاصلُ الفرق أنَّ في المسألة الأولى وهـي مـا إذا قال : أخذتُ منك ألفاً...الخ، أقر المقر بموجب الضمان، وهو الأخذ، ثمّ ادّعى المقر موجب البراءة وهو الإذن، والمقر له ينكره، والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه. وفي المسألة الثانية، وهي ما إذا قال : أعطيتنيه وديعة...الخ أضاف المقر الفعل إلى غيره، وذلك الغير يدعي عليه بسبب الضمان وهو الغصب، فكان القولُ لمنكر.
And the other said: rather as a ghasb, he is liable. And in: You gave it to me as a deposit, and the other said: you grabbed it from me, he is not liable. The difference is that in the first, he admitted the obligation of liability, which is the taking; in the second, he did not admit that, rather the other claims ghasb against him, and he denies it, so the statement is his. And if he says: I took a thousand from you as a deposit and it perished in my hand without transgression, and the confessee says: no, you took it from me as a ghasb, then the confessor is liable for what he admitted taking, because he admitted the cause of liability which is the taking, then he claimed what necessitates immunity, which is permission to take, and the other denies, so the statement is his with his oath. Contrary to if the confessee says: rather you took it as a loan, where the statement is of the confessee, because they both agree that the taking occurred with his permission, and this does not impose liability on the taker except due to the taking, considering the contract of liability, so the owner claims the contract, and he denies, so the statement is of the denier. [1] His saying: and in: You gave it to me as a deposit... etc., means if the confessor says: you gave me a thousand dirhams as a deposit and it perished, and the other says: no, you grabbed that thousand from me, then the confessor is not liable because he did not admit liability, rather he admitted giving, which is an act of the confessee, so he is not admitting against himself a cause of liability, and the confessee claims liability against him, and he denies, so the statement is of the denier. If it is said: Giving and payment cannot be without taking, so it must be an admission of taking. The answer is that payment may be by vacating and placing before him, and even if that implies it, the implication is not general because it is established by necessity, so it is limited to the extent of necessity, thus it does not appear in respect of its being a cause of liability because it is absent in respect of others. [2] His saying: and the difference... etc.; the conclusion of the difference is that in the first case, where he said: I took a thousand from you... etc., the confessor admitted the requirement of liability, which is the taking, then he claimed the cause of immunity, which is permission, and the confessee denies it, so the statement is of the denier with his oath. In the second case, where he said: you gave it to me as a deposit... etc., the confessor attributed the act to someone else, and that other claims liability against him, which is ghasb, so the statement is of the denier.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage deals with two scenarios where the parties disagree about the nature of a transaction. In the first scenario, the confessor says 'I took a thousand dirhams from you as a deposit and it perished' and the confessee says 'no, you took it as a ghasb'. Here, the confessor is liable because he admitted taking the property, which is the cause of liability (daman). He then claims that the taking was with permission (as a deposit), but the confessee denies that permission, so the confessor must prove permission or be liable. The ruling is that the confessor is liable with his oath (since he admits taking but claims immunity, and the burden is on him). In the second scenario, the confessor says 'You gave me a thousand dirhams as a deposit and it perished' and the confessee says 'no, you grabbed it as a ghasb'. Here, the confessor is not liable because he did not admit taking; he attributed the action to the confessee (giving). Since he denies ghasb, and the confessee claims it, the burden is on the confessee to prove ghasb. The distinction is based on who admits what: in the first case, the confessor admits an act that implies liability (taking); in the second, he admits only the other party's act (giving). The commentary also addresses an objection: if giving implies taking, then admission of giving should imply admission of taking. The answer is that giving can be by constructive delivery (takhliya), which does not necessarily entail actual possession by the recipient in a way that creates liability. Therefore, the admission of giving does not automatically create liability. This is a nuanced rule in Hanafi fiqh about the effect of admissions.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Liability or indemnity for property; in this context, the obligation to compensate for taken property.
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission; the confessor claims he took with permission, which would negate liability for ghasb.
التخلية (al-takhliya): Vacating possession; a form of delivery where the owner leaves the property for the other to take, not necessarily requiring physical grasp.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where someone says 'You gave me a thousand dirhams as a deposit and it perished', and the other says 'No, you grabbed it as a ghasb', what is the ruling according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The confessor is liable because he admitted receiving the property.
B) The confessor is not liable because he did not admit taking.
C) The confessee must prove the ghasb claim.
D) Both B and C are correct.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the admission of taking creates a presumption of liability, shifting the burden to the confessor to prove permission; admission of giving does not.

Confessor's Claim in Deposit vs. Rental – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on intra-Hanafi dispute over confessor's claim in deposit and rental

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi positions on the confessor's claim in deposit and rental, explain the basis of istihsan as distinguished from qiyas, and apply the ruling to new scenarios.

Core Idea
The nature of possession determines whether a confessor's claim is accepted: proprietary hand vs. necessary hand.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of admitting another's hand (yad). The central question: when a person confesses that another had possession of an item, can he later claim ownership of that item without proof? Imam Abu Hanifa distinguishes between situations where the hand is proprietary (like deposit) and where the hand is merely necessary for benefit (like rental). His two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, apply analogy uniformly, requiring the confessor to hand over the item first. The commentary explains the reasoning and provides an important exception from Al-Kifayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and distinguish between the deposit case (the confessor says 'This was a deposit with you, I took it back') and the rental/lending case (the confessor says 'I rented my horse to you, you used it and returned it'). Note the different rulings.
The deposit case involves proprietary hand; the rental case involves necessary hand.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa: In deposit, the confessor must hand over the item to the confessee and then sue. In rental/lending, the confessor is believed without handing over first. Understand the reasoning: in rental, the hand is only for benefit, not ownership.
The confessor's admission of hand in rental does not imply ownership.
3
State Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: In both cases, the confessor must hand over the item to the confessee and then sue. Their reasoning is qiyas: admitting possession is admitting ownership, so the confessor's later claim is contradictory.
They treat deposit and rental identically based on the general principle of iqrar.
4
Analyze the istihsan reasoning
The commentary explains that Abu Hanifa's view is based on istihsan. The hand in rental is established necessarily for the fulfillment of the contract (usufruct), not as an intentional admission of ownership. Therefore, it does not prejudice the confessor's claim.
Istihsan outweighs qiyas here because of the difference in the nature of possession.
5
Note the exception from Al-Kifayah
If the item is known to belong to the confessor (e.g., a garment with his name), then even Sahibayn agree that the confessor is believed initially. This exception shows that the dispute is limited to cases where ownership is unknown.
The exception preserves the general rule of ownership evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإقرار باب الاستثناء وإقرار المريض
وفي : هذا كان وديعة لي عندك، فأخذته ، فقال : هو لي أَخَذَه، وصُدِّقَ مَن قال : أجرت فرسي، أو ثوبي هذا ، فركبه ، أو لبسه ورده علي، أو خاط ثوبي هذا بكذا فقضته
[٢]
(وفي : هذا كان وديعة لي عندك ، فأخذته ، فقال : هو لي أَخَذَه) : أي الْمُقَرُّ له ؛ لأنه أقر بيده ، ثُمَّ ادَّعى أنَّه كان لي فأخذتُه ، فيسلمه إلى المقر له، ويقيم البينة. وصدق من قال : أجرتُ فرسي ، أو ثوبي هذا، فركبه ، أو لبسه ورده علي، أو خاط ثوبي هذا بكذا فقبضته ) ، هذا عند أبي ه، وعندهما:
[٤]
يجب أن يُسَلَّمَ إلى المُقَرِّ له ، ثُمَّ يدعيه كما في مسألة الوديعة [١] قوله : وفي هذا ...الخ ؛ يعني لو قال المقر للمقرّ له : إن هذا الشيء كان لي عندك وديعة، فأخذتُها منك ، وقال المقوله : هو لي، فهو يأخذه. [٢] قوله : لأنه أقر...الخ ؛ يعني لأنَّ المقر أقرَّ باليد له، ثم بالأخذ منه وهو سبب الضمان ، ثمَّ ادَّعى استحقاقه عليه فلا تقبل دعواه ، فوجب عليه رد عينه إن كان قائماً ، وقيمته إن كان هالكاً، ثمّ يقيم البينة على صدق دعواه إن قدر على ذلك). [۳] قوله : وصدق من قال....الخ ؛ يعني قال : أجرت فرسي هذا فلاناً فركبه ورده إلي أو قال : أجرتُ ثوبي هذا فلاناً فلبسه وردَّه إلي ، وقال فلان : كذبت بل الفرس والثوب لي ، فالقول قول المقر ويصدق. وكذا الإعارة والإسكان، فمن قال : أعرتُك داري هذه ثم رددت علي، أو أسلفتك داري هذه ثم رددت علي، وقال الآخر : كذبت، وهذه الدار اليا، فصدق المقر على الخلاف، ولو قال : خاط فلان ثوبي بدرهم مثلاً ، ثم قبضه، وقال فلان : كذبت والثوب ثوبي ، فصدق المقر، ويكون القول قوله.
[٤]قوله : وعندهما يجب أن يسلم ... الخ ؛ يعني عند أ . أبي يوسف ومحمد له يكون القولُ قولُ الذي أخذ منه الفرس أو الثوب، ويجب على المقر أن يسلّمه إلى المقر له، ثم يدعيه عند القاضي، كما في مسألة الوديعة. قال في «الكفاية": هذا إذا لم يكن الدابة والثوب معروفاً أنه للمقر، أما لو كان الثوب معروفاً أنه للمقرّ أو الدابةُ أو الدار ، فقال : قبضته فيه، فكان القولُ قولُ المقرّ في قولهم ؛ لأن الملك فيه معروف للمقر. انتهى.

Book of Confession, Chapter of Exception and Confession of a Sick Person. And regarding: 'This was a deposit with you, and I took it,' and he said 'It is mine, he took it.' [1] His saying: 'And regarding this...' means if the confessor said to the confessee: 'This thing was a deposit with you, and I took it from you,' and the confessee said 'It is mine,' he takes it. [2] His saying: 'Because he confessed...' means because the confessor confessed the hand (possession) to him, then [confessed] taking from him which is a cause of liability, then claimed entitlement against him, so his claim is not accepted, so he must return the substance if it exists, or its value if it perished, then he (the confessee) establishes proof of his claim if able. [3] His saying: 'And the one who said... is believed' means: He said 'I rented my horse to so-and-so, he rode it and returned it to me' or 'I rented my garment to so-and-so, he wore it and returned it to me,' and so-and-so said 'You lie, rather the horse and garment are mine,' then the saying is the saying of the confessor and he is believed. Likewise for lending and housing, so whoever said 'I lent you this house of mine then you returned it to me' or 'I housed you in this house of mine then you returned it to me,' and the other said 'You lie, this house is mine,' the confessor is believed according to the dispute. And if he said 'So-and-so sewed my garment for a dirham for example, then I took it,' and so-and-so said 'You lie, the garment is my garment,' then the confessor is believed, and his saying is the saying. [4] His saying: 'And according to them it must be handed over...' means according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the saying is the saying of the one who took the horse or garment, and the confessor must hand it over to the confessee, then he claims it before the judge, as in the deposit case. Al-Kifayah said: 'This applies when the animal or garment is not known to belong to the confessor. But if the garment is known to belong to the confessor, or the animal or the house, and he said 'I took it,' then the saying is the saying of the confessor according to both of them, because ownership of it is known for the confessor.' End quote. [1] His saying: 'It is the analogy' and its reasoning: The confessor confessed the hand (possession) to him, then claimed entitlement against him, so his confession for him is accepted over his claim against him, so he must return as in the deposit and loan case as mentioned. [2] His saying: 'And the basis of istihsan...' its explanation: The hand in rental is established necessarily to fulfill the contracted benefits, so it is not a confession of hand to them intentionally, thus it does not appear in the matter of entitlement against the confessor, unlike deposit and loan, because the hand in them is intentional, so confessing them is a confession of hand.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses two related scenarios in the chapter of Iqrar (confession). The first scenario: A person (the confessor) says to another 'This was a deposit (wadi'ah) with you, and I took it back.' The other person (the confessee) says 'It is mine.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the confessor must hand over the item to the confessee because the confessor has already admitted that the confessee had possession (yad) and then claimed ownership, which is contradictory; his claim is not accepted without proof. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples) differ: they say the confessor must hand over the item first, then he can file a claim of ownership before a judge, similar to the deposit case. Their view is based on qiyas (analogy) with the deposit case where the same procedure is followed. The commentary from Al-Kifayah adds an exception: if the item is known to belong to the confessor (e.g., a garment with his name), then even the two disciples agree that the confessor is believed initially. The second scenario: A person says 'I rented out my horse/garment, the other person used it and returned it to me.' The other person claims it is his. Here, Imam Abu Hanifa says the confessor is believed (his statement is accepted). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad again require handing over first. The commentary explains the reasoning: the qiyas (analogy) would suggest the confessor's claim is not accepted because he admitted the other's hand, but istihsan (juristic preference) prevails because in rental (ijarah) and loan (i'arah), the hand (possession) of the user is necessary for the purpose of benefit, not a true proprietary hand. Therefore, the confessor's admission of hand does not imply ownership. This is unlike deposit (wadi'ah) and loan (qard) where the hand is proprietary.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession or admission: a statement by which a person acknowledges a right of another against himself.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference: setting aside analogy for a stronger or more appropriate ruling due to necessity or custom.
اليد (al-yad): Hand or possession; a legal indication of ownership or control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the confessor believed in the rental case but not in the deposit case?
A) Because rental involves a known amount of compensation
B) Because the hand in rental is necessary for benefit, not an admission of ownership
C) Because the confessor is more trustworthy in rental matters
D) Because the rent has already been paid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the nature of possession (intentional vs. necessary) determines whether a confessor's claim of ownership is accepted without proof.

Priority of Debts in Marad al-Mawt: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Debate on priority of debts in marad al-mawt: Hanafi vs Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on priority of debts in marad al-mawt and explain the key difference with the Shafi'i school using the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
In terminal illness, debts from known causes take precedence over mere acknowledgments to prevent fraud against creditors.

This passage presents a classical Hanafi–Shafi'i disagreement about how to prioritize debts for a terminally ill debtor. The Hanafi school distinguishes between debts whose cause is known (e.g., witnessed loans) and debts that are merely acknowledged without a known cause. The Shafi'i school treats all acknowledgments equally based on the trustworthiness of the individual. Understanding this dispute reveals the Hanafi concern for protecting creditors' rights.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the three categories of debts mentioned: health debts, illness debts from known causes, and illness debts from mere acknowledgment. The Hanafi ruling is that debts from known causes (loans, purchases, mahr) are given precedence over mere acknowledgments.
The first two categories are treated equally, but both are superior to the third.
2
Identify the Shafi'i opposition
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all acknowledgments in marad al-mawt are equal in priority, because the cause of the debt is the same: the acknowledgment itself, which is a truthful report from a rational and religious person.
Shafi'i sees no reason to suspect fraud if the person is of sound mind and faith.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof (qiyas)
The Hanafi argument uses qiyas jaliy: just as a pawn or lease restricts disposition to protect the rights of others, so too does an acknowledgment in illness not override the prior rights of creditors, because the right of health creditors is attached to the wealth when the debtor is incapable of earning.
The analogy highlights that acknowledging a debt is a form of disposition that can nullify others' rights.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
The Hanafi response argues that in health, creditors' rights are not attached to specific wealth because the person can earn and pay; in illness, attachment is necessary because the debtor cannot earn and might waste assets. Therefore, mere acknowledgments are suspect.
The difference hinges on the debtor's capacity to earn.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that in a Hanafi court, if a terminally ill person acknowledges a debt without witnesses or known cause, that debt is paid after debts from known causes. In a Shafi'i court, all are paid proportionally.
This affects how the estate is distributed among creditors.

كبدل ما ملكه ، أو أتلفه، أو مهر عرسه سواء ، وقدما على ما أقر به في مرض موته، هذا عندنا، وعند الشّافِعِي هذا يساوي الأولين ؛ لاستواء السبب، وهو الإقرار

ولنا : أن إقرار المريض وَقَعَ بما تعلّق به من حق الغير.

وبالزيادة على الثلث إذا لم يكن عليه دين ، فحالـة الـصـحـة والمـرض لـو كـانـتا متساويين لما منع من التبرع إذ المحاباة في حال المرض، كما في حال الصحة بخلاف النكاح، فإنّه من الحوائج الأصلية حال كونه بمهر المثل ، أما الزيادة على ذلك فباطلة، والنكاح جائز، والمرء غير ممنوع من الحوائج الأصلية، وإن كان ثمة ديـن الصحة كالصرف إلى ثمن الأدوية والأغذية.
فإن قلت : لو تزوج شيخ فان رابعة جاز، وليس بمحتاج إليها.
قلنا : النكاح في أصل الوضع من مصالح المعيشة، والعبرة لأصل الوضع، لا للحال، فإنَّ الحال مما لا يتوقف عليها، وبخلاف البيع بمثل القيمة فإنّه لا يبطل حقهم أيضاً، فإنّ حقهم في المالية وهي باقية.
فإن قيل : لو تعلق حق الغرماء بمال المديون بطل إقراره بالدين حالة الصحة، فإنَّ الإقرار المتضمن لإبطال حق الغيرِ غير معتبر كما تقدَّم مع أنّ ذلك ليس بباطل إجماعاً. أجيب : بأن حال الصحة لم يتعلّق فيها حق الغرماء بالمال ؛ لقدرته علي الاكتساب، فلم يحتج إلى تعليق حق الغرماء بماله، فيتحقق التثمير وحالة المرض حالة العجز ، فإنَّ الإنسانَ لَمَّا مَرِضَ مرض الموت وعجز عن الاكتساب فلو لم يتعلق حق الغرماء بالمال ولم ينتقل من الذمة إليه ينوي دينه ؛ لأنَّ المريض يتلف المال سريعاً فيؤدي إلى إبطال حقه فيهم
[٢]قوله : مقدم على الإرث ؛ وإن شمل جميع المال فإنّ الوارث لا يستحق شيئاً (1) ينظر: «العناية» (۸: ۳۸۱ - ۳۸۳) .
Like the substitute for what he owned, or what he destroyed, or the mahr of his marriage, all equal, and they are given precedence over what he acknowledged during his terminal illness. This is our (Hanafi) view. According to al-Shafi'i, this is equal to the first two due to the equality of the cause, which is acknowledgment. [1] His statement: 'like the substitute for what he owned... etc.' is an example of a debt due to a known cause that occurred during that illness and was known without acknowledgment. So 'substitute for what he owned' means by borrowing or by purchase, as when he borrows money during his illness with witnesses present, the lender hands over the money, or he buys something with witnesses present and takes delivery, or he rents something with witnesses, or he becomes the owner of property. [2] His statement: 'or the mahr of his marriage' is as when he marries a woman with her proper mahr and witnesses witness the marriage. [3] His statement: 'equal' means they are equal in degree, so neither is given precedence over the other in adjudication; because when the cause is known, suspicion in the acknowledgment is removed, so it becomes like a debt established by evidence during his illness. [4] His statement: 'and they are given precedence over what he acknowledged during his illness' means that the debt of health and the debt of illness due to a known cause that occurred during that illness and was known without acknowledgment are given precedence over a debt acknowledged during his illness, even if what is acknowledged is a deposit, as stated in 'al-Bahr' and others. [0] His statement: 'this is equal... etc.' means that what he acknowledged during his illness is equal in degree to the first two due to the equality in the cause of obligation, which is acknowledgment emanating from reason and religion; for reason and religion prevent a person from lying in his report, and acknowledgment is a report about an obligation in his liability, so he does not lie in his report due to the existence of these two characteristics. In this there is agreement between whether the acknowledging person is healthy or ill; rather, illness increases the likelihood of truthfulness because illness is a state of repentance and turning back. And its locus is the liability (dhimma), for it is the place of obligation in states of health and illness; hence the debt of his illness equals the debt of his health if its obligation is due to a known cause, like a purchase at fair value and marriage with a proper mahr. (1) See: al-Mahalli (3:54), Nihayat al-Muhtaj (5:70), Fatuhat al-Wahhab (3:432). (2) al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (7:254). Book of Acknowledgment, Chapter of Exception and Acknowledgment of the Sick. And all are given precedence over inheritance, even if it encompasses his wealth. Our proof: The acknowledgment of the sick person occurred regarding something to which the right of others is attached. (And all are given precedence over inheritance, even if it encompasses his wealth: meaning the three debts—the debt of health, the debt of illness due to a known cause, and the debt of illness known merely by acknowledgment—are given precedence over inheritance, even if they encompass all the wealth. [1] His statement: 'Our proof... etc.': its explanation: Indeed, acknowledgment is not considered a proof if it involves nullifying the right of another. Do you not see that if he pawns an item or leases it, he is not allowed to dispose of it because it involves nullifying the right of another? And here too in his acknowledgment it is so, because the right of the creditors of health is attached to this wealth for collection; hence the sick person is prevented from donating and showing favoritism at all if debts surround his wealth. And regarding the excess over one-third if there is no debt on him—if the states of health and illness were equal, he would not be prevented from donation, since favoritism in illness is like in health, unlike marriage, for it is one of the essential needs when conducted with a proper mahr; as for excess beyond that, it is void, while marriage is permissible. A person is not prevented from essential needs even if there is a debt of health, like spending on the price of medicines and food. If you say: If an old man marries a fourth wife, it is permissible, and he is not in need of her. We reply: Marriage in its original nature is among the benefits of livelihood, and consideration is given to the original nature, not to the specific situation, for the situation is not depended upon. And unlike a sale at fair value, for it does not nullify their right either, because their right lies in the monetary value, which remains. If it is said: If the right of creditors is attached to the debtor's wealth, his acknowledgment of debt in a state of health would be void, for an acknowledgment that entails nullifying the right of another is not considered, as previously stated, yet that is not void by consensus. The answer: In the state of health, the right of creditors is not attached to his wealth because he is capable of earning, so there is no need to attach the creditors' right to his wealth, thereby enabling profit-making; whereas the state of illness is a state of incapacity, for when a person falls terminally ill and becomes unable to earn, if the creditors' right were not attached to his wealth and did not transfer from the liability to his wealth, his debt would not be fulfilled because the sick person quickly wastes wealth, leading to the nullification of their right. [2] His statement: 'given precedence over inheritance' even if it encompasses all the wealth, for the heir does not deserve anything. (1) See: al-Inayah (8:381-383).
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the priority of debts in the estate of a person who died terminally ill (marad al-mawt). The text begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: debts that arise from known causes during the illness—such as loans, purchases, or mahr for marriage—are treated equally and are given precedence over mere acknowledgments of debt made during the illness. The Shafi'i position is that all acknowledgments are equal because the cause is the same: the act of acknowledgment itself, which is a truthful report from a rational and religious person. The Hanafi response argues that an acknowledgment is not a valid proof if it nullifies the rights of others, similar to the case of a pawn or lease where the owner cannot dispose of the property. Since the right of health creditors is attached to the sick person's wealth, mere acknowledgments could be a means to defraud them. The text further clarifies that all three types of debts (health debts, illness debts from known causes, and illness debts from mere acknowledgment) are preferred over inheritance, even if they consume the entire estate. The Hanafi proof uses qiyas: just as a pawn or lease restricts disposition to protect the rights of others, so too does acknowledgment in illness not override the prior rights of creditors. The discussion also addresses objections about marriage and sales, distinguishing between essential needs and transactions that preserve monetary value. Finally, the superiority of debts from known causes over mere acknowledgments is based on the principle that the latter can easily be used to deprive creditors.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): An acknowledgment or admission of a debt or obligation. In this text, it refers to the sick person's declaration of a debt.
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Terminal illness during which a person is expected to die. It triggers special rules to protect the rights of heirs and creditors.
بدل ما ملكه (badal ma malakahu): Substitute for what he owned, i.e., a debt incurred through borrowing or purchase in exchange for something owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which type of debt is given priority in the distribution of the estate of a terminally ill person?
A) A debt acknowledged in the illness, even without witnesses.
B) A debt arising from a loan or purchase during the illness with witnesses.
C) A debt acknowledged in health, regardless of cause.
D) All debts are treated equally regardless of how they arose.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives priority to debts with known causes in marad al-mawt to protect creditors' rights, while the Shafi'i school treats all acknowledgments equally.

Acknowledging Paternity of an Unknown Child in Death Illness
Ruling with proof on paternity acknowledgment in death illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on establishing paternity through acknowledgment in death illness, identify the conditions required, and explain why lineage is considered a fundamental need that overrides the usual restrictions on death-illness declarations.

Core Idea
Lineage is a fundamental need; paternity acknowledgment in illness is valid even if it harms heirs.

In death illness (marad al-mawt), financial bequests are restricted to one-third of the estate to protect heirs' rights. However, acknowledging paternity is not a financial transaction—it establishes lineage, a fundamental human need. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a sick person acknowledging an unknown boy as his son, the conditions for validity, and the rationale that distinguishes it from ordinary bequests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its conditions
State the two conditions for the acknowledgment to establish lineage: (1) the boy must be of an age that could be his son (minimum 12 years younger), and (2) the boy must confirm the acknowledgment if he is discerning.
The age condition ensures possibility; confirmation is required because the right to lineage belongs to the boy.
2
Analyze the rationale for validity in death illness
Explain why the Hanafi school allows this acknowledgment despite the general restriction on death-illness declarations: lineage is a fundamental need (haaja asliyya) and there is no suspicion (tuhma) of defrauding heirs, unlike financial bequests.
This is based on the principle that necessity overrides restrictions.
3
Examine the Maliki objection and Hanafi response
Understand Imam Malik's view that if people are certain the boy is not the son, lineage is not established. Then see the Hanafi response using legal stratagem (ihtiyal): lineage can be established if possible; certainty is not required.
The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa due to ease and the fundamental nature of lineage.
4
Define 'unknown lineage' in this context
Clarify that a boy of unknown lineage means his father is unknown in the town where he currently resides, not his birthplace, to avoid hardship in verifying lineage across distant locations.
This definition is from al-Qunya and Majma' al-Anhur.
5
Determine the impact on inheritance
Once lineage is established, the boy shares in the inheritance as a necessary consequence. The acknowledgment is binding and the boy becomes a legal heir.
This distinguishes paternity acknowledgment from a bequest, which is limited to one-third.

Source Text

ولو أقر ببنوة غلام جهل نسبه ويولد مثله لمثله ويصدقه الغلام ثبت نسبه ولو في مرض وشارك الورثة. قوله: ويولد مثله لمثله أي يكون سنه أقل من سن المقر باثني عشر سنة، وهي أدنى مدة يحتمل فيها الغلام. قوله: وصدقه الغلام قيد بتصديق الغلام لأن الحق له فلا يثبت بدون تصديقه إذا كان مميزاً، والكلام فيه بخلاف ما إذا كان لا يعبر عن نفسه حيث لا يعتبر تصديقه لأنه في يد غيره. قوله: ثبت نسبه وقال مالك إن استيقن الناس أنه ليس ولده لا يثبت نسبه منه، كما إذا كان الغلام سندياً وذلك الرجل المقر فارسياً، ولنا إن النسب مما يحتال لإثباته إذا أمكن، وفي هذه الصورة يمكن. قوله: ولو في مرض إنما لم يمتنع ثبوت النسب بمرض المقر لأن النسب من الحوائج الأصلية ولا تهمة فيه. قوله: وشارك الورثة في الميراث لأنه من ضرورات النسب. قوله: تصديق الغلام.... الخ؛ حاصله إن تصديق الغلام إنما شرط في هذه الصورة إذا كان من يعبر، وإن لم يعبر ومات المقر ثبت نسبه وشارك الورثة بلا تصديق. النسب من لا يعلم له أب في بلده على ما ذكر في «شرح تلخيص الجامع» لأكمل الدين، والظاهر أن المراد به بلد هو فيه، كما ذكر في «القنية» لا مسقط رأسه كما ذكر البعض؛ لأن المغربي إذا انتقل إلى المشرق فوقع عليه حادثة يلزم أن نفتش عن نسبه في المغرب، وفيه من الحرج ما لا يخفى. انتهى. وقال في «مجمع الأنهر»: مجهول النسب في بلد هو فيها، وهو المراد من مجهول النسب في كل موضع على ما في «القنية»، لكن في أكثر الكتب أن يجهل نسبه في مولده، فإن عرف نسبه فيه فهو معروف النسب. انتهى. وقيد بقوله: جهل نسبه؛ لأنه لو كان معروف النسب امتنع ثبوت نسبه من غيره، ولا حاجة إلى إثباته لاستغنائه به عنه.
If someone acknowledges the sonship of a boy whose lineage is unknown, and a boy of his type can be born to a man of his type, and the boy confirms it, his lineage is established even if in illness, and he shares in the inheritance. [Commentary:] His statement: 'and a boy of his type can be born to a man of his type' means his age is less than the age of the acknowledger by twelve years, which is the minimum period in which the boy is possible. His statement: 'and the boy confirms him' is conditioned on the boy's confirmation because the right belongs to him, so it is not established without his confirmation if he is discerning. His statement: 'his lineage is established' – Malik said: If people are certain that he is not his son, his lineage is not established from him, as if the boy is from Sind and the acknowledging man is Persian. Our proof: Lineage is something that can be established by stratagem when possible, and in this case it is possible. His statement: 'even if in illness' – the establishment of lineage is not prevented by the illness of the acknowledger because lineage is one of the fundamental needs and there is no suspicion in it. His statement: 'and he shares in the inheritance' because it is a necessary consequence of lineage. His statement: 'the boy's confirmation' – the summary is that the boy's confirmation is only required in this case if he is articulate; if he is not articulate and the acknowledger dies, his lineage is established and he shares in the inheritance without confirmation. Lineage applies to one who has no known father in his town, as mentioned in Sharh Talkhis al-Jami' by Akmal al-Din, and the apparent meaning is the town where he is present, as mentioned in al-Qunya, not his birthplace as some say, because if a Maghribi moves to the Mashriq and an incident occurs, we would have to investigate his lineage in the Maghrib, which is clearly burdensome. End quote. It is said in Majma' al-Anhur: 'Unknown lineage' in the town where he is, and that is what is meant by 'unknown lineage' in every place according to al-Qunya, but in most books it is said that one's lineage is unknown in his birthplace; if his lineage is known there, he is of known lineage. End quote. He restricted it by saying 'whose lineage is unknown' because if he were of known lineage, establishing his lineage from someone else would be prevented, and there is no need to establish it due to sufficiency.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling from the chapter on acknowledgment, specifically acknowledging paternity of a boy of unknown lineage during the acknowledger's death illness. The ruling: If a sick person acknowledges that a boy (whose lineage is unknown, i.e., no known father in his town) is his son, the boy's lineage is established and he inherits, provided two conditions are met: (1) the boy is of such age that he could be the son of the acknowledger (minimum 12 years younger), and (2) the boy confirms the acknowledgment if he is discerning. The commentary explains that the age condition ensures the claim is not contradicted by external reality. The confirmation condition is required because the boy has a right to his lineage; if he is non-discerning (e.g., an infant), confirmation is not needed. The Hanafi school also responds to Imam Malik, who held that if people are certain the boy is not the son (e.g., different ethnicities), lineage is not established. The Hanafi response is that lineage can be established by legal stratagem if possible, and in this case it is possible (the boy could be a foundling). The reason acknowledgment in illness is valid is that lineage is a fundamental human need and there is no suspicion (tuhma) of defrauding heirs, unlike bequests which are limited to one-third. The boy's confirmation is only required if he can express himself; if he cannot, his silence suffices. The passage also defines 'majhul al-nasab' (unknown lineage) as one whose father is unknown in the town where he currently resides, not his birthplace, to avoid hardship.

Key Terms
جهل نسبه (jahila nasabuhu): A person whose father is unknown in the town where he currently resides (not his birthplace), so he is considered of unknown lineage.
إقرار (iqrar): A legal acknowledgment or admission of a right or liability, here specifically acknowledgment of paternity.
حاجة أصلية (hajah asliyyah): A fundamental need essential for human welfare, such as lineage, which warrants leniency in rules of suspicion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must be met regarding the age of the boy in the acknowledgment of paternity?
A) The boy must be older than the acknowledger.
B) The boy must be at least twelve years younger than the acknowledger.
C) The boy must be the same age as the acknowledger.
D) There is no age condition.

Key Takeaway
Paternity acknowledgment in death illness is valid and establishes lineage and inheritance, because lineage is a fundamental need that overrides suspicion, provided the boy is of possible age and confirms if discerning.

Acknowledgment to a Non-Heir Who Later Becomes an Heir
Ruling on acknowledgment validity when person later becomes heir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid acknowledgments to a non-heir who later becomes an heir, state the Hanafi position, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute with Zufar.

Core Idea
An acknowledgment to a non-heir is binding; later becoming an heir by a new cause does not invalidate it, but by an existing cause does.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi inheritance law: what if a person acknowledges a debt or property to someone who is not an heir at that time, but later becomes an heir? The ruling hinges on whether the cause of inheritance existed at the time of acknowledgment or arose afterward. We will explore the Hanafi position, Zufar's objection, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core distinction
Note that the passage asks us to look at whether the person became an heir due to a cause existing at the time of acknowledgment (e.g., having a son who later dies) or a new cause (e.g., marriage, clientage).
This distinction is the key to the ruling.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For an existing cause, the acknowledgment is invalid. For a new cause, it is valid.
The Hanafi proof: the acknowledgment was issued for a non-heir, so it is binding.
3
Follow Zufar's objection and the Hanafi response
Zufar argues that the acknowledgment is for an heir at death, analogizing to lineage. The Hanafi response distinguishes because in lineage the cause existed at the time of acknowledgment, whereas here it is new.
The analogy fails due to the different timing of the cause.
4
Examine the sub-case of the acknowledged dying before the sick person
If the acknowledged heir dies first, he ceases to be an heir; Abu Yusuf initially disagreed but later agreed with Muhammad that the acknowledgment is valid.
This settles the intra-Hanafi dispute in favor of validity.
5
Understand the underlying principle
An acknowledgment is binding when issued and not revoked by subsequent changes, unlike a bequest which is treated as a will and subject to the one-third rule at death.
The principle: 'al-iqrar yanfudh wa yalzam' (acknowledgment takes effect and becomes binding).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم يكن وارثاً وقت الإقرار، ثم صار وارثاً وقت الموت ينظر : فإن صار وارثاً بسبب كان قائماً وقت الإقرار بأن أقر لأخيه وله ابن ثم مات الابن قبل الأب لا يصح، وإن صار وارثاً بسبب جديد كالتزويج ، وعقد الموالاة، جاز. وقال زفر : لا يجوز؛ لأنَّ الإقرار حصل للوارث وقت الموت، فصار كما إذا صار وارثاً بالنسب. ولنا : إنَّ الإقرار حين صدر حصل للأجنبي لا للوارث، فنفذ ولزم فلا يبطل بخلاف الهبة ؛ لأنها وصيّة ؛ ولهذا يعتبر من الثلث، فيعتبرُ وقتُ الموتِ بخلاف ما إذا صار وارثاً بالنسب، بأن أقرّ مسلم مريض لأخيه الكافر ثم أسلم قبل موته أو كان محجوباً بالابن ثم مات الابن، حيث لا يجوز الإقرار له ; لأن سبب الإرث كان قائماً وقت الاقرار. ولو أقر لوارثه ثم مات المقر له ثم المريض، ووارث المقر له من ورثة المريض لم يجز إقراره عند أبي يوسف له أولاً ; لأن إقراره حصل ابتداء وانتهاء، وقال آخراً : يجوز، وهو قول محمد ; لأنه بالموت قبل موت المريض خرج من أن يكون وارثا ، وكذلك لو أقر لأجنبي ثم مات المقر ثم المريض ووارثه من ورثة المقر; لأن إقراره كان للأجنبي، فيتم به، ثم لا يبطل بموته. صرح به في «التبيين»، وغيره، فاحفظه فإنه ينفعك إن شاء الله تعالى.
If [the person] was not an heir at the time of acknowledgment, then becomes an heir at the time of death, we look: if he became an heir due to a cause that existed at the time of acknowledgment, such as acknowledging his brother while having a son, then the son dies before the father, it is not valid. If he became an heir due to a new cause, like marriage or a contract of clientage, it is valid. Zufar said: It is not valid because the acknowledgment was for an heir at the time of death, so it becomes like the case where he becomes an heir by lineage. Our proof: The acknowledgment when issued was for a non-heir, not for an heir, so it takes effect and becomes binding and is not invalidated, unlike a gift because it is a bequest; for this reason it is considered from one-third, so the time of death is considered, unlike the case where he becomes an heir by lineage, such as a Muslim sick person acknowledging his disbelieving brother, then he converts before death, or [the brother] was excluded by a son then the son dies, where the acknowledgment is not valid because the cause of inheritance existed at the time of acknowledgment. If he acknowledged an heir, then the acknowledged one died, then the sick person died, and the heir of the acknowledged one is from among the heirs of the sick person, his acknowledgment is not valid according to Abu Yusuf initially because his acknowledgment began and ended [with an heir], and later he said it is valid, and that is the statement of Muhammad because by dying before the sick person he ceased to be an heir. Similarly, if he acknowledged a non-heir, then the acknowledger died, then the sick person [the original acknowledger?] died, and his heir is from among the heirs of the acknowledger, because his acknowledgment was for a non-heir, so it is completed by it, then it is not invalidated by his death. This is stated in al-Tabyin and other works, so memorize it; it will benefit you, God willing.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on acknowledgment discusses the validity of an acknowledgment made during death illness when the person acknowledged later becomes an heir at the time of the acknowledger's death. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) If the person becomes an heir due to a cause that already existed at the time of acknowledgment (e.g., the acknowledger had a son who dies after the acknowledgment, making his brother an heir), the acknowledgment is invalid because the cause of inheritance was already present, and the acknowledgment is treated as a bequest to an heir, which is not valid. (2) If the person becomes an heir due to a new cause (e.g., marrying the acknowledger after the acknowledgment, or a contract of clientage), the acknowledgment is valid because at the time of acknowledgment he was a non-heir, and the new cause does not retrospectively invalidate the binding acknowledgment. The Hanafis rebut Zufar, who analogized to inheritance by lineage, by arguing that the acknowledgment was executed when the person was a non-heir, so it is binding and not vitiated by subsequent change, unlike a gift which is considered a bequest and subject to the one-third rule at death. The passage also covers the case where the acknowledged dies before the sick person: if the acknowledged was an heir initially, Abu Yusuf initially held it invalid (since it was for an heir from start to finish), but later held it valid, agreeing with Muhammad, because the acknowledged ceased to be an heir by dying first. For a non-heir who later dies, his heirs inherit from the acknowledger, and the acknowledgment remains valid.

Key Terms
وارث (warith): An heir entitled to inherit from the deceased at the time of death, whether by lineage or other cause.
سبب جديد (sabab jadid): A new cause of inheritance arising after the acknowledgment, such as marriage or clientage contract, not present at the time of acknowledgment.
إقرار لوارث (iqrar li-warith): An acknowledgment made to a person who is already an heir at the time of acknowledgment; such acknowledgment is generally invalid in death illness because it is treated as a bequest to an heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim in death illness acknowledges his Christian brother (non-heir). The brother converts to Islam before the sick person dies. What is the ruling?
A) Invalid because he becomes an heir.
B) Valid because conversion is a new cause.
C) Invalid because he was a non-heir at acknowledgment.
D) Valid only if conversion was before acknowledgment.

Key Takeaway
An acknowledgment to a non-heir remains valid even if he later becomes an heir, provided the cause of inheritance is new and not existing at the time of acknowledgment.

Conditions for Acknowledgment of Lineage in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary on matn explaining lineage acknowledgment conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid acknowledgment of lineage in Hanafi fiqh and apply them to distinguish cases where confirmation is or is not required.

Core Idea
Lineage is established by acknowledgment if biologically plausible and the child confirms, unless incapable.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya explains the precise mechanism by which a child's lineage (nasab) is established through the father's acknowledgment (iqrar). The Hanafi school sets two conditions: the age difference must allow biological possibility, and the child, if capable, must confirm the acknowledgment. The text also addresses a dispute with Imam Malik and clarifies scenarios where confirmation is unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
From the matn: 'one like him is born to one like him' (age plausibility) and 'the boy confirms him' (child's confirmation if capable).
These are the core prerequisites for iqrar al-nasab.
2
Understand the age condition
The commentary specifies the age difference must be at least 12 years to avoid biological impossibility.
This is a minimal threshold; greater difference is unproblematic.
3
Analyze the confirmation condition and its exception
Confirmation is required only if the child can express himself (mu'abbir). If not (e.g., infant, insane) and the acknowledger dies, lineage is established without confirmation.
The child's incapacity places him in another's hand, so his confirmation is irrelevant.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the Maliki dispute
Imam Malik holds that if people are certain the child is not his (e.g., different ethnicity), lineage is not established. The Hanafi response: lineage can be established by legal means if possible.
The Hanafi reliance on 'ihtiyal' (legal contrivance) allows flexibility.
5
Note additional rulings
Acknowledgment is valid even in the acknowledger's final illness (marad al-mawt) and leads to the child inheriting as a necessary consequence.
Lineage is a primary need, so no suspicion of fraud applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُولَدُ مثلُهُ لمثله وصَدِّقَهُ الغلامُ ثبت نسبه ولو في مرض، وشارك الورثة . ويُولَدُ مثلُهُ لمثله : أي هما في السِّنِّ بحيث يولَدُ مثلُهُ لمثله . وصَدَّقَهُ الغلام . ثبت نسبه ولو في مرض وشارك الورثة . تصديق الغلام إنما يشترط إذا كان من يُعبر، وإن لم يُعبّر ومات المُقِرُّ ثبت نسبه وشارك الورثة بلا تصديق . النسب من لا يعلم له أب في بلده على ما ذكر في شرح تلخيص الجامع لأكمل الدين، والظاهر أن المراد به بلد هو فيه، كما ذكر في القنية لا مسقط رأسه كما ذكر البعض؛ لأن المغربي إذا انتقل إلى المشرق فوقع عليه حادثة يلزم أن نفتش عن نسبه في المغرب، وفيه من الحرج ما لا يخفى. انتهى. وقال في مجمع الأنهر : مجهول النسب في بلد هو فيها، وهو المراد من مجهول النسب في كل موضع على ما في القنية، لكن في أكثر الكتب أن يجهل نسبه في مولده، فإن عرف نسبه فيه فهو معروف النسب. انتهى. وقيد بقوله: جهل نسبه؛ لأنه لو كان معروف النسب امتنع ثبوت نسبه من غيره، ولا حاجة إلى إثباته لاستغنائه به عنه. [١] قوله: ويولد مثله لمثله؛ أي يكون سنه أقل من سن المقر باثني عشر سنة، وهي أدنى مدة يحتمل فيها الغلام، ذكره البرجندي في شرح النقاية، وقيد به لئلا يكذبه الظاهر. [٢] قوله: وصدقه الغلام؛ قيّد بتصديق الغلام؛ لأن الحق له فلا يثبت بدون تصديقه إذا كان مميزاً، والكلام فيه بخلاف ما إذا كان لا يعبر عن نفسه حيث لا يعتبر تصديقه؛ لأنه في يد غيره. [٣] قوله: ثبت نسبه؛ وقال مالك : إن استيقن الناس أنه ليس ولده لا يثبت نسبه منه، كما إذا كان الغلام سنديّاً وذلك الرجل المقر فارسيّاً، ولنا: إن النسب مما يحتال لإثباته إذا أمكن، وفي هذه الصورة يمكن. [٤] قوله: ولو في مرض؛ إنما لم يمتنع ثبوت النسب بمرض المقر؛ لأن النسب من الحوائج الأصلية ولا تهمة فيه. [٥] قوله: وشارك الورثة في الميراث؛ لأنه من ضرورات النسب. [٦] قوله: تصديق الغلام.... الخ؛ حاصله: إن تصديق الغلام إنما شرط في هذه المسألة إذا كان الغلام معبراً عن نفسه؛ لأنه حينئذ يكون في يد نفسه. وأما إذا لم يكن معبراً ومات المقر فنسبه يثبت من المقر ويشارك الورثة في الميراث ولا يعتبر تصديقه؛ لأنه في يد غيره فينزل منزلة التهمة. وهذا عندنا، وأما عند الأئمة الثلاثة يثبت نسب المعبر فلا يعتبر تصديقه أيضاً لو غير مكلف. صرح به في المنح وغيره.
And one like him is born to one like him, and the boy confirms him, his lineage is established even if in illness, and he shares with the heirs. 'One like him is born to one like him' means: they are close in age such that one like him could be born to one like him. 'And the boy confirms him.' 'His lineage is established even if in illness and he shares with the heirs.' The confirmation of the boy is only required if he can express himself; if he cannot express himself and the acknowledger dies, his lineage is established and he shares with the heirs without confirmation. The lineage of one whose father is unknown in his town according to what was mentioned in Sharh Talkhis al-Jami' by Akmal al-Din, and the apparent meaning is the town he is in, as mentioned in al-Qunyah, not his birthplace as some mentioned; because if a Moroccan moves to the East and an incident occurs to him, we would have to investigate his lineage in Morocco, and therein is hardship not hidden.
