Istihqaq in Sulh with Silence or Denial: Reversion to Original Positions
Rules of recovery when property is reclaimed from either party after settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when property or compensation is reclaimed after a settlement with silence or denial, and apply it to both scenarios: recovery from the defendant and recovery from the plaintiff.

Core Idea
A settlement with silence or denial is conditional; if the property or compensation is reclaimed, the parties revert to their original positions.

In Hanafi law, a settlement (ṣulḥ) is meant to end a dispute, but what happens if a third party proves ownership of the disputed property or the compensation paid? This passage reveals that the settlement is not final in such cases—it is conditional on the property remaining as intended. We will explore the two scenarios and the rationale behind the reversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage addresses two distinct cases: (1) the claimed property (mudda‘ā) is reclaimed from the defendant, and (2) the substitute (badal) paid by the defendant is reclaimed from the plaintiff. Both occur after a settlement with silence or denial.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
If the claimed property is reclaimed from the defendant, the plaintiff must return the compensation (or a proportional share) to the defendant and then litigate with the third-party reclaimer. If the compensation is reclaimed from the plaintiff, the plaintiff may reinstate his original claim against the defendant (in full or proportionally).
3
Understand the rationale (ta‘līl)
The defendant paid compensation only to avoid litigation and keep the property. If the property is lost, his purpose is defeated, so the compensation must be returned. The plaintiff accepted the substitute in lieu of his claim; if the substitute is lost, he returns to the claim. This is based on the principle that the settlement is conditional on the property remaining as intended.
Dalil type: talīl (rationale)
4
Distinguish from settlement with acknowledgment (iqrār)
If the settlement was with acknowledgment by the defendant, the rule differs: upon recovery of the substitute, the plaintiff returns to the original property (mubdal) because the acknowledgment is binding. In silence or denial, he returns to the claim itself.
5
Note the exception for sale wording
The passage ends by noting that if the settlement was phrased as a sale (e.g., 'I sold you this for that'), the ruling may differ. This exception is not elaborated here but shows that the legal form matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما استحق من المدعى رد المدعي حصته من العوض، ورجع بالخصومة فيه، وما استحق من البدل من يد المدعي رجع إلى الدعوى في كله، أو بعضه (وما اسْتُحِق من المدعى ردّ المدعي حصته من العوض، ورجع بالخصومة فيه) : أي يخاصمُ المُسْتَحِقُّ فيما استحقه، (وما استحق من البدل من يد المدعي رجع إلى الدعوى في كله أو بعضه) : أي إن استحق بعض البدل من يد المدعي رَجَعَ إلى دعوى حصة ما استحق من المصالح . عنه ، وإن استحق كلَّه رَجَعَ إلى دعوى الكل، وفي الصلح مع الإقرار إذا استحق البدل رَجَعَ إلى المُبْدَل ؛ لوجود إقرار المدعى عليه ، وفي السكوتِ والإنكار رَجَعَ إلى دعوى المبدل. [1]قوله : وما استحق...الخ ؛ يعني ما استحق من المدعى كلا أو بعضاً في صورة الصلح مع سكوت أو إنكار، يردُّ المدعي على المدعى عليه حصته من البدل، ويرجعُ المدعي بالخصومة مع المستحق فيما استحقه كلاً كان أو بعضاً. وصورته : إنَّ رجلاً ادّعى داراً في يد رجل فأنكر أو سكت، ثمَّ صالح عنها على مئة درهم، فصارت المئة في يد المدعي، والدار في يد المدعى عليه، ثم استحق كل الدار أو بعضه، فالمدعي يرد المئة إذا استحق كل الدار، أو الخمسين إن استحق نصفها للمدعى عليه، ويرجع بالخصومة في الدار إلى المستحق؛ لأنه أخذه على زعمِهِ عوضاً عما ادعى. فإذا استحق ذلك يرجع عليه المدعى عليه بناءً على زعمه، كأنه اشتراه منه ; ولأنَّ المدعى عليه لم يدفع العوض إلا ليدفع خصومةً عن نفسه، وليبق المدعى في يده من غير خصومة أحد، فإذا استحق لم يحصل له المقصود). (٢) قوله : وما استحق من البدل...الخ ; أي وما استحق من البدل كلاً أو بعضاً يرجع المدعي إلى دعواه في قدره، فإن استحق الكل يرجع إلى الكل، وإن استحق البعض يرجع إلى البعض ; لأن المدعي لم يترك الدعوى إلا ليسلم له البدل، فإذا لم يسلم له رجع بالمبدل وهو الدعوى. بخلاف ما إذا وقع الصلح بلفظ البيع، بأن قال أحدهما : بعتك هذا الشيء بهذا .
And whatever is reclaimed from the defendant: the plaintiff returns his share of the compensation, and returns to litigation over it. And whatever is reclaimed of the substitute from the hand of the plaintiff, he returns to the claim in its entirety or partially. (And whatever is reclaimed from the defendant: the plaintiff returns his share of the compensation, and returns to litigation over it) meaning: he litigates with the reclaimer over what was reclaimed. (And whatever is reclaimed of the substitute from the hand of the plaintiff, he returns to the claim in its entirety or partially) meaning: if some of the substitute is reclaimed from the hand of the plaintiff, he returns to the claim for the share of what was reclaimed from the settled property; and if all of it is reclaimed, he returns to the claim for the whole. In settlement with acknowledgment, if the substitute is reclaimed, he returns to the substituted property due to the existence of the defendant's acknowledgment; in silence and denial, he returns to the claim for the substituted property. [1] His statement: 'And whatever is reclaimed... etc.': means whatever is reclaimed from the defendant, wholly or partially, in the case of settlement with silence or denial: the plaintiff returns to the defendant his share of the substitute, and the plaintiff returns to litigation with the reclaimer over what was reclaimed, wholly or partially. Its form: a man claimed a house in the hand of a man who denied or was silent, then he settled with him for one hundred dirhams, so the hundred came into the hand of the plaintiff, and the house into the hand of the defendant. Then the entire house or part of it was reclaimed. So the plaintiff returns the hundred if the entire house is reclaimed, or fifty if half of it is reclaimed, to the defendant, and returns to litigation over the house with the reclaimer, because he took it on his claim as compensation for what he claimed. When that is reclaimed, the defendant returns to him based on his claim, as if he bought it from him; and because the defendant only paid the compensation to deflect litigation from himself and to keep the claimed property in his hand without anyone's litigation, so if it is reclaimed, his purpose is not achieved. (2) His statement: 'And whatever is reclaimed of the substitute... etc.': meaning whatever is reclaimed of the substitute, wholly or partially, the plaintiff returns to his claim in its extent; if the whole is reclaimed, he returns to the whole; if part is reclaimed, he returns to the part, because the plaintiff only abandoned the claim in order that the substitute be secured for him, so if it is not secured for him, he returns to the substituted property, which is the claim. Unlike if the settlement occurs with the wording of sale, e.g., one of them says: 'I sold you this thing for this.'
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof regarding the effects of istihqāq (recovery of property by a third party) on a settlement (ṣulḥ) that was concluded with silence or denial by the defendant. The text distinguishes two scenarios: (1) If the claimed property (the original disputed house) is recovered from the defendant by a third party, then the plaintiff must return to the defendant the compensation he received (or a proportional share if only part is recovered). The plaintiff then enters litigation with the third party over the recovered property. (2) If the substitute (the compensation paid by the defendant, e.g., 100 dirhams) is recovered from the plaintiff by a third party, then the plaintiff may reinstate his original claim against the defendant (for the whole or part, depending on how much was recovered). The commentary explains the rationale: the plaintiff only accepted the substitute in lieu of his claim; if it is lost, he reverts. For the first scenario, the defendant only paid to avoid litigation; since the property is reclaimed, his aim fails, so compensation must be returned. The text also notes that if the settlement was with acknowledgment (iqrār) by the defendant, the rule differs: upon recovery of the substitute, the plaintiff returns to the original property (mubdal), not the claim, because the acknowledgment is binding. The passage ends with a brief mention that if the settlement was phrased as a sale, the ruling may differ.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Recovery of property by a third party who proves rightful ownership, invalidating the transfer made via settlement.
بدل (badal): The substitute or compensation given in a settlement, e.g., money or goods.
مبدل (mubdal): The original property that was the subject of the claim or dispute, as opposed to the compensation (badal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a settlement with silence or denial, if the defendant's compensation (badal) is wholly reclaimed by a third party, what is the plaintiff's recourse?
A) He must accept the loss and cannot pursue any further claim.
B) He may reinstate his original claim against the defendant in full.
C) He may only claim proportional compensation from the third party.
D) He must return the property to the defendant and litigate with the reclaimer.

Key Takeaway
A settlement with silence or denial is conditional; if the property or compensation is reclaimed by a third party, the parties revert to their original legal positions.

6 min
Settling for Part of the Claimed Property: Invalidity and Stratagem
States ruling and provides two legal stratagems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on settling for part of a claimed property and apply the legal stratagem to make it valid.

Core Idea
Part cannot compensate the whole; stratagem of extra payment or claim release salvages the settlement.

When a plaintiff settles for only part of the property he claims, the settlement is invalid because part cannot serve as compensation for the whole. The Hanafi school provides two legal stratagems to make it valid: adding extra compensation or releasing the claim for the remainder. This lesson explains the ruling, its rationale, and the practical stratagems.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is settling for part of a claimed property (e.g., a house claimed in full but settled for a specific room). The ruling is that this settlement is invalid (fasid) unless a stratagem is used.
The settlement leaves the remaining claim intact.
2
Trace the legal reasoning (illa) for invalidity
Part cannot compensate the whole because it would be a thing compensating itself (the part is included in the whole). Also, relinquishment (isqat) does not apply to specific properties (a'yan), only to debts.
The plaintiff receives part of his own right, so he still has a claim for the rest.
3
Learn the two stratagems (hila)
Stratagem 1: The defendant adds something extra to the compensation (beyond the part), so the extra becomes compensation for the remainder. Stratagem 2: The plaintiff releases the defendant from the claim of the remaining part (ibra' from da'wa, not from the property itself).
Ibra' from the property itself ('ayn) is invalid; only ibra' from the claim is valid.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The narration of Ibn Samaca holds the settlement valid absolutely without a stratagem. However, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position requires the stratagem, as stated in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and al-Tahtawi.
The practical consequence: with stratagem, the claim is extinguished; without it, the plaintiff can still sue for the rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولو صالح على بعض دار يدعيها لم يصح ، وحيلته أن يزيد في البدل شيئاً ، أو يُبْراً (ولو صالح على بعض دار يدعيها لم يصح، وحيلته أن يزيد في البدل شيئاً ، أو يُبرا) [1] قوله : ولو صالح ... الخ ؛ يعني لو ادعى رجلٌ على آخر دارا فصالحه على قطعةٍ معلومة من تلك الدَّارِ لا يصح هذا الصلح ، وهو على دعوى في الباقي ؛ لأنَّ البعض لا يصلح عوضاً عن الكل للزوم أن يكون الشئ عوضاً عن نفسه، إذ البعض داخل في ضمن الكل، ولأنَّ ما قبضه من عين حقه فيكون على طلبه في باقي الدار، إذ الإسقاط لا يقع عن الأعيان ؛ لكونه مخصوصاً بالديون. [٢) قوله : وحيلته ؛ يعني إن حيلة جواز هذا الصلح أن يزيد المدعى عليه في البدل شيئاً، فيصير الزائد عوضاً عن الباقي، أو يبرأ عن دعوى الباقي، بأن يقول المدعي : أبرأتك أو برأت عن دعوى هذه الدار ؛ لأنّ الإبراء عن دعوى العين جائز، صرح به الشمني ) ، وغيره. وإن كان الإبراء عن العين غير جائز كما إذا مات أحد ، فبرئ واحد من ورثته عن نصيبه من التركة، فلا يصح؛ لأنّ هذه براءة العين، واعلم أنّ ما في «المتن» رواية ابن سماعة ، وظاهر الرواية : الصحة مطلقاً ؛ أي ولو من غير هذه الحيلة، فلا تصح الدعوى بعده ، وإن برهن ومشى عليه في الاختيار (۳)، كما في «الدر المختار» وحاشيته للعلامة الطحطاوي . [۳] قوله : أو يبرأ ؛ بضم الياء، وفتح الراء - ، على صيغة المجهول، والضمير
His saying: 'And if he settles for part of a house he claims, it is not valid, and its stratagem is that he adds something to the compensation, or he is released (from the claim).' (And if he settles for part of a house he claims, it is not valid, and its stratagem is that he adds something to the compensation, or he is released). [1] His saying: 'And if he settles...' means: If a man claims a house from another and settles for a specific piece of that house, this settlement is not valid, and he remains on his claim for the remainder. Because part cannot serve as compensation for the whole, since it would necessitate that a thing serve as compensation for itself, as the part is included within the whole. Also, what he receives is part of his very right, so he remains seeking the rest of the house, because relinquishment (isqat) does not apply to specific properties (a'yan), being restricted to debts. [2) His saying: 'And its stratagem' means: The stratagem to make this settlement permissible is that the defendant adds something to the compensation, so the extra becomes compensation for the remainder, or he (the plaintiff) releases (yubri') the claim for the remainder, by the plaintiff saying: 'I release you' or 'you are released from the claim of this house.' Because release (ibra') from a claim regarding a specific property is permissible, as stated by al-Shamni and others. However, release (ibra') from the specific property itself is not permissible, as when someone dies and one of his heirs releases his share of the estate, that is not valid because it is a release of the property itself. Know that what is in the Matn is the narration of Ibn Samaca, and the apparent narration is that it is valid absolutely, i.e., even without this stratagem, so the claim cannot be made afterwards, even if he proves it. And this is adopted in al-Ikhtiyar, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss by al-Tahtawi. [3] His saying: 'or he is released' (aw yubra') - with a damma on the ya, and a fatha on the ra, on the passive form, and the pronoun...
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a plaintiff settles for only part of the property he claims, e.g., he claims a whole house but settles for a specific room inside it. The commentator rules that such a settlement is invalid (lam yasihh). The reason (illa) is that part cannot be valid compensation for the whole, because that would effectively make the thing compensation for itself—the part is included in the whole. Moreover, what the plaintiff receives is part of his own right, so he still has a claim for the remainder; relinquishment (isqat) applies only to debts, not to specific tangible properties (a'yan). The author then provides two legal stratagems (hila) to make such a settlement valid: (1) The defendant adds something extra to the compensation (e.g., a small additional amount), so the extra becomes the true compensation for the part the plaintiff gives up, and the par no longer serves as compensation for the whole. (2) The plaintiff explicitly releases (yubri') the defendant from the claim of the remaining part of the house. The commentary clarifies that ibra' from a claim (da'wa) regarding a specific property is valid, but ibra' from the property itself (al-'ayn) is not. For example, if an heir releases his share of the estate, that is invalid because it is a release of the property itself. The passage also notes that according to a narration from Ibn Samaca, the settlement for part is valid even without any stratagem, and this view is supported by some later Hanafi authorities like the author of al-Ikhtiyar. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to the majority is the one requiring the stratagem. The lesson thus combines a substantive ruling with a practical legal stratagem and an internal Hanafi difference of opinion.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem or device that allows a transaction to be valid while circumventing a legal impediment, in this case making a settlement for part of the property permissible.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): A release or discharge from a claim or obligation. Here, it specifically refers to the plaintiff releasing the defendant from the claim for the remaining part of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what makes it valid to settle for part of the claimed property?
A) The plaintiff must sue for the rest.
B) The defendant adds something extra to the compensation or the plaintiff releases the claim for the remainder.
C) The defendant must pay the full value of the entire property.
D) The settlement is always valid regardless.

Key Takeaway
A settlement for part of a claimed property is invalid unless the defendant adds extra compensation or the plaintiff releases the claim for the remainder.

Settlement for Part of a Claimed Property and Its Validating Stratagem
Explains the invalidity of settlement for part of claimed property and the stratagem to make it valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of settlement for part of a claimed property according to the matn, identify the legal stratagem to make it valid, and distinguish between the Nawadir and Zahir al-Riwayah positions.

Core Idea
Part cannot compensate for the whole; the stratagem adds extra consideration or a release of the claim.

In Hanafi fiqh, a settlement (sulh) is a contract to end a dispute. But what happens when a claimant sues for a house and then settles for only a part of it? The matn (base text) rules such a settlement invalid because part cannot be a price for the whole. However, the commentator offers a legal stratagem (hila) to make it valid, and also informs us that the relied-upon view (Zahir al-Riwayah) holds the settlement valid without any stratagem. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the stratagem, and the intra-Hanafi difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sulh (settlement) where a claimant takes a specific part of the claimed property in exchange for dropping the whole claim. According to the matn, this is invalid (fasid).
The invalidity is due to the part being from the whole, causing a thing to be price for itself.
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
The part is included within the whole, so taking part as compensation for the whole means the claimant receives something that already belongs to him in a sense. Also, releasing rights to the remaining part is not valid for physical assets (ayn) – only for debts.
The key principle: al-ibra' 'an al-ayn is invalid; al-ibra' 'an al-da'wa is valid.
3
Learn the legal stratagem (hila)
Two ways: (1) the defendant adds extra consideration (e.g., a dirham) to make the total compensation cover both the part and the remainder; (2) the plaintiff explicitly releases (ibra') the defendant from the claim for the remainder.
The release must be from the claim, not from the property itself. E.g., 'I release you from my claim regarding the remainder.'
4
Analyze the different types of ibra'
The text distinguishes ibra' from ayn (invalid for physical property), ibra' from dayn (valid), and ibra' from da'wa (valid). It also discusses specific vs. general expressions and insha' vs. ikhbar.
This detail helps in drafting valid releases in practice.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The matn (Ibn Sama'a's narration) requires the hila. However, Zahir al-Riwayah (the six books of Imam Muhammad) holds the settlement valid absolutely, even without hila. This is the mufta bihi position.
A mufti should follow the Zahir al-Riwayah view, but must be aware of the alternative to respond to questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو صالح على بعض دار يدعيها لم يصح، وحيلته أن يزيد في البدل شيئاً، أو يُبْراً. وقال الآخر : اشتريت، حيث يرجع المدعي عند الاستحقاق على المدعى عليه بالمدعى نفسه لا بالدعوى ؛ لأن إقدام المدعى عليه على المبايعة إقرار منه بأن المدعى ملك المدعي فلا يعتبر إنكاره بخلاف الصلح ؛ لأنه لم يوجد منه ما يدل على أنه أقر بالملك له ، إذ الصلح قد يقع لدفع الخصومة. ذكره في «التبيين»، وغيره. قوله : ولو صالح ... الخ ؛ يعني لو ادعى رجلٌ على آخر دارا فصالحه على قطعةٍ معلومة من تلك الدَّارِ لا يصح هذا الصلح ، وهو على دعوى في الباقي ؛ لأنَّ البعض لا يصلح عوضاً عن الكل للزوم أن يكون الشئ عوضاً عن نفسه، إذ البعض داخل في ضمن الكل، ولأنَّ ما قبضه من عين حقه فيكون على طلبه في باقي الدار، إذ الإسقاط لا يقع عن الأعيان ؛ لكونه مخصوصاً بالديون. قوله : وحيلته ؛ يعني إن حيلة جواز هذا الصلح أن يزيد المدعى عليه في البدل شيئاً، فيصير الزائد عوضاً عن الباقي، أو يبرأ عن دعوى الباقي، بأن يقول المدعي : أبرأتك أو برأت عن دعوى هذه الدار ؛ لأنّ الإبراء عن دعوى العين جائز، صرح به الشمني، وغيره. وإن كان الإبراء عن العين غير جائز كما إذا مات أحد ، فبرئ واحد من ورثته عن نصيبه من التركة، فلا يصح؛ لأنّ هذه براءة العين، واعلم أنّ ما في «المتن» رواية ابن سماعة ، وظاهر الرواية : الصحة مطلقاً ؛ أي ولو من غير هذه الحيلة، فلا تصح الدعوى بعده ، وإن برهن ومشى عليه في الاختيار، كما في «الدر المختار» وحاشيته للعلامة الطحطاوي . قوله : أو يبرأ ؛ بضم الياء، وفتح الراء - ، على صيغة المجهول، والضمير عن دعوى الباقي : أي إنما لم يصح؛ لأنَّ بعض الدار لا يصح عوضاً عن الكل، فإذا زاد في البدل شيئاً كدرهم، أو ثوب، يكون ذلك عوضاً عما بقي في يدِ المدعى عليه، وإن أبرأه المدعي عن دعوى الباقي يصح أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ هذه براءة عن دعوى الأعيان، وهي صحيحة، وإن لم يكن البراءة عن الأعيان صحيحة راجع إلى المدعى عليه، أو بضم الياء، وكسر الراء - ، على صيغة المعروف ، والضمير راجع إلى المدعي ؛ أي يُبرِئ المدَّعي المدعى عليه، اعلم أنَّ الإبراء إن كان على وجه الإنشاء. فإن كان عن العين بطل من حيث الدعوى، فله الدعوى بها على المخاطب وغيره، ويصح من حيث نفي الضمان فإن كان عن دعواها ، فإن أضاف الإبراء إلى المخاطب كابرأتك عن هذه الدار ، أو عن خصومتي فيها ، أو عن دعواي فيها لا تسمع دعواه على المخاطب فقط. وإن أضافه إلى نفسه، كقوله : برئت عنها ، أو أنا بريء، فلا تسمع مطلقاً، هذا لو على طريق الخصوص: أي عين مخصوصة، فلو على العموم فله الدعوى على المخاطب وغيره، كما لو تبارأ الزوجان عن جميع الدعاوى، وله أعيان قائمة له الدعوى بها ؛ لأنه ينصرف إلى الديون لا إلى الأعيان. أما إذا كان على وجه الإخبار كقوله : هو بريء ما لي قبله، فهو صحيح متناول للدين والعين، فلا تسمع الدعوى، وكذا لا تلك لي في هذه العين. ذكره في «المبسوط» و«المحيط». فعلم أن قوله : لا استحق قبله حقاً مطلقاً، ولا دعوى يمنع الدعوى بالعين والدين؛ لما في «المبسوط»: لا حق لي قبله يشمل كل يمين ودين ، فلو ادعى حقاً لم يسمع ما لم يشهدوا أنه بعد البراءة. كذا في «البحر»، وفيه تفصيل إن شئت فارجع إليه.
And if he settles for part of a house he claims, it is not valid, and its stratagem is that he increases in the consideration something, or he (the plaintiff) discharges. And the other (party) said: I bought, where the claimant returns upon entitlement against the defendant with the claimed thing itself, not with the claim; because the defendant's proceeding to the sale is an acknowledgment that the claimed is the property of the claimant, so his denial is not considered, unlike the settlement; because nothing has occurred from him indicating that he acknowledged ownership to him, for settlement may occur to repel litigation. Mentioned in al-Tabyin, etc. The author's statement: 'And if he settles...' means if a man claims a house from another and he settles with him for a known piece of that house, this settlement is not valid, and he remains upon the claim for the remainder; because the part is not suitable as compensation for the whole due to the necessity that a thing be compensation for itself, since the part is included within the whole, and because what he received is from the very right, so he is upon his demand for the remaining part of the house, for dropping (rights) does not occur regarding corporeal property, being specific to debts. The author's statement: 'its stratagem' means the stratagem for the validity of this settlement is that the defendant increases something in the consideration, so the increase becomes compensation for the remainder, or he discharges the claim for the remainder, by the plaintiff saying: I discharge you or I am discharged from the claim for this house; because discharge from the claim of a corporeal property is permissible, stated by al-Shumni and others. Although discharge from the corporeal property itself is not permissible, as when someone dies and one of his heirs is discharged from his share of the estate, it is not valid; because this is a discharge of the corporeal property. Know that what is in the text is a transmission of Ibn Sama'a, and the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah) is validity absolutely, i.e. even without this stratagem, so the claim cannot be made afterward, even if he produces proof, and it is adopted in al-Ikhtiyar, as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its hashiyah by al-Allamah al-Tahtawi. The author's statement: 'or he is discharged' – with dammah on the ya and fatha on the ra, on the passive voice, and the pronoun refers to 'the claim for the remainder': i.e. it is not valid because part of the house is not suitable as compensation for the whole, so if he increases in the consideration something like a dirham or a garment, that becomes compensation for what remains in the defendant's hand, and if the plaintiff discharges him from the claim for the remainder, it is also valid; because this is a discharge from the claim of corporeal properties, and it is valid, although the discharge from corporeal properties themselves is not valid – or with dammah on the ya and kasr on the ra, on the active voice, and the pronoun refers to the plaintiff: i.e. the plaintiff discharges the defendant. Know that discharge, if it is by way of creation (insha'), if it is from the corporeal property itself, it is invalid from the perspective of the claim, so he has the claim for it against the addressee and others, and it is valid from the perspective of negating liability; but if it is from the claim of it, then if he adds the discharge to the addressee, like 'I discharge you from this house' or 'from my contention in it' or 'from my claim in it', his claim is not heard only against the addressee. And if he adds it to himself, like his saying 'I am discharged from it' or 'I am free', then it is not heard absolutely. This is if it is specific, i.e. a specific property; if it is general, then he has the claim against the addressee and others, like when the two spouses discharge each other from all claims, and he has extant properties, he has the claim for them; because it (the general discharge) is understood to refer to debts, not to properties. As for if it is by way of information (ikhbar), like his saying 'he is free, nothing is due to me from him', then it is valid and encompasses debt and property, so the claim is not heard, and likewise 'no thing is for me in this property'. Mentioned in al-Mabsut and al-Muhit. You know that his saying 'I have no entitlement from him absolutely, and no claim' prevents the claim for property and debt; because in al-Mabsut: 'no right is due to me from him' includes every oath and debt, so if he claims a right, it is not heard unless they testify that it is after the discharge. Likewise in al-Bahr, and it has detail; if you wish, refer to it.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah deals with the ruling on settling (sulh) for part of a claimed property. The author begins by stating the matn: if a person claims a house from another and they settle for a specific part of that house, the settlement is invalid. The reason is that part cannot be compensation for the whole, as it would entail a thing being compensation for itself. The claimant remains entitled to sue for the remainder. The commentator then explains the legal stratagem (hila) to make such a settlement valid: either the defendant adds something extra (like a dirham) to the consideration, or the plaintiff explicitly releases the defendant from the claim for the remainder. The release (ibra') from the claim of a corporeal property is valid, whereas release from the corporeal property itself is not. Importantly, the commentator notes an intra-Hanafi difference: the matn follows the narration of Ibn Sama'a (Nawadir), which holds invalidity without the stratagem. However, Zahir al-Riwayah (the relied-upon transmission) holds the settlement valid absolutely, even without any stratagem, and this is the position adopted in al-Ikhtiyar, al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and al-Tahtawi's hashiyah. The passage then delves into a detailed discussion of ibra' (discharge), distinguishing between insha' (creating discharge) and ikhbar (informing), and between specific and general expressions. It explains the effect on the right to sue: if the discharge is from the claim specifically and addressed to the defendant, the plaintiff cannot sue that defendant; if general, he can sue both the defendant and others for specific properties, as the general discharge is understood to apply to debts, not physical assets. If the discharge is phrased as an acknowledgment of having no right, it bars all claims. The passage concludes by referencing al-Mabsut, al-Muhit, and al-Bahr for further details.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contract of compromise or settlement, often for resolving a dispute.
حيلة (hila): A legal stratagem or way to achieve a lawful result without violating the rules.
إبراء (ibra'): A legal release or discharge from a right or claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the legal stratagem (hila) to make a settlement for part of the claimed property valid?
A) The defendant adds extra consideration or the plaintiff releases the claim.
B) The plaintiff pays the defendant extra money.
C) The settlement must be written and witnessed.
D) The defendant must admit ownership in court.

Key Takeaway
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Zahir al-Riwayah), a settlement for part of a claimed property is valid without any stratagem, contrary to the matn's ruling which requires a hila.

Ibra' from Claim vs. Property: Validity and Scope
Explains the validity of ibra' from claim vs. property, and the distinction between inšā' and ikhbār.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ibra' from a claim and ibra' from the property itself, and state the Hanafi ruling for each, including the effect of different phrasings.

Core Idea
Discharging a claim is valid; discharging the property itself is invalid – only liability is removed.

The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between a discharge from the right to sue (ibra' from the claim) and a discharge from the property itself (ibra' from the 'ayn). This lesson unpacks the passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah to clarify when a plaintiff's statement bars them from future litigation and when it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of ibra'
Locate where the passage discusses ibra' and note whether it is framed as inšā' (creating) or ikhbār (informing).
The text explicitly divides ibra' into these two categories.
2
Distinguish ibra' from claim vs. property
Read the passage to find the ruling for discharging the claim itself versus discharging the specific property. Note the different consequences.
Ibra' from the property is invalid regarding claims but valid for negating liability.
3
Analyze the phrasing of inšā' ibra'
Examine the examples given: 'abra'tuka min hadhihi al-dār' vs. 'bari'tu 'anha' or 'anā barī'un'. Understand who is barred from suing in each case.
Addressee‑specific phrasing bars only that addressee; self‑attribution bars all.
4
Contrast with ikhbār ibra'
Read the passage on ikhbār (e.g., 'huwa barī'un, mā lī qibalahu shay'). Note that it covers both debt and property, barring all claims.
Ikhbār is comprehensive and does not require a specific addressee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلح تعريفه وأقسامه
عن دعوى الباقي : أي إنما لم يصح؛ لأنَّ بعض الدار لا يصح عوضاً عن الكل، فإذا زاد في البدل شيئاً كدرهم، أو ثوب، يكون ذلك عوضاً عما بقي في يدِ المدعى عليه، وإن أبرأه المدعي عن دعوى الباقي يصح أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ هذه براءة عن دعوى الأعيان، وهي صحيحة، وإن لم يكن البراءة عن الأعيان صحيحة راجع إلى المدعى عليه، أو بضم الياء، وكسر الراء - على صيغة المعروف، والضمير راجع إلى المدعي ؛ أي يُبرِئ المدَّعي المدعى عليه، اعلم أنَّ الإبراء إن كان على وجه الإنشاء.
فإن كان عن العين بطل من حيث الدعوى، فله الدعوى بها على المخاطب وغيره، ويصح من حيث نفي الضمان فإن كان عن دعواها ، فإن أضاف الإبراء إلى المخاطب كابرأتك عن هذه الدار ، أو عن خصومتي فيها ، أو عن دعواي فيها لا تسمع دعواه على المخاطب فقط. وإن أضافه إلى نفسه، كقوله : برئت عنها ، أو أنا بريء، فلا تسمع مطلقاً، هذا لو على طريق الخصوص: أي عين مخصوصة، فلو على العموم فله الدعوى على المخاطب وغيره، كما لو تبارأ الزوجان عن جميع الدعاوى، وله أعيان قائمة له الدعوى بها ؛ لأنه ينصرف إلى الديون لا إلى الأعيان.
أما إذا كان على وجه الإخبار كقوله : هو بريء ما لي قبله، فهو صحيح متناول للدين والعين، فلا تسمع الدعوى، وكذا لا تلك لي في هذه العين. ذكره في «المبسوط» و«المحيط».
فعلم أن قوله : لا استحق قبله حقاً مطلقاً، ولا دعوى يمنع الدعوى بالعين والدين؛ لما في «المبسوط»: لا حق لي قبله يشمل كل يمين ودين ، فلو ادعى حقاً لم يسمع ما لم يشهدوا أنه بعد البراءة. كذا في «البحر » ) ، وفيه تفصيل إن شئت فارجع إليه.
[١]قوله : عن دعوى الباقي ؛ قال الشامي في حاشيته على «الدر المختار»(۲) : قيد بالبراءة عن دعواه؛ لأنّ الإبراء عن عينه غير صحيح كذا في «المبسوط»، إن ملك بأن يقول : برئت عنها أو عن خصومتي فيها أو عن دعوى هذه الدار، فلا تسمع دعواه ، ولا بينته ، وأما لو قال : أبرأتك عنها أو عن خصومتي فيها ، فإنّه باطل، وله أن يخاصم كما لو قال لمن بيده عبد : برئت منه ؛ فإنّه يبرأ ، ولو قال : أبرأتك لا لأنه إنما أبرأه عن ضمانه. كما في «الأشباه» من «أحكام الدين». قلت : ففرقوا بين : أبرأتك ، وبرئت، وأنا بريء ؛ لإضافة البراءة لنفسه، فتعم بخلاف أبرأتك ؛ لأنه خطاب الواحدِ فله مخاصمة غيره، كما في «حاشيتها» مغـريا [؟] للولوالجية وشرح الملتقى. انتهى.
والفرق بينهما يَظْهَرُ فيما إذا كان الدَّارُ في يد المدعى عليه، فيبرأُ المدعي عن دعواها يصح، وإن لم تكن في يد المدعى عليه كما إذا مات واحد، وترك ميراثاً، فبرئ واحد عن نصيبه لا يصح ؛ لأنه هذه براءة عن الأعيان.
Book of Sulh: Its Definition and Sections. Concerning claim of the remainder: That is, it is not valid because a part of the house cannot be a substitute for the whole. So if he adds something to the substitute, like a dirham or a garment, that becomes a substitute for what remains in the hand of the defendant. And if the plaintiff discharges him from the claim of the remainder, it is also valid; because this is a discharge from the claim of specific properties, and it is sound. And if the discharge from the specific properties is not sound, the pronoun refers to the defendant (or with the vowel yā' and kasr of rā' in the passive form, and the pronoun refers to the plaintiff: i.e., the plaintiff discharges the defendant). Know that discharge if it is in the manner of creation (inšā'). If it is concerning the property itself, it is invalid in terms of the claim, so he has the right to claim it against the addressee and others, and it is valid in terms of negating liability. If it is concerning the claim of the property, then if he attributes the discharge to the addressee, like saying 'I discharge you from this house' or 'from my litigation in it' or 'from my claim in it', his claim is not heard against the addressee only. And if he attributes it to himself, like saying 'I am free from it' or 'I am free', then it is not heard absolutely. This is if it is in a specific manner: i.e., a specific property. If in a general manner, then he has the right to claim against the addressee and others, like if two spouses mutually discharge each other from all claims, and he has existing properties, he has the right to claim them; because it refers to debts, not properties. As for if it is in the manner of informing (ikhbār), like his saying 'He is free, I have nothing due from him', then it is sound, covering debt and property, so the claim is not heard. Similarly, 'I have no such thing in this property'. It is mentioned in al-Mabsūt and al-Muhīt. So it is known that his saying 'I have no right absolutely due from him, and no claim' prevents the claim of property and debt; because in al-Mabsūt: 'No right is due from him' includes every oath and debt. So if he claims a right, it is not heard unless they testify that it is after the discharge. Thus in al-Bahr, and there is detail if you wish, refer to it. [1] His statement: 'concerning claim of the remainder'; al-Shāmī said in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtār: it is restricted by discharge from his claim, because discharge from the property itself is not sound, as in al-Mabsūt. If the owner says 'I am free from it' or 'from my litigation in it' or 'from the claim of this house', then his claim is not heard, nor his evidence. But if he says 'I discharge you from it' or 'from my litigation in it', then it is void, and he has the right to litigate, like if he says to the one in whose hand is a slave: 'I am free from him', then he is free (discharged). If he says 'I discharge you', it is not (effective) because he only discharged him from its liability. As in al-Ashbāh from 'Ahkām al-Dayn'. I said: They distinguished between 'abra'tuka' and 'bari'tu' and 'anā barī'un'; because attributing the discharge to oneself makes it general, contrary to 'abra'tuka' because it is addressed to one, so he can sue others, as in its gloss from al-Walwālijiyya and Sharh al-Multaqā. End. The difference between them becomes apparent in the case when the house is in the hand of the defendant: if the plaintiff discharges him from his claim, it is valid. But if it is not in the hand of the defendant, like when a person dies and leaves inheritance, and one discharges (something) from his share, it is not valid; because this is a discharge from specific properties.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the legal nuances of ibra' (discharge) in property disputes within the Hanafi school. The author begins by explaining why part of a house cannot serve as substitute for the whole in a settlement, but a discharge from the claim of the remainder is valid because it is a discharge from a claim, not from the property itself. He then distinguishes between two forms of ibra': inšā' (creating) and ikhbār (informing). For ibra' in the form of inšā', if the discharge is directed at the property itself (e.g., 'I discharge you from this house'), it is invalid regarding the claim but valid in negating liability. If directed at the claim (e.g., 'I discharge you from my claim on this house'), it bars the claim only against the addressee. However, if the discharge is attributed to oneself (e.g., 'I am free from it'), it bars the claim absolutely against all. In contrast, ibra' in the form of ikhbār (e.g., 'He is free, I have nothing due from him') is valid for both debt and property, preventing any claim. The commentator then cites al-Shāmī's gloss, which clarifies that the ruling is restricted to discharge from the claim, not the property itself. A key distinction is made between 'abra'tuka' (I discharge you) and 'bari'tu' (I am free): the latter, being self-attribution, is general and bars claims against all, while the former is specific to the addressee. The practical difference is illustrated by a case where the property is in the defendant's hand: discharge from the claim is valid; if not in his hand (e.g., inheritance share), it is invalid because it amounts to discharge from the property itself.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibrā'): Legal discharge from a claim or liability, either by creating (inšā') or informing (ikhbār).
المدعى عليه (al-mudda‘ā ‘alayhi): The defendant against whom a claim is made.
البراءة عن الأعيان (al-barā’a ‘an al-a‘yān): Discharge from the specific property itself, as opposed to discharge from the claim on it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a plaintiff says to the defendant 'abra'tuka min hadhihi al-dār' (I discharge you from this house), what is the effect on the plaintiff's right to claim the house from others?
A) The plaintiff cannot claim the house from anyone.
B) The plaintiff can claim the house from the defendant but not from others.
C) The plaintiff can claim the house from others but not from the defendant.
D) The plaintiff can claim the house from anyone, including the defendant.

Key Takeaway
Ibra' from a claim is valid; ibra' from the property itself is invalid regarding claims but valid for negating liability; and the scope depends on whether the phrasing is addressee-specific or self-attributed.

The Scope of Ibra': Self-Attribution vs. Addressee-Attribution
Explains the difference between 'abra'tuka' and 'bari'tu' in ibra', and the scope of each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between self-attributed ibra' (bari'tu) and addressee-attributed ibra' (abra'tuka) to determine who is barred from being sued.

Core Idea
Self-attributed ibra' bars all claims; addressee-attributed ibra' bars only that addressee.

The Hanafi school derives a precise rule from the phrasing of ibra': if a plaintiff says 'bari'tu' (I am free) or 'anā barī'un', the discharge is general and prevents any future claim against anyone. If they say 'abra'tuka' (I discharge you), only the person addressed is protected. This lesson focuses on the passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah that clarifies this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Find the two phrasings
In the source passage, locate the two types of inšā' ibra': one where the plaintiff adds the discharge to himself (bari'tu), and one where he adds it to the addressee (abra'tuka).
The text gives examples: 'bari'tu 'anhā' and 'abra'tuka 'anhā'.
2
Identify the legal effect for each
Read the consequences: 'abra'tuka' bars claim only against the addressee; 'bari'tu' bars claim absolutely.
The passage says: 'fa in aḍāfahu ilā al-mukhāṭab... lā tusma‘u da‘wāhu ‘alā al-mukhāṭab faqaṭ; wa in aḍāfahu ilā nafsihi... falā tusma‘u muṭlaqan'.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the distinction
The gloss by al-Shāmī explains that self-attribution (bari'tu) is general because it relates to the plaintiff's own claim, while addressee-attribution (abra'tuka) is specific to that person.
Al-Shāmī: 'li-iḍāfat al-barā’a li-nafsihi fa ta‘ummu bi-khilāf abra'tuka li-annahu khiṭāb al-wāḥid'.
4
Apply the rule to a general discharge
Consider the case of a general discharge (e.g., spouses discharging each other from all claims). The passage says this refers to debts, not properties, so the plaintiff can still claim properties.
This is an exception: general ibra' does not cover properties unless explicitly stated.

Source Text

أما إذا كان على وجه الإخبار كقوله : هو بريء ما لي قبله، فهو صحيح متناول للدين والعين، فلا تسمع الدعوى، وكذا لا تلك لي في هذه العين. ذكره في «المبسوط» و«المحيط».
فعلم أن قوله : لا استحق قبله حقاً مطلقاً، ولا دعوى يمنع الدعوى بالعين والدين؛ لما في «المبسوط»: لا حق لي قبله يشمل كل يمين ودين ، فلو ادعى حقاً لم يسمع ما لم يشهدوا أنه بعد البراءة. كذا في «البحر » ) ، وفيه تفصيل إن شئت فارجع إليه.

Book of Sulh: Its Definition and Sections. Concerning claim of the remainder: That is, it is not valid because a part of the house cannot be a substitute for the whole. So if he adds something to the substitute, like a dirham or a garment, that becomes a substitute for what remains in the hand of the defendant. And if the plaintiff discharges him from the claim of the remainder, it is also valid; because this is a discharge from the claim of specific properties, and it is sound. And if the discharge from the specific properties is not sound, the pronoun refers to the defendant (or with the vowel yā' and kasr of rā' in the passive form, and the pronoun refers to the plaintiff: i.e., the plaintiff discharges the defendant). Know that discharge if it is in the manner of creation (inšā'). If it is concerning the property itself, it is invalid in terms of the claim, so he has the right to claim it against the addressee and others, and it is valid in terms of negating liability. If it is concerning the claim of the property, then if he attributes the discharge to the addressee, like saying 'I discharge you from this house' or 'from my litigation in it' or 'from my claim in it', his claim is not heard against the addressee only. And if he attributes it to himself, like saying 'I am free from it' or 'I am free', then it is not heard absolutely. This is if it is in a specific manner: i.e., a specific property. If in a general manner, then he has the right to claim against the addressee and others, like if two spouses mutually discharge each other from all claims, and he has existing properties, he has the right to claim them; because it refers to debts, not properties. As for if it is in the manner of informing (ikhbār), like his saying 'He is free, I have nothing due from him', then it is sound, covering debt and property, so the claim is not heard. Similarly, 'I have no such thing in this property'. It is mentioned in al-Mabsūt and al-Muhīt. So it is known that his saying 'I have no right absolutely due from him, and no claim' prevents the claim of property and debt; because in al-Mabsūt: 'No right is due from him' includes every oath and debt. So if he claims a right, it is not heard unless they testify that it is after the discharge. Thus in al-Bahr, and there is detail if you wish, refer to it. [1] His statement: 'concerning claim of the remainder'; al-Shāmī said in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtār: it is restricted by discharge from his claim, because discharge from the property itself is not sound, as in al-Mabsūt. If the owner says 'I am free from it' or 'from my litigation in it' or 'from the claim of this house', then his claim is not heard, nor his evidence. But if he says 'I discharge you from it' or 'from my litigation in it', then it is void, and he has the right to litigate, like if he says to the one in whose hand is a slave: 'I am free from him', then he is free (discharged). If he says 'I discharge you', it is not (effective) because he only discharged him from its liability. As in al-Ashbāh from 'Ahkām al-Dayn'. I said: They distinguished between 'abra'tuka' and 'bari'tu' and 'anā barī'un'; because attributing the discharge to oneself makes it general, contrary to 'abra'tuka' because it is addressed to one, so he can sue others, as in its gloss from al-Walwālijiyya and Sharh al-Multaqā. End. The difference between them becomes apparent in the case when the house is in the hand of the defendant: if the plaintiff discharges him from his claim, it is valid. But if it is not in the hand of the defendant, like when a person dies and leaves inheritance, and one discharges (something) from his share, it is not valid; because this is a discharge from specific properties.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the legal nuances of ibra' (discharge) in property disputes within the Hanafi school. The author begins by explaining why part of a house cannot serve as substitute for the whole in a settlement, but a discharge from the claim of the remainder is valid because it is a discharge from a claim, not from the property itself. He then distinguishes between two forms of ibra': inšā' (creating) and ikhbār (informing). For ibra' in the form of inšā', if the discharge is directed at the property itself (e.g., 'I discharge you from this house'), it is invalid regarding the claim but valid in negating liability. If directed at the claim (e.g., 'I discharge you from my claim on this house'), it bars the claim only against the addressee. However, if the discharge is attributed to oneself (e.g., 'I am free from it'), it bars the claim absolutely against all. In contrast, ibra' in the form of ikhbār (e.g., 'He is free, I have nothing due from him') is valid for both debt and property, preventing any claim. The commentator then cites al-Shāmī's gloss, which clarifies that the ruling is restricted to discharge from the claim, not the property itself. A key distinction is made between 'abra'tuka' (I discharge you) and 'bari'tu' (I am free): the latter, being self-attribution, is general and bars claims against all, while the former is specific to the addressee. The practical difference is illustrated by a case where the property is in the defendant's hand: discharge from the claim is valid; if not in his hand (e.g., inheritance share), it is invalid because it amounts to discharge from the property itself.

Key Terms
أبرأتك (abra'tuka): Form of ibra' where the discharge is attributed to the addressee ('I discharge you').
برئت (bari'tu): Form of ibra' where the discharge is attributed to oneself ('I am free').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A plaintiff says: 'I am free from my claim against you regarding this house.' According to the Hanafi school, whom does this ibra' bar from being sued?
A) Only the addressee (the defendant).
B) The addressee and all others who might be liable.
C) Only the plaintiff himself, not the defendant.
D) No one, because it is an invalid form.

Key Takeaway
The scope of ibra' depends on the pronoun: 'abra'tuka' is limited to the addressee; 'bari'tu' is absolute.

Difference Between 'Bari'tu' and 'Abra'tuka' in Legal Discharge
Distinguishes two forms of discharge in property claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid formulations of discharge (ibra') and apply the Hanafi rule regarding possession and wording.

Core Idea
Discharge from a claim is valid only when using self-attributed wording; addressing another person is insufficient.

In Hanafi law, the wording of discharge (ibra') determines its legal effect. A claimant may say 'I am free from' (bari'tu) or 'I discharge you' (abra'tuka). This lesson examines the subtle but crucial distinction between these two formulations, as explained by classical Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two formulations
Recognize 'bari'tu' (I am free from) and 'abra'tuka' (I discharge you) as the key terms.
2
Determine the object of discharge
Is the discharge about a physical asset ('ayn) or a debt/claim? The ruling differs.
3
Apply the possession condition
Check whether the property is in the defendant's possession. This affects validity.
4
State the ruling for each scenario
For 'bari'tu' with possession: valid; for 'bari'tu' without possession: invalid; for 'abra'tuka' in all cases regarding 'ayn: invalid.
5
Understand the ratio legis
Why does 'bari'tu' work? Because it is self-attributed, making the discharge general. 'Abra'tuka' is directed to a specific person, so it does not discharge the claim fully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق بينهما يَظْهَرُ فيما إذا كان الدَّارُ في يد المدعى عليه، فيبرأُ المدعي عن دعواها يصح، وإن لم تكن في يد المدعى عليه كما إذا مات واحد، وترك ميراثاً، فبرئ واحد عن نصيبه لا يصح ؛ لأنه هذه براءة عن الأعيان. بالإبراء عن دعواه ؛ لأنّ الإبراء عن عينه غير صحيح كذا في «المبسوط»، إن ملك بأن يقول : برئت عنها أو عن خصومتي فيها أو عن دعوى هذه الدار، فلا تسمع دعواه ، ولا بينته ، وأما لو قال : أبرأتك عنها أو عن خصومتي فيها ، فإنّه باطل، وله أن يخاصم كما لو قال لمن بيده عبد : برئت منه ؛ فإنّه يبرأ ، ولو قال : أبرأتك لا لأنه إنما أبرأه عن ضمانه. كما في «الأشباه» من «أحكام الدين». قلت : ففرقوا بين : أبرأتك ، وبرئت، وأنا بريء ؛ لإضافة البراءة لنفسه، فتعم بخلاف أبرأتك ؛ لأنه خطاب الواحدِ فله مخاصمة غيره، كما في «حاشيتها» مغـريـا «للولوالجية» و«شرح الملتقى». انتهى.
And the difference between them appears in the case when the house is in the possession of the defendant, then the plaintiff's discharge from his claim is valid, and if it is not in the possession of the defendant, such as when a man dies and leaves inheritance, so one [heir] discharges his share, it is not valid because this is a discharge of physical assets. [And] by discharge from his claim because the discharge from the physical asset itself is invalid. As in al-Mabsut: If he owns, he says: 'I am free from it' or 'from my litigation in it' or 'from the claim of this house', then his claim is not heard, nor his evidence. But if he says: 'I discharge you from it' or 'from my litigation in it', then it is invalid, and he may litigate. Just as if he said to one in whose hand is a slave: 'I am free from him', then he is free; and if he says: 'I discharge you', no, because he only discharged him from his guarantee. As in al-Ashbah from Ahkam al-Din. I said: So they differentiated between 'abra'tuka' and 'bari'tu' and 'ana bari'un'; because the attribution of discharge to oneself makes it general, unlike 'abra'tuka' because it is addressing one person, so he may litigate others, as in its gloss from al-Walwalijiyya and Sharh al-Multaqa. End.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal effect of different formulations of discharge (ibra') in the Hanafi school. The key distinction is between saying 'bari'tu' (I am free from) and 'abra'tuka' (I discharge you). When the property is in the defendant's possession, the plaintiff's discharge of his claim is valid. But if the property is not in his possession, like an heir discharging his share of inheritance, it is invalid because it constitutes a discharge of a physical asset ('ayn), which is not valid in Hanafi law. The author clarifies that if the owner says 'I am free from this house' or 'from my claim', his future claim will not be heard. However, if he says 'I discharge you', it is invalid and he retains the right to sue. This is analogous to saying to someone holding a slave: 'I am free from him' (valid) vs 'I discharge you' (invalid, as it only releases guarantee). The reasoning is that 'bari'tu' is attributed to oneself and thus general, while 'abra'tuka' is directed to a specific person, so it does not affect claims against others. This discussion is cited from authoritative Hanafi sources like al-Mabsut, al-Ashbah, and commentaries.

Key Terms
براءة (bara'ah): Legal discharge from a claim or liability.
إبراء (ibra'): Act of discharging someone from a debt or claim.
برئت (bari'tu): First person declaration of being free from a claim or asset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone says 'I am free from this house' while the house is in his possession, what is the ruling?
A) His claim is still heard
B) His claim is not heard
C) He must provide evidence
D) The discharge is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the validity of a discharge depends on the wording: self-attributed 'bari'tu' is effective for claims on property in possession, while addressing the other party with 'abra'tuka' is ineffective for physical assets.

Settlement for Property and Usufruct Claims
Establishes validity and conditions of sulh for mal and manfa'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the validity of sulh involving usufruct and apply the Hanafi analogy to determine when it is permissible.

Core Idea
Sulh for usufruct is valid only when the exchanged usufructs differ in type, analogizing to the rules of lease.

Sulh is a versatile contract used to resolve disputes. When it involves property, it resembles a sale; when it involves usufruct, it resembles a lease. However, to avoid riba, the Hanafi school imposes a critical condition: if usufruct is exchanged for usufruct, the types must differ. This lesson examines the ruling and the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims and settlement types
Distinguish between sulh for a claim of property (mal) and sulh for a claim of usufruct (manfa'ah). The ruling covers both, but the conditions differ.
The text first states validity for both, then narrows to usufruct conditions.
2
Understand the analogies (qiyas) to sale and lease
Sulh for property is 'in the meaning of sale' because it transfers ownership of a known asset. Sulh for usufruct is 'in the meaning of lease' because it grants temporary use. These analogies determine which rules apply.
The analogy to ijara triggers the prohibition of exchanging like for like.
3
Apply the jins condition for usufruct exchange
When usufruct is exchanged for usufruct, the two must be of different types (e.g., residence vs service). If the type is the same (e.g., residence for residence), the sulh is invalid due to riba, analogous to renting a usufruct for its like.
The text gives examples: sakan (residence) for khidmat (service) is valid; sakan for sakan is invalid.
4
Confirm the ruling and scope
The passage concludes that sulh for property is absolutely permissible, while sulh for usufruct is permissible only when types differ, with a mention from al-Bahr confirming this.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text

Section on categories of sulh. [1] (And sulh is valid for a claim of property and usufruct.) [2] It was said: The form of sulh for a claim of usufruct is that he claims against the heirs that the deceased had bequeathed the service of this slave, and the heirs deny. [1] His saying: And sulh for a claim of property is valid because it is in the meaning of sale regarding both parties if it occurs for property upon admission, and regarding the claimant alone if it occurs upon denial or silence, and regarding the other to avoid the oath and end litigation; and it is in the meaning of lease if it occurs for usufructs. All that is permissible as has passed. And the usufruct: it is owned by a lease contract, so similarly by sulh. Do you not see that if the heirs made sulh with the legatee of service for property or usufruct, it is permissible. So this is more appropriate because it is known, since it has a finite term. But sulh for usufructs in exchange for usufruct is only permissible if they are of different types, such as making sulh for residence in exchange for the slave's service, or cultivation of land, or wearing clothes. As for if their type is the same, such as making sulh for residence in exchange for residence, or for cultivation in exchange for cultivation, it is not permissible. Because it is not permissible to rent a usufruct for its own type of usufructs, so similarly sulh. And when the type differs, it is permissible to rent it for a usufruct, so similarly sulh. Al-Ayni and al-Zaylai mentioned it in Sharh al-Kanz. [2] His saying: It was said... etc.; thus it is mentioned in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj quoting from al-Mustasfa, but it is mentioned in al-Bahr that sulh for a claim of property absolutely and for usufruct is permissible, like the sulh of the lessee with the lessor when he denies the lease or the claimed period and amount of rent, and also the heirs when they make sulh with the legatee of service for property absolutely, and for usufructs if their types differ, then it is permissible; if they are the same, not. End.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of sulh (compromise/settlement) regarding claims of property and usufruct. The matn states that sulh is valid for such claims. The commentary elaborates: sulh for property is analogous to a sale if it is done for property upon an admission, and it serves to avoid an oath and end litigation. Sulh for usufruct is analogous to a lease because usufruct is owned through an ijara contract. However, there is a condition: if the sulh is exchanging one usufruct for another, the two must be of different types (e.g., right of residence for service of a slave) because exchanging usufruct of the same type would be like renting one usufruct for another of its kind, which is not allowed. The commentary also mentions a view from Al-Bahr stating that sulh for property is absolutely permissible, and for usufruct it is permissible if the types differ. This follows the Hanafi school's general principle that sulh is valid in meaning of sale or lease, while observing rules against riba.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): A contract of compromise to end a dispute, which in Hanafi fiqh can be in the meaning of sale, lease, or release.
المنفعة (al-manfa'ah): Usufruct or benefit, such as the right to use a property or service of a slave.
الجنس (al-jins): Type or category of usufruct (e.g., residence, cultivation, service).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The usufructs must be of the same type.
B) The usufructs must be of different types.
C) The usufructs must be mentioned in the Quran.
D) The usufructs must be for a fixed term only.

Key Takeaway
Sulh for usufruct is valid if the exchanged usufructs are of different types, following the rule of ijara to avoid riba.

Validity of Sulh for Criminal Offences (Jinayat)
Validates sulh for intentional and accidental homicide and injury.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sulh for criminal offences and cite the Qur'anic evidence (2:178) with its exegetical basis.

Core Idea
Sulh for intentional murder is valid; the Qur'an commands gracious conciliation.

This passage extends the validity of sulh from property disputes to criminal offences. The key proof is Qur'an 2:178, which commands 'gracious pursuit' and 'payment with good conduct' after remission. We will examine how the Hanafi school interprets this verse to allow settlement even for intentional homicide, and address a common misconception that sulh is limited to accidental cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the subject of discussion? (Sulh for criminal offences). What is the ruling? (Sulh is valid for all types of jinaayah: intentional or accidental, against life or limb).
The passage states: 'sulh is valid for criminal offences against the self and what is less than it, whether intentional or accidental.'
2
Locate the dalil
Which proof is cited? The Quranic verse 2:178. Identify the specific phrase used as evidence.
The verse: 'But whoever is granted some remission from his brother, then a gracious pursuit and payment to him with good conduct.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the verse prove the ruling? The verse commands the guardian to pursue payment graciously and the killer to pay promptly after remission. This necessarily implies that a valid sulh agreement for diyah exists.
The commentators (Ibn Abbas, Dahhak, Hasan) explicitly state that the verse was revealed concerning sulh over intentional blood.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Is there disagreement? Some earlier scholars restricted sulh to accidental cases. How does the Hanafi school respond?
The Hanafi position relies on the generality of the verse and the interpretation of early authorities.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
What is the mufta bihi position? Sulh is unconditionally permissible for all types of jinaayah. The strength comes from the explicit Quranic text and consensus of the Companions' interpretations.
The text cites tafsir of Ibn Abbas, Dahhak, and Hasan as evidence for intentional blood.

Source Text

And [sulh is valid] for criminal offences against the self and what is less than it, intentionally or accidentally. [1] His saying: Because the narration is preserved... etc., meaning we followed all the narrations in this issue and preserved them and did not find in them permission for sulh regarding a claim of hiring a specific object. [2] His saying: And criminal offences; it is conjoined to his saying: claim of property, i.e., sulh is valid for criminal offences against the self and what is less than it, whether intentional or accidental, whether upon admission or denial or silence. As for intentional against the self, the proof is the saying of the Most High: 'But whoever is granted some remission from his brother, then a gracious pursuit and payment to him with good conduct.' Ibn Abbas, Dahhak, and Hasan said: The verse was revealed concerning sulh over intentional blood. Al-Ayni, al-Zaylai, and al-Shami mentioned it. He said in Mafatih al-Tanzil: The meaning of the verse according to the majority is: Whoever is granted from his brother some remission, i.e., a portion of pardon—the verb is attributed to the source as in 'he walked with Zayd some walking'—and 'brother' is the guardian of the slain, and the use of 'brother' encourages sympathy due to common lineage and Islam, and the pronoun 'he' refers to the killer who is granted remission for his crime, and the other object is omitted. And it was said: 'he' is used in place of 'him', and the pronoun in 'for him' and 'his brother' refers to the person, and 'to him' refers to the brother or the pursuer. So 'pursuit' means the claimant should follow the killer with good conduct, asking graciously, and the obligated (the killer) should pay the blood money with good conduct, neither delaying nor reducing.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage extends the validity of sulh to claims arising from criminal offenses (jinaayat), whether the crime is intentional or accidental, and whether it involves life (murder) or less than life (injuries). The primary proof is the Qur'anic verse 2:178. The commentators Ibn Abbas, Dahhak, and Hasan explain that this verse was revealed concerning sulh over intentional blood (i.e., compromise accepting diyah instead of qisas). The commentary then provides a detailed exegesis of the verse: 'whoever is granted some remission from his brother' means the guardian of the slain accepts diyah. The word 'brother' is used to encourage kindness. Then 'a gracious pursuit' commands the guardian to pursue payment courteously, and 'payment to him with good conduct' commands the killer to pay promptly and fully. This establishes the permissibility of sulh in homicide and injury cases. The Hanafi school allows sulh for all types of jinaayah, whether based on admission or denial, and whether intentional or accidental. The passage also includes a note that there is no preserved narration permitting sulh for a claim of hiring a specific object (istijar al-ayn), meaning that sulh is not valid for that type of claim.

Key Terms
الجناية (al-jinaayah): Criminal offence, including homicide and bodily injury, whether intentional or accidental.
العفو (al-'afw): Remission or pardon, specifically the guardian's acceptance of diyah instead of qisas.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money paid to the victim's heirs in lieu of qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Qur'anic evidence for the validity of sulh in intentional homicide?
A) Surah al-Ma'idah 5:32
B) Surah al-Baqarah 2:178
C) Surah al-Nur 24:2
D) Surah al-An'am 6:151

Key Takeaway
Sulh for criminal offences, including intentional homicide, is valid in the Hanafi school, supported by Qur'an 2:178 and its interpretation by early commentators.

Valid Compromise for a Slave's Life by His Owner
Ruling with proof: owner's compromise for slave's life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the owner's compromise for his slave's intentional murder is valid, explain the proof (dalil) that the slave is the owner's earnings, and distinguish this from the authorized slave and mukatib cases.

Core Idea
The owner's compromise for his slave's life is valid because the slave is his property and earnings.

This lesson presents a foundational ruling from Kitab al-Sulh: the owner of a slave who intentionally kills someone may validly compromise for the slave's life. The proof is that the slave is from the owner's earnings (kash), giving the owner full authority. The text then contrasts this with the authorized slave (ma'dhun), who cannot compromise for himself with compensation, and the mukatib, who can. Understanding these distinctions clarifies the limits of a slave's legal capacity and the owner's property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
What is the specific act being ruled upon? The text states: the owner's compromise for the life of his slave who committed intentional murder is valid (ṣaḥḥa ṣulḥuhu ʿan nafsi ʿabdi lahū).
The ruling applies to the owner's act, not the slave's.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof given is: 'because his slave is from his earnings' (li-anna ʿabdahu min kashiyahi). This means the slave is property acquired through the owner's efforts, so the owner has full disposal rights over him.
The notion of 'kash' (earnings) signifies ownership and control.
3
Compare with the authorized slave (al-ma’dhūn)
The text explains that an authorized slave (permitted to trade) cannot validly compromise for himself with compensation (bi-ʿiwaḍ) because that would dispose of the master's property without permission. Any obligation from such a compromise is postponed until after emancipation.
The key distinction: the authorized slave is a legal person for his own acts but not for disposing of his master's property.
4
Contrast with the mukatib
The mukatib (slave under contract to buy freedom) can compromise for himself because he is like a free person in terms of his person – he is no longer fully under the master's control.
The mukatib's unique status allows him to bind himself for compensation related to his person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The book of Compromise: its definition and divisions. And valid is his compromise for the life of his slave who intentionally killed a man, and the compromise. And valid is his compromise for the life of his slave who intentionally killed a man) because his slave is from his earnings, so his disposal regarding him is valid and his release. (And the compromise) is from the category of trade, and his disposal concerning himself is not from its category, so it does not take effect against the master's right if it is with compensation; because it is a disposal in the property of another without his permission, and he is the master. Do you not see that it is not permissible for the authorized slave to sell himself, and it is valid between him and his master, so much so that it is not permissible to kill him after pardon; because he is legally responsible, so his disposal concerning his own person is valid, and the substitute for the property is not obligatory on him; because he and what is in his hand are the property of the master, and it is postponed until after emancipation, unlike the mukatib, where it is permissible for him to compromise for himself because he is like a free person due to his removal from the master's control. Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Kitab al-Sulh. It opens by stating the ruling: the owner of a slave who committed intentional murder can validly compromise for the slave's life. The proof is that the slave is the owner's property (kash), so the owner has full authority over him. It then compares this to the authorized slave (ma'dhun) who cannot compromise for himself with compensation because that would be disposing of the master's property without permission. The authorized slave's compromise for himself is valid only without compensation (e.g., pardon) and any obligation is deferred until after emancipation. This contrasts with the mukatib (slave under contract to buy his freedom) who can compromise for himself because he is effectively free regarding himself. The commentary then addresses the word 'al-Sulh' from the matn, explaining that it is a commercial transaction. A second note discusses an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: if a usurper (ghasib) compromises for a usurped item that perished, paying more than its value, Imam Abu Hanifa allows it, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) invalidate the excess if it is a significant difference (la yataghaban). However, if the compromise is with a commodity (`ard) rather than cash, all agree it is valid regardless of value. This shows the hanafi position on ghubn fahish.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-ṣulḥ): A contract of compromise to settle a dispute, here used for indemnifying criminal liability.
العبد المأذون (al-ʿabd al-ma’dhūn): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
المكاتب (al-mukātib): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
القيمة (al-qīmah): Monetary value or compensation owed for a lost or destroyed item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the slave is legally responsible for his actions.
B) Because the slave is the owner's earnings (min kashiyahi).
C) Because the victim's family must accept the compromise.
D) Because the slave consents to the compromise.

Key Takeaway
The owner's compromise for his slave's life is valid because the slave is his property (earnings), but this authority is limited by the slave's legal capacity (e.g., authorized slave's compromise deferred).

Compromise by a Usurper for a Usurped Item with Excess: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on usurper's compromise with excess payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of a usurper's compromise for a lost item with excess payment, identify the thamarah al-khilaf, and apply the rule to cash versus commodity cases.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows a usurper's compromise for a lost item at any price; the Sahibayn invalidate excessive cash payments as usury.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute from a commentary on Kitab al-Sulh. The issue: a usurper (ghasib) who caused a loss of an item offers to settle by paying more than its market value. Imam Abu Hanifa holds the entire compromise valid, while his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) deem the excess void if it is a significant discrepancy (ghubn fahish). However, if the settlement is made with a commodity rather than cash, all agree it is valid. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurists balance contractual freedom (based on trade) with the prohibition of riba.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the disputed issue
The scenario: a usurper (ghāṣib) compromises for a usurped item (maghṣūb) that has perished, paying more than its value. The dispute: is the entire amount valid or is the excess void?
The item was edible or perishable? Not specified, but it survived as a claim.
2
Record Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa says: it is permissible (jāza) – the entire compromise is valid, including the excess.
His reasoning: compromise is a trade contract (bāb al-tijāra), so parties can agree on any price.
3
Record the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say: the excess over the value is void if it is something in which people do not overlook (lā yataghāban al-nās fīhi). This means the excess is invalid if it exceeds the normal range of tolerable discrepancy in business.
They view the excess as riba because the owner's right is only to the value.
4
Identify the exception: compromise with a commodity
If the compromise is with a commodity (ʿarḍ) instead of cash, it is valid by agreement (muttāfaq ʿalayhi), regardless of whether the commodity's value is more or less than the lost item's value.
The reason: commodity exchange is not considered riba; it is straight trade.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: in cash, according to Sahibayn, the excess must be returned if ghubn fahish exists; according to Abu Hanifa, no return. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position for cash, but caution is advised. For commodities, there is no practical difference.
The text states 'wa hādha muttafaqun ʿalayhi' for the commodity case.

Source Text

Compromise for Usurped Item: Excess Over Value – The Hanafi Dispute
Extended commentary on compromise for perished usurped item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on paying more than the value in a compromise for a perished usurped item and apply the mufta bihi to real scenarios.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits the entire excess; the Sahibayn declare it ribā unless negligible – the relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.

This passage examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: if a usurped item perishes and the usurper pays more than its value in a compromise, is the excess valid? Imam Abu Hanifa holds it is valid, while his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider the excess ribā if it is a significant discrepancy. The text presents the arguments, conditions, and consensus points, and declares Abu Hanifa’s view as the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognise that the act is a compromise (ṣulḥ) after the usurped item has perished, where the usurper pays cash exceeding the item's value. The dispute is whether the extra amount is valid or void.
2
State the positions of the imams
Abu Hanifa: the whole payment is valid. The Sahibayn: excess beyond a negligible amount (ghubn fāḥish) is void and must be returned.
3
Trace the Sahibayn's dalīl
The owner's right is only the value (qīma) because the item is qīmī (not fungible). Any extra is ribā, as value is a known obligation.
The text states: 'li-anna ḥaqqahū fī al-qīmah, fa al-zā'idu ribā'.
4
Note the conditions and consensus points
The compromise must be before a judge's ruling (after ruling, unanimous invalidity). The item must be qīmī of known value. Compromise with a commodity (ʿaraḍ) is always valid by consensus. If the item still exists, compromise for more is valid by consensus.
Also, a negligible excess (mā yataghābanu al-nās) is overlooked even by the Sahibayn.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The correct position is Abu Hanifa's, so in practice the full amount is valid unless the excess is extremely large and the judge rules otherwise.
tarjih from al-Fatāwā al-Hindiyya: 'wal-ṣaḥīḥu madhhabu Abī Ḥanīfa'.
