[1]قوله : عن مغصوب ؛ قيد بكون المصالح عنه مغصوباً ؛ لأنه هو المحتاج إلى الصلح غالباً، والمراد قيمي معلوم القيمة، أما كونه قيمياً ؛ فلأن الصلح عن كُر حنطة مثلاً على دراهم أو دنانير جائز بالإجماع سواء كانتا أكثر من قيمته أم لا ، لكن القبض شرط، وإن كانتا بأعيانهما ؛ لئلا يلزم الكالي بالكالئ. وأما كونه معلـوم القيمة ؛ ليظهر الغبنُ الفاحش المانع من لزومِ الزيادة عندهما ، وقيد بالتلف ؛ لأنَّ المغصوب إذا كان قائماً جازَ الصلحُ على أكثر من قيمته بالإجماع. صرح به في «العناية»، وغيرها. وقيد بأكثر من قيمته ليحترز به عن الصلح بمثل قيمته، وبما نقص، فإنه يجوز فيهما اتفاقاً ، صرح به قاضي خان في «شرح الجامع الصغير»، واعلم أنه لا بد من تقييد بما قبل القضاء، فإنّه لو قضى القاضي عليه بالقيمة ثم صالحه على أكثر من قيمته لا يجوز اتفاقاً. كما ذكرناه نقلاً عن «الفتاوى الهندية)) (۲) [٢] قوله : لأنّ حقه في القيمة...الخ ; تقريره : إنّ ما وقع عليه الصلح كان بمقابلة ما وجب في الذمة، وهي القيمة ، فإنَّ العين ليست من ذوات الأمثال، فلا يمكن وجوبها في الذمة، بخلاف القيمة فإنّ وجوبها يمكن في الذمة، فتكون هي واجبة، وهي مقدّرة، والزيادة عليها تكون رباً. (۱) «العناية» (۷: ۳۹۰). (۲) (الفتاوى الهندية ٤ : ٢٤٢ - ٢٤٣).
It is stated in al-Fatāwā al-Hindiyya (1): He usurped a garment worth one hundred and destroyed it, then he compromised with him for more than one hundred; it is permissible, and they (the Sahibayn) said: The excess over its value is void if it is something in which people do not overlook [discrepancies]. And the correct (position) is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa. Thus in Khizānat al-Fatāwā. When the usurped item is a slave that escaped or perished in his hand, and he compromised for more than its value, it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: The excess over its value is void if it is something in which people do not overlook. And among our companions some said: The disagreement is when the slave escaped; but if it is consumed, then compromising for more than its value is not permissible according to them. And the more correct (view) is that the disagreement applies to both cases. Thus mentioned Fakhr al-Dīn Qāḍī Khān in Sharḥ al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr. (1) (al-Fatāwā al-Hindiyya 4:242-243). 310 Zubdat al-Nihāya li-ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya: 'an maghsūbin talafa bi-akthara min qīmatihī, aw ‘arad) — this is according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (the Sahibayn): it is not valid for more than its value unless it is an increase in which people overlook [discrepancies], because his right is in the value, so the excess is ribā. And on this disagreement, if he usurped a slave that perished in his hand and then compromised with an amount of money, then the usurper presented evidence that its value is less than what he compromised for, his evidence is not accepted according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: His evidence is accepted and the excess over the value is returned to the usurper. Thus in Ghāyat al-Bayān Sharḥ al-Hidāya. They all agreed that if he compromised for a commodity, it is valid, whether it is of great value or little value. And they all agreed that if the judge had given a ruling for the value and then he compromised for more than the value, it is not valid. Thus in al-Khulāṣa. End, so preserve it. [1] His saying: 'an maghsūb (from a usurped item) — restricted to the thing compromised being usurped, because it is what is usually in need of compromise. And what is meant is a qīmī (value-based good) of known value. As for being a qīmī, because compromise for a quantity of wheat, for example, for dirhams or dīnārs is permissible by consensus, whether they are more than its value or not, but possession is a condition, even if they are in kind, to avoid committing debt for debt (kālī bil-kālī). As for being of known value, so that the gross discrepancy (ghubn fāḥish) that prevents the validity of the excess according to them is apparent. And it is restricted to perishing, because if the usurped item is still in existence, compromise for more than its value is permissible by consensus. This is explicitly stated in al-ʿInāya and others. And it is restricted to being more than its value to avoid the case of compromising for its equivalent or less, because in both cases it is permissible by agreement, as stated by Qāḍī Khān in Sharḥ al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr. And know that it must be restricted to before judgment, because if the judge had given a ruling for the value and then he compromised for more, it is not valid by agreement. As we mentioned from al-Fatāwā al-Hindiyya. [2] His saying: 'Because his right is in the value...' Explanation: What the compromise falls upon is in exchange for what is obligatory in the dhimmah, which is the value. Since the specific item is not fungible (laysa min dhawāt al-amthāl), its obligation in the dhimmah is not possible, unlike the value whose obligation in the dhimmah is possible, so it is obligatory and it is estimated. The increase over it is ribā.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the ruling for compromise when a usurped item (maghsūb) has perished and the usurper pays more than its value. It begins by quoting al-Fatāwā al-Hindiyya: a garment worth 100 is destroyed; compromise for more than 100 is valid per Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn invalidate the excess if it is a significant discrepancy (mā lā yataghābanu fīh). The text states that the correct (sahīh) position is Abu Hanifa's. It then discusses the case of a slave that escaped or perished: the same ikhtilaf applies. Some Hanafi scholars restrict the ikhtilaf to the case of escape, but the author declares the more correct view that it applies in both cases. The core argument of the Sahibayn is provided: the rightful claim of the owner is only the value; any excess is ribā. This is explained further: when the item is qīmī (not fungible), it cannot be owed in the dhimmah, so the value becomes the obligation. That value is fixed; any extra is usury. The commentary then explains restrictions: the item must be qīmī (not mithlī), of known value (to make ghubn fāḥish apparent), and the compromise must have occurred before a judge's ruling on the value. After a judicial ruling, compromise for more is unanimously invalid. The passage also notes that compromise with a commodity (ˁaraḍ) rather than cash is valid by consensus, as it avoids the tainted excess. The overall teaching is that the Hanafi school (via Abu Hanifa) is more lenient in allowing such compromises, but with conditions to avoid ribā.

Key Terms
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fāḥish): Gross discrepancy or excessive difference in value that people would not normally overlook in transactions.
القيمي (al-qīmī): A good that is not fungible (mithlī), i.e., unique items like slaves, real estate, etc., whose value must be assessed individually.
الكالي بالكالي (al-kālī bil-kālī): A prohibited transaction of debt for debt, where both countervalues are deferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the passage, what is the ruling if someone usurps a slave worth 200, it perishes, and then he compromises for 250 in cash?
A) The compromise is void because the extra 50 is ribā.
B) The compromise is valid for the full 250.
C) The compromise is valid only for 200, and the 50 must be returned.
D) It depends on whether the slave escaped or perished.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, paying more than the value in a compromise for a perished usurped item is valid if it is before a judge's ruling, following Abu Hanifa's position, as declared in al-Fatāwā al-Hindiyya.

Liability for Substitute in Settlement (Sulh): Agent vs. Stranger
Rulings on liability for badal in sulh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain who bears the substitute (badal) in a sulh when it is performed by an agent or a stranger, and identify the conditions that make a stranger's settlement valid.

Core Idea
The substitute in a settlement follows its nature: pure relinquishment (isqat) places liability on the principal; exchange (mu'awadah) places it on the agent.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules on who bears the substitute (badal) in a settlement (sulh) when conducted by an agent or a stranger. The key distinction is whether the settlement is based on denial (inkar) or acknowledgment (iqrar). In cases of denial, the sulh is pure relinquishment, making the agent a mere messenger. In acknowledgment-based exchanges, the agent becomes an original party. The lesson also covers the conditions under which a stranger's (fuduli) settlement is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two base scenarios
The passage states two cases: sulh for intentional blood-money and sulh for part of a claimed debt. Both involve denial, so the sulh is pure relinquishment (isqat mahd).
These are examples; the ruling applies to similar cases.
2
Determine the agent's liability
In pure relinquishment, the agent is a mere messenger (safir). The substitute is on the principal, not the agent, unless the agent voluntarily guarantees it.
Analogy: agent in marriage also bears no financial liability.
3
Contrast with settlement based on acknowledgment
If the sulh is based on iqrar and involves an exchange of different types of property, it is like a sale (bay'). The agent becomes an original party (asil) and bears the substitute, with recourse to the principal.
If based on denial, the agent is never liable.
4
Analyze the stranger (fuduli) scenario
A stranger's settlement is valid only if the stranger guarantees the substitute, adds it to his own property, specifies a particular payment without reference to himself, or makes an absolute promise and actually pays.
If none of these conditions are met, the settlement is invalid.
5
Apply to other contracts
The same principle applies wherever the agent is a mere messenger, e.g., marriage, khul', or settlement for injury less than homicide.
The example of blood-money is not restrictive.

Source Text

باب التبرع بالصلح والتوكيل به
وبدل صلح عن دم عمد، أو على بعض دين يدعيه يلزم الموكل لا وكيله؛ لأنَّ الصَّلحَ في هاتين الصورتين ليس بمنزلة البيع. قوله : وبدل صلح ...الخ ؛ صورة المسألة: إن زيداً مثلاً صالح بكراً بالصلح عن دم عمداً أو على بعض دين يدعيه من المكيلات والموزونات لزم بدل ذلك الصلح على الموكل دون الوكيل ؛ لأنه إسقاط محض، فكان الوكيل سفيراً محضاً، فلا ضمان عليه، كالوكيل بالنكاح إلا أن يضمن الوكيل بدل الصلح ؛ فإنّه حينئذ يكون مؤاخذا بالضمان لا بالصلح.
كما لو وقع الصلحُ من الوكيل عن مال بمال عن إقرار، فإنّ الوكيل يلزمُهُ ما صالح عليه، ثم يرجع به على الموكل ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل أصيل في المعاوضة المالية، فترجع الحقوق إليه دون الموكل ، فيطالب هو بالعوض دون الموكل، أما إذا كان عن إنكار لا يجب البدل على الوكيل مطلقاً. كما في «البحر»، وغيره.
قوله : عن دم عمد؛ هذا بطريق التمثيل لا بطريق التخصيص بجريانه في غير ذلك: كالصلح عن جناية العمد فيما دون النفس، والصلح عن كل عقد يكون الوكيل فيه سفيراً محضاً، كالنكاح والخلع وغيرهما. كما صرحوا به.
قوله : يدعيه؛ الأولى أن يقال: يدعيه عليه آخر؛ لما علمت أنَّ التوكيل من طرف المدعى عليه.
قوله : يلزم الموكل؛ قال شيخنا العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر المختار: هذا ظاهر فيما إذا كان الوكيلُ من طرف الجاني، ولا يظهر إذا كان من طرف الولي؛ لأنه آخذ فكيف يقال: يلزمه، وكذا لا يظهر في جانب الدين إذا كان الموكل هو المدعي؛ لأنَّ الموكل يدع فكيف يلزمه. انتهى.
قوله : في هاتين الصورتين؛ أي في الصلح عن دم عمد، والصلح على بعض دين يدعيه.
إلا أن يضمنه، وفيما هو كبيع لزم وكيله، وإن صالح فضولي، وضَمِنَ البدل، أو أضاف إلى ماله، أو أشار إلى نقد أو عَرَضِ بلا نسبة إلى نفسه، أو أطلق ونقد صح.
أما في الأول فظاهر، وأما في الثاني؛ فلانه أخذ البعض، وحَطّ الباقي، فيرجع الحقوق إلى الموكل. 
قوله: إلا أن يضمنه: أي الوكيل، فحينئذ يكون البدل عليه لأجل الكفالة.
قوله: وفيما هو كبيع لزم وكيله: أي فيما يكون الصلح عن مال على مال من غير جنس المصالح عنه، ويكون مع الإقرار.
قوله: وإن صالح فضولي، وضَمِنَ البدل، أو أضاف إلى ماله، أو أشار إلى نقد أو عَرَض بلا نسبة إلى نفسه، أو أطلق ونقد صح.
قوله: أما في الأوّل فظاهر؛ يعني أمّا في الصلح عن دم عمد فظاهر أنه ليس بمنزلة البيع؛ لأنه ليس هنا مبادلة مال بمال، بل إسقاط محض.
قوله: فيرجع الحقوق إلى الموكل؛ فإنّه إسقاط محض، فكان الوكيل فيه سفيراً محضاً، فلا ضمان عليه كالوكيل بالنكاح.
قوله: ويكون مع الإقرار؛ وأما إذا كان الصلح مع الإنكار فلا يجب بدل الصلح على الوكيل في شيء.
قوله: وإن صالح؛ هذه المسألة على أربعة أوجه؛ لأنه لا يخلو: إما أن يكون الفضولي ضامناً لبدل الصلح أو لا، فإن لم يكن ضامناً له فلا يخلو: إما أن يضيف الذي وقع عليه الصلح إلى نفسه أو لا. وإن لم يضفه إلى نفسه فلا يخلو إما أن يسلّم العوض أو لا، فالصلح جائز في الوجوه كلها إلا في الوجه الأخير، وهو ما إذا لم يضمن البدل ولم يضفه إلى نفسه، ولم يسلّمه إلى المدعي، وهذا التربيع اختاره في كثير من المتون، والمصنف له خمسه بأن جعل غير المضاف إلى نفسه على شقين: الأول: ما إذا أطلق ونقد.
Chapter of voluntary settlement and appointing an agent for it. And the substitute in a settlement for intentional blood-money, or for part of a debt claimed, binds the principal, not his agent, because the settlement in these two forms is not like a sale. He said: 'And the substitute in a settlement... etc.' The picture of the issue: If Zayd, for example, settles with Bakr in a settlement for intentional blood-money or for part of a debt claimed from measured or weighed items, then the substitute of that settlement is binding on the principal, not the agent, because it is pure relinquishment, so the agent is a mere messenger, thus there is no liability on him, like an agent in marriage, unless the agent guarantees the substitute of settlement; then he is held liable for the guarantee, not the settlement. As if the settlement occurred from the agent for property in exchange for property based on acknowledgment, then the agent is bound by what he settled upon, then he recovers it from the principal, because the agent is an original party in financial exchange, so rights revert to him, not the principal; thus he is demanded for the compensation, not the principal. But if it is based on denial, the substitute is not binding on the agent at all, as in 'al-Bahr' and others. He said: 'for intentional blood-money' – this is by way of example, not specification, it applies in other cases: like settlement for intentional injury less than homicide, and settlement for every contract in which the agent is a mere messenger, like marriage, khul', and others, as they explicitly stated. He said: 'claims' – it is better to say: 'another claims from him', because you know that the agency is from the side of the defendant. He said: 'binds the principal' – our teacher, the eminent al-Tahtawi, said in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar: This is apparent when the agent is from the side of the offender; it is not apparent when he is from the side of the guardian, because he is a taker, so how can it be said that it binds him? Likewise, it is not apparent regarding debt when the principal is the claimant, because the principal claims, so how does it bind him? End. He said: 'in these two forms' – i.e., in settlement for intentional blood-money and settlement for part of a debt claimed. Except if he guarantees it, and in what is like a sale, it binds his agent; and if a stranger settles, guarantees the substitute, or adds it to his property, or points to cash or goods without relation to himself, or is absolute and pays, it is valid. As for the first, it is apparent; as for the second, because he took part and dropped the rest, so rights revert to the principal. He said: 'except if he guarantees it' – i.e., the agent, then the substitute is on him because of suretyship. He said: 'and in what is like a sale, it binds his agent' – i.e., in cases where the settlement is for property in exchange for property of a different kind from the settled item, and it is with acknowledgment. He said: 'and if a stranger settles, guarantees the substitute, or adds it to his property, or points to cash or goods without relation to himself, or is absolute and pays, it is valid.' He said: 'as for the first, it is apparent' – meaning in the settlement for intentional blood-money, it is apparent that it is not like a sale because there is no exchange of property; rather, it is pure relinquishment. He said: 'so rights revert to the principal' – because it is pure relinquishment, so the agent is a mere messenger, thus there is no liability on him, like an agent in marriage. He said: 'and it is with acknowledgment' – but if the settlement is with denial, the substitute of settlement is not binding on the agent at all. He said: 'and if a stranger settles' – this issue has four aspects: because it is either that the stranger is guarantor for the substitute or not; if he is not a guarantor, then it is either that he adds the settled item to himself or not; if he does not add it to himself, it is either that he delivers the compensation or not. The settlement is valid in all these ways except the last, which is when he does not guarantee the substitute, does not add it to himself, and does not deliver it to the claimant. This quadripartition is chosen in many texts, and the author has made it a fivefold division by making that which is not added to oneself into two parts: the first is when he is absolute and pays.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the matn of Al-Wiqayah addresses the liability for the substitute (badal) in a settlement (sulh) when the settlement is conducted by an agent or by a stranger (fuduli). The author begins by stating two scenarios: (1) sulh for intentional blood-money (usually a case of denial, where the offender settles to avoid qisas), and (2) sulh for part of a claimed debt (again, a case where the debtor denies part or all). In both, the substitute is binding on the principal (muwakkil), not the agent, because the settlement here is pure relinquishment (isqat mahd) — the right is dropped without any exchange of property. Thus, the agent is merely a messenger (safir) and incurs no liability. This is likened to an agent in marriage (wakil bil-nikah), who also bears no financial liability. However, if the agent voluntarily guarantees (dhamana) the substitute, he will be liable as a guarantor, not because of the settlement itself. The passage then contrasts this with a case where the settlement is based on acknowledgment (iqrar) and involves an exchange of property from one type to another (e.g., paying goods instead of cash). This type of sulh is like a sale (kabay'), so the agent becomes an original party (asil) in the financial transaction, and the substitute is binding on him, after which he can recover from his principal. If the settlement is based on denial (inkar), the agent is never liable. The commentary also clarifies that the examples given are not restrictive; the same rulings apply to similar cases like settlement for intentional injury less than homicide or any contract where the agent is a mere messenger. A secondary point from al-Tahtawi notes that the ruling that the substitute binds the principal is clear when the agent represents the offender, but less clear when the agent represents the guardian (wali) or when the principal is the claimant. Finally, the passage addresses settlement by a stranger (fuduli) — an unauthorized agent who acts without prior appointment. The validity of such a settlement depends on four or five conditions: if the stranger guarantees the substitute, adds the settled item to his own property, specifies a particular cash or commodity (without referencing himself), or makes an absolute promise and actually pays, then the settlement is valid. The only invalid case is when the stranger does not guarantee, does not add to his property, and does not deliver the compensation to the claimant. This refinement is part of the Hanafi legal methodology.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A contractual settlement compromising a claim by agreement, often involving forfeiture or exchange.
بدل (badal): The substitute or consideration given in a settlement, such as money, goods, or the remission of a claim.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or debt, which makes sulh akin to a sale because the defendant admits the claim and then settles.
إنكار (inkar): Denial of a claim, which makes sulh a pure relinquishment (isqat mahd) because the defendant is settling to avoid litigation despite maintaining his denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following cases does the agent bear the substitute (badal) in a sulh contract?
A) Sulh for intentional blood-money when the agent does not guarantee
B) Sulh for part of a claimed debt when the agent does not guarantee
C) Sulh based on acknowledgment for property of a different kind
D) Sulh by a stranger who does not guarantee, add to his property, or pay

Key Takeaway
Liability for the substitute in sulh hinges on whether the settlement is pure relinquishment (principal bears) or an exchange (agent bears); a stranger's settlement is valid only if he personally guarantees, adds, specifies, or pays.

Settlement by an Unauthorized Person: Five Scenarios of Validity
Expanding the rulings on sulh by agent and fuduli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five scenarios in which a settlement by an unauthorized person (fuduli) is valid in Hanafi law and explain the legal reasoning (istihsan) behind each.

Core Idea
A settlement by an unauthorized person is valid if the plaintiff receives the substitute, because the plaintiff's satisfaction is the key.

In Hanafi fiqh, the principle of istihsan allows flexibility in transactions to fulfill the parties' objectives. This passage from the Sharh al-Wiqayah expands the rulings on settlement (sulh) when performed by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The commentary presents five distinct scenarios, detailing when such a settlement is immediately valid and when it remains suspended pending ratification. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the concept of fuduli in dispute resolution.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish agent vs. unauthorized person
First, note the two separate topics: the liability of an agent (wakil) in sulh (pure release vs. sale-like) and the validity of a fuduli's settlement. The latter is the main focus. Identify that a fuduli is a third party without prior authorization.
The commentary on the agent is a prelude; the detailed analysis is on the fuduli.
2
Enumerate the five scenarios for fuduli
List the five cases mentioned: (1) the fuduli guarantees the substitute, (2) adds it to his own property, (3) points to specific coin or goods without attribution to himself, (4) releases and pays, and (5) releases and does not pay (the suspended case).
The first four are valid immediately; the fifth requires ratification.
3
Analyze the rationale for validity
For each valid scenario, understand why the settlement is effective. The common thread: the plaintiff receives or is assured of receiving the substitute, thus his right is satisfied. The defendant's consent is not required because he only benefits from release.
The commentary uses qiyas: the fuduli is like the defendant in matters of release.
4
Contrast the invalid (suspended) scenario
Identify the one scenario where settlement is not immediately valid: fuduli does not guarantee, add, specify, or pay. In that case, the plaintiff has no assurance, so ratification by the defendant is needed.
This suspension protects the defendant from unintended obligations.
5
Note intra-Hanafi variation
Recognize that earlier texts divided the cases into four; the commentator (sahib al-Muhit) expanded to five by splitting 'release and pay' from 'release without pay'. The five-case division is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
This refinement does not change the substantive ruling but adds precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بطل الصلح. [1] قوله : وإن لم ينقد ؛ أي وإن لم يُسَلّم المصالح عليه إلى المدعي ولم يضمنه ولم يضفه إلى نفسه، فالصلح موقوف إن أجازه المدعى عليه جاز، ولزمه المصالح عليه، وإن لم يجزه بطل. [٢]قوله : صح الصلح ؛ أما إذا ضمن بدل الصلح ؛ فلأنَّ الحاصل للمدعى عليه ليس إلا البراءة، وفي مثل ذلك يستوي المدعى عليه والأجنبي ؛ لأنه لا يُسَلِّم إلى المدعى عليه شيء، كما لا يُسَلِّمُ إلى الأجنبي، ومع ذلك جاز اشتراط بدل الصلح على نفسه،
فكذا للأجنبي. والمقصود من هذا الصلح رضاءُ صاحب الحق لا رضا المدعى عليه، إذ لا حظ له فيه والمدعي ينفرد بالصلح فيما لا معاوضةً فيه، غير أنه لم يرض بسقوط حقه مجاناً ، فإذا سُلِمَ له العوضُ من جهة المتبرع صح ، بخلاف ما إذا كان الصلح بأمر المدعى عليه، فإنّه لا يصيرُ مُتبرعا بل يرجع.
و اور و
وأما إذا أضافه إلى ماله ؛ فلأنّ الإضافة إلى نفسه إلتزام منه للتسليم إلى المدعي ، وهو قادر على ذلك، فيجب عليه تسليمه، فيصح لتمام رضاه به ، وأما إذا أشار إلى نقد أو عرض بلا نسيئة إلى نفسه ؛ فلأنَّ المعرَّف المشار إليه كالمضاف إلى نفسه ؛ لأنه تعين للتسليم إليه بشرطه، فيتم به الصلح. وأما إذا أطلق ونقد فلأنَّ التسليم إلى المدعي يوجب سلامة العوض له، فيتم عقد
Except that he guarantees it, and in what is like a sale, his agent is bound. And if an unauthorized person makes a settlement, and guarantees the substitute, or adds it to his own property, or points to coin or goods without attribution to himself, or he releases and pays, it is valid. [Commentary:] As for the first, it is apparent. As for the second, because he took some and remitted the rest, so the rights return to the principal. Except that he guarantees it: meaning the agent, then the substitute is upon him because of suretyship. And in what is like a sale, his agent is bound: meaning in the case where the settlement is for property in exchange for property of a different type, and it is with acknowledgment. And if an unauthorized person makes a settlement, and guarantees the substitute, or adds it to his own property, or points to coin or goods without attribution to himself, or he releases and pays, it is valid. [1] He said: As for the first, it is apparent: meaning as for the settlement for premeditated blood, it is apparent that it is not like a sale because there is no exchange of property, rather it is pure release. (2) He said: So the rights return to the principal: for it is pure release, so the agent is a mere messenger, so there is no guarantee upon him like the agent for marriage. [3] He said: And it is with acknowledgment: but if the settlement is with denial, then the substitute of the settlement is not obligatory upon the agent at all. [4] He said: And if an unauthorized person makes a settlement: this issue has four aspects; because either the unauthorized person guarantees the substitute of the settlement or not. If he does not guarantee it, then either he adds what the settlement is made upon to himself or not. If he does not add it to himself, then either he delivers the substitute or not. So the settlement is valid in all aspects except the last: which is when he does not guarantee the substitute, does not add it to himself, and does not deliver it to the plaintiff. This quadripartite division is chosen in many texts, and the author made it five by making the not added to himself into two parts: first: when he releases and pays. (1) Because this settlement is pure release, so the agent is a mere messenger and interpreter, so the substitute is not upon him like the agent for marriage. See: Fath Bab al-Inayah (3:190). And if he does not pay, if the defendant accepts it, the substitute is binding upon him, otherwise it is rejected. [Page 315] And if he does not pay: if the defendant accepts it, the substitute is binding upon him, otherwise it is rejected. That is, the unauthorized person made a settlement on behalf of the defendant with the plaintiff, and he guaranteed the substitute of the settlement, or said: I settle with you for one thousand dirhams from my property, or this one thousand, or this slave of mine, or said: I settle with you for this one thousand, or this slave without attributing them to himself, or he released it and said: I settle with you for one thousand dirhams and paid it; in these forms the settlement is valid. [2] The second: when he releases and does not pay: the settlement is effective in the four forms, and suspended in the fifth form; if the defendant accepts it, the settlement is valid and what is settled upon is binding upon him, otherwise the settlement is void. [1] He said: And if he does not pay: meaning if he does not deliver what is settled upon to the plaintiff, and does not guarantee it, and does not add it to himself, then the settlement is suspended; if the defendant accepts it, it is valid and what is settled upon is binding upon him; if he does not accept it, it is void. [2] He said: The settlement is valid: as for if he guarantees the substitute of the settlement, because what is obtained for the defendant is only release, and in such a case the defendant and a stranger are equal, because nothing is delivered to the defendant, just as nothing is delivered to the stranger, and despite that it is permissible to stipulate the substitute of the settlement upon himself, so likewise for the stranger. The purpose of this settlement is the satisfaction of the right-holder, not the satisfaction of the defendant, for he has no benefit in it, and the plaintiff alone has authority over the settlement in what is not a compensation, but he is not satisfied with dropping his right for free; so if the consideration is delivered to him from the volunteer, it is valid, contrary to if the settlement was by the command of the defendant, then he does not become a volunteer but rather returns. And as for if he adds it to his own property, because the addition to himself is a commitment from him to deliver to the plaintiff, and he is capable of that, so delivery is obligatory upon him, so it is valid due to his complete satisfaction. And as for if he points to coin or goods without delay to himself, because that which is pointed to is like added to himself, because it is specified for delivery to him by condition, so the settlement is completed by that. And as for if he releases and pays, because delivery to the plaintiff causes the consideration to be safe for him, so the contract is completed.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Wiqayah expands on the rulings of settlement (sulh) when performed by an agent (wakil) or by an unauthorized third party (fuduli). The commentary opens by distinguishing two types of settlement: (1) pure release (isqat mahd), such as settlement for blood money (sulh 'an dam 'amd), where the agent is merely a messenger and bears no liability if he acts without guarantee; (2) settlement through property exchange (like a sale), where the agent is bound if he accepts a substitute. If the settlement involves acknowledgment (iqrar) by the defendant, the agent is not liable for the substitute unless he guarantees it expressly. The second major topic is settlement by an unauthorized person (fuduli). The author initially presents four scenarios, then the commentator (sahib al-Muhit) expands to five. The key is that the settlement is valid in all cases except where the fuduli does NOT guarantee the substitute, does NOT add it to his own property, and does NOT deliver it to the plaintiff. If he guarantees, adds, points to specific goods, or pays, the settlement is valid immediately; otherwise it is suspended pending ratification by the defendant. The underlying rationale is that the plaintiff's right is privity: he agrees to drop his claim only if he receives something in return, whether from the defendant or from a third party. Thus if a stranger (ajnabi) volunteers the payment, the plaintiff is satisfied, and the settlement is valid. The text also explains that pointing to specific coin or goods is equivalent to adding to one's own property, because the item is earmarked for delivery. Paying immediately also perfects the contract. This analysis is consistent with Hanafi principles of istihsan (juristic preference) based on the concept of meeting the parties' intentions and avoiding hardship.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized person who acts on behalf of another without prior permission.
صلح (sulh): A settlement or compromise agreement that resolves a dispute, often involving an exchange or relinquishment of rights.
بدل (badal): The substitute or consideration given in a settlement, such as money or goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a scenario where a settlement by an unauthorized person (fuduli) is valid immediately?
A) The fuduli does not guarantee the substitute and does not deliver it.
B) The fuduli guarantees the substitute but does not add it to his property nor deliver it.
C) The fuduli adds the substitute to his own property or points to specific goods.
D) The fuduli releases and does not pay, and the defendant later ratifies.

Key Takeaway
A settlement by an unauthorized person is valid in Hanafi law if the plaintiff receives the substitute through the fuduli's guarantee, addition, pointing, or payment; otherwise it is suspended pending ratification.

Stranger's Compromise: Binding vs Contingent
Commentary on the ruling of a stranger's compromise without delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a stranger's compromise (sulh fuduli) is immediately binding and distinguish it from a contingent compromise.

Core Idea
A stranger's compromise is binding if he guarantees or delivers the substitute; otherwise, it is contingent.

When a stranger (fuduli) intervenes to settle a dispute by offering a compromise to the plaintiff on behalf of the defendant, the validity depends on whether the stranger has taken personal responsibility for the substitute. The main commentary in the source text explains the key scenarios and the analogical reasoning (qiyas) that supports the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides the scenarios into two groups: (1) where the stranger guarantees, delivers, attributes the substitute to himself, or points to a specific item; and (2) where he makes a loose offer without any such action.
The first group yields immediate validity; the second group is contingent.
2
List the five binding scenarios
The binding scenarios are: (a) guaranteeing the substitute, (b) attributing the substitute to his own wealth, (c) pointing to cash or goods and attributing to himself, (d) loosely stating 'I compromise for a thousand dirhams' and then delivering, and (e) loosely stating 'I compromise on this slave' without attribution but delivering.
Each involves the stranger undertaking personal responsibility.
3
Understand the reasoning for guarantee
The commentary uses qiyas: the stranger who guarantees is analogous to the defendant because both result in acquittal (bara'ah) for the defendant. The plaintiff's satisfaction is the purpose, and the stranger's guarantee achieves it.
The effective cause is the achievement of acquittal.
4
Identify the contingent scenario
If the stranger merely says 'I make a compromise' without guarantee, delivery, or attribution, the contract is suspended (mawquf). It requires the defendant's ratification (ijazah) to become binding.
Without ratification, the compromise is void.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a factual scenario, classify whether the stranger's act falls into the binding or contingent category, and determine the legal consequence.
Focus on what the stranger did, not what the defendant said.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وأما إذا أضافه إلى ماله ؛ فلأنّ الإضافة إلى نفسه إلتزام منه للتسليم إلى المدعي ، وهو قادر على ذلك، فيجب عليه تسليمه، فيصح لتمام رضاه به ، وأما إذا أشار إلى نقد أو عرض بلا نسيئة إلى نفسه ؛ فلأنَّ المعرَّف المشار إليه كالمضاف إلى نفسه ؛ لأنه تعين للتسليم إليه بشرطه، فيتم به الصلح. وأما إذا أطلق ونقد فلأنَّ التسليم إلى المدعي يوجب سلامة العوض له، فيتم عقد الصلح ؛ لحصول مقصوده، وهو سلامة العوض للمدعي). قوله : إن أجاز...الخ ؛ لأن الأصل في العقد إنما هو المدعى عليه ؛ لأن دفع الخصومة حاصل له إلا أنَّ الفضولي يصيرُ أصيلاً بواسطة إضافة الضمان إلى نفسه. ألا ترى أنه يجبر على الأداء كما يجبر الأصيل عليه ، فإذا لم يضفه إلى نفسه بقي عاقداً من جهة المطلوب، فيتوقف على إجازته ، فإن أجاز المدعى عليه جاز، أو لزمه المصالح عليه ؛ لالتزامه باختياره، وإن ردّه بطل الصلح ، لأن المصالح لا ولاية له على المطلوب، فلا ينفذ عليه تصرفه.

Book of Compromise, Chapter on Donation in Compromise and Agency therein. "And if he does not deliver, if the defendant ratifies it, the substitute becomes incumbent upon him, otherwise it is rejected." That is, the stranger compromised on behalf of the defendant with the plaintiff, and guaranteed the substitute of the compromise, or said: I make a compromise with you for one thousand dirhams from my wealth, or this thousand, or this slave of mine, or said: I make a compromise with you for this thousand or this slave without attributing them to himself, or said loosely, and said: I make a compromise with you for one thousand dirhams and delivered it; in these cases the compromise is valid. The second: if he said loosely and did not deliver, the compromise is permissible and effective in the first four cases, and contingent in the fifth case. If the defendant ratifies it, the compromise takes effect and the subject of the compromise becomes binding on him; otherwise the compromise is void. He said: 'And if he does not deliver' means if he did not hand over the subject of the compromise to the plaintiff, nor guarantee it, nor attribute it to himself, then the compromise is contingent. If the defendant ratifies it, it takes effect and the subject is binding on him; if not, it is void. He said: 'the compromise is valid' — as for when he guaranteed the substitute, it is because what accrues to the defendant is only acquittal, and in such a situation the defendant and the stranger are equal, because nothing is handed over to the defendant, just as nothing is handed over to the stranger, yet it is permissible to stipulate the substitute upon himself, so likewise for the stranger. The purpose of this compromise is the satisfaction of the right-holder, not the satisfaction of the defendant, for he has no interest in it, and the plaintiff alone can compromise regarding matters that involve no exchange, except that he is not willing to forfeit his right for free. So if the compensation is handed over to him from a donor, it is valid, contrary to the case where the compromise is by the defendant's order, for then he does not become a donor but rather a principal. And as for when he attributes it to his wealth, it is because attributing it to himself is an undertaking from him to hand over to the plaintiff, and he is able to do so, so he must deliver it, thus it is valid due to complete satisfaction. As for when he points to cash or goods without deferment and attributes it to himself, it is because what is clearly identified by pointing is like what is attributed to himself, because it becomes specified for delivery by his condition, so the compromise is completed thereby. As for when he says loosely and delivers cash, it is because delivery to the plaintiff ensures the compensation reaches him, so the contract of compromise is completed because its purpose is achieved, which is the compensation reaching the plaintiff. He said: 'If he ratifies etc.', because the principal party in the contract is the defendant, since the removal of litigation accrues to him, but the stranger becomes a principal by means of attaching the guarantee to himself. Do you not see that he is compelled to pay just as the principal is compelled? So if he does not attribute it to himself, he remains as a contractor on behalf of the one sought, so it is contingent upon his ratification. If the defendant ratifies it, it takes effect, and the subject of the compromise becomes binding on him due to his voluntary undertaking, and if he rejects it, the compromise becomes void, because the compromiser has no authority over the one sought, so his action does not bind him. (1) See: Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, 5:40-41.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn concerning a stranger (fuduli) who enters into a compromise (sulh) on behalf of the defendant without authorization. The commentary distinguishes two categories: cases where the stranger delivers or guarantees the consideration (thereby making the compromise binding), and cases where he does not (rendering the compromise contingent on the defendant's ratification). In the first category, the stranger may guarantee the substitute, attribute the consideration to himself, physically deliver it, or point it out. In all these, the compromise is immediately valid and binding because the plaintiff's right is satisfied or the stranger has undertaken a binding obligation. In the second category, where the stranger merely says 'I make a compromise with you' without any delivery, guarantee, or attribution to himself, the compromise is suspended (mawquf) upon the defendant's ratification. If the defendant ratifies, the compromise is binding; if not, it is void. The commentary supports this with analogical reasoning (qiyas): the stranger who guarantees the substitute is likened to the defendant himself, because the defendant's only benefit from the compromise is acquittal (bara'ah), and the guarantee provides that same acquittal. The plaintiff's satisfaction is the key, not the defendant's, since the defendant has no direct interest. The commentary also explains that if the defendant had ordered the compromise, the stranger becomes an agent, not a donor.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts on behalf of another without prior authorization, in this context the stranger who makes a compromise without being the defendant or his agent.
صلح (ṣulḥ): A contract of compromise resolving a dispute or claim by mutual agreement, often involving the payment of consideration.
بدل الصلح (badal al-ṣulḥ): The consideration paid or given in a compromise, which substitutes the original claim.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification or approval by the defendant, which makes the contingent contract binding.
نقد (naqd): Delivery or payment of the consideration in cash at the time of contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the stranger's guarantee of the substitute make the compromise immediately valid?
A) Because the defendant benefits from acquittal, and the stranger is in a similar position when he guarantees.
B) Because the stranger has the authority to act on behalf of the defendant.
C) Because the plaintiff accepts the guarantee regardless of the defendant.
D) Because the guarantee is considered a separate contract.

Key Takeaway
A stranger's compromise is binding if he personally guarantees or delivers the substitute; otherwise it is contingent upon the defendant's ratification.

Settlement by a Third Party and Ratification
Explanation of the ruling on settlement by an unauthorized third party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a settlement made by an unauthorized third party (fudūlī) and explain why it is suspended on the defendant's ratification.

Core Idea
A fudūlī’s settlement binds the defendant only if he ratifies it.

When a third party steps in to settle a dispute without authorization, the legal effect depends on the defendant's approval. The Hanafi school treats such a settlement as suspended: it becomes valid and binding only if the defendant later ratifies it. This lesson unpacks the rational proof (dalīl ‘aqlī) behind this rule and distinguishes it from positions of other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a settlement (ṣulḥ) made by a fudūlī on behalf of the defendant. The ruling: it is suspended (mawqūf) until the defendant ratifies.
2
Locate the dalīl
The proof is rational: the defendant is the real party; the fudūlī has no authority over him. Therefore, the contract cannot bind the defendant without his consent.
No specific Quranic or hadith text is cited; the reasoning is based on principles of agency and liability.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The key point: if the fudūlī does not add liability to himself, he acts solely on the defendant's behalf. Since he lacks authority, the contract depends on the defendant's ratification (ijāzah). Ratification retroactively validates the contract.
The text draws an analogy: the fudūlī can be forced to pay if he guarantees the amount, similar to an original party.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
Some other schools (e.g., Shāfi‘ī) generally consider a fudūlī's contract invalid ab initio for sales and marriages. For ṣulḥ, the Hanafi view allows ratification, providing flexibility.
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ
The Hanafi position (muftā bihī) is that the settlement is valid upon ratification. This is based on the principle that retrospective approval cures the lack of prior authorization.
Later Ḥanafī authorities uphold this view.

Source Text

وإن لم ينقدِ الألف إن أجاز المدعى عليه لَزِمَهُ وإلا فلا.
الصلح ؛ لحصول مقصوده، وهو سلامة العوض للمدعي). [١]قوله : إن أجاز...الخ ؛ لأن الأصل في العقد إنما هو المدعى عليه ؛ لأن دفع الخصومة حاصل له إلا أنَّ الفضولي يصيرُ أصيلاً بواسطة إضافة الضمان إلى نفسه. ألا ترى أنه يجبر على الأداء كما يجبر الأصيل عليه ، فإذا لم يضفه إلى نفسه بقي عاقداً من جهة المطلوب، فيتوقف على إجازته ، فإن أجاز المدعى عليه جاز، أو لزمه المصالح عليه ؛ لالتزامه باختياره، وإن ردّه بطل الصلح ، لأن المصالح لا ولاية له على المطلوب، فلا ينفذ عليه تصرفه.
And if he does not pay the thousand, if the defendant ratifies, it becomes binding on him, otherwise not. Settlement is for achieving its purpose, which is the safety of the consideration for the plaintiff. [1] His statement: 'If he ratifies...etc.' because the original party to the contract is the defendant, as the removal of the dispute is achieved for him; however, the unauthorized person becomes a principal by adding liability to himself. Do you not see that he is forced to pay just as the principal is forced? So if he does not add it to himself, he remains contracting from the respondent's side, and it depends on his ratification. If the defendant ratifies, it is valid, or he becomes liable for the settlement amount due to his own commitment by choice. If he rejects, the settlement is void, because the settler has no authority over the respondent, so his action does not affect him.

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a third party (فضولي) makes a settlement on behalf of the defendant without authorization. The text clarifies that the settlement is not automatically binding; rather, it is contingent upon the defendant's ratification. The reasoning is that the original party to the dispute is the defendant, and the third party only becomes a principal if he adds liability to himself. If he does not, he acts on behalf of the defendant, and thus the contract depends on the defendant's approval. If the defendant ratifies, the settlement is valid and binding; if he rejects, it is void. This ruling is based on the principle that the settlement's purpose is to end the dispute, and that cannot be achieved without the consent of the party against whom the claim is made. The passage also notes that the third party can be compelled to pay if he guarantees the amount, similar to an original party.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A third party who acts without authorization in a contract on behalf of another.
إجازة (ijāzah): Ratification or approval by the principal after the fact.
أصيل (aṣīl): Principal party to a contract; the person who originally has authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A settlement by an unauthorized third party is not binding on the defendant until the defendant ratifies it; ratification retroactively validates the contract.

Invalidity of Sulh Involving Change in Time or Quality
Rulings on invalid sulh due to differing currency, time, or quality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a sulh becomes an exchange (mu'awadah) and explain why it is invalid under Hanafi rules of sarf and riba.

Core Idea
Sulh that changes time or quality is an exchange subject to riba rules, not a valid reduction.

This lesson examines two scenarios of invalid sulh (settlement) in debts. In the Hanafi school, if the creditor accepts a different time, quality, or currency than originally owed, the settlement becomes an exchange (mu'awadah) subject to the strict rules of sarf and riba. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Recognize the two cases: (1) deferred debt settled for half immediately, (2) black dirhams settled for white dirhams.
2
State the ruling
Both are invalid (haram) because they involve an exchange of something not originally owed.
3
Understand the reasoning (qiyas)
The settlement is analogous to sarf (currency exchange) or riba, so it must follow rules like immediate delivery.
4
Apply the principle of mu'awadah vs ibra
When both parties give something up, it's an exchange; when only the creditor reduces, it's a valid ibra.
5
Distinguish from valid sulh
Compare with valid sulh: reducing same debt of same type and time without change.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عن ألف مؤجل على نصفه حالاً، أو عن ألف سود على نصفه بيضاء لأنَّ هذا الصلح معاوضة فيكون صرفاً، فيشترط قبض الدنانير قبل الافتراق) ، (أو عن ألف مؤجل على نصفه حالاً ؛ لأنَّ وصف الحلول في مقابلة خمسمئة ، وذلك الوصف ليس بمال ، أو عن ألف سوداء على نصفه بيضاء) [1] قوله : لأن هذا الصلح ...الخ ؛ تقريره : إن الدنانير غير مستحقة بعقد المداينة ؛ لأن من له الدراهم لا يستحق الدنانير، فكان معاوضة، وهو صرف فلا يجوز تأجيله، ويمنع حمل الأجل على تأخير الحقِّ، فإنّ حقّ الطالب كان في الدراهم لا في الدنانير. [٢) قوله : لأن وصف الحلول...الخ ؛ تحريره : إن المعجل غير مستحق بعقدِ المداينة، إذ المستحق بعقد المداينة هو المؤجل، والمعجل خير من المؤجل، فقد وقع الصلح على ما لم يكن مستحقاً بعقد المداينة، فصار معاوضة، والأجـل كـان حـق المديون. وقد تركه بإزاء ما حط عنه من الدين، فكان اعتياضاً عن الأجل، وهو حرام ؛ لأنَّ الأجل صفة كالجودة، والاعتياض عن الجودة لا يجوز، فكذا عن الأجل، ألا ترى أنَّ ربا النسيئة حرام، وليس فيه إلا مقابلة المال بالأجل شبهة ؛ فلأن تحرم حقيقته أولى. والأصل فيه : إن الإحسان متى وجد من الطرفين يكون محمولاً على المعاوضة كهذه المسألة ؛ فإنَّ الدائن أسقط من حقه خمسمئة، والمديون أسقط من حقه وهو الأجل من خمسمئة الباقية، فيكون معاوضة. بخلاف ما تقدم من الصلح عن ألف على خمسمئة ؛ فإنه يكون محمولاً على إسقاط بعض الحق دون المعاوضة، فإنّ الإحسان لم يوجد إلا من طرف ربِّ الدين فقط. [٣]قوله : أو عن ألف سوداء ...الخ ؛ يعني وإن كان له ألف سوداء فصالحه على خمسمئة بيضاء لا يجوز هذا الصلح، والمراد بالدراهم السوداء ما كانت الفضة فيها أكثر من الغش. صرح به في «جامع المضمرات»، وغيره. (۱) أي لا وجه لصحة ذلك سوى المعاوضة، وبيع الدراهم بالدنانير نساء لا يجوز، لا يمكن حمله على التأخير ؛ لأن الدنانير غير مستحقة بعقد المداينة. ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(۳: ۱۹۱).
Or [a debt of] a thousand deferred in exchange for half of it payable immediately, or a thousand black dirhams in exchange for half of it white — because this sulh is an exchange, so it is a currency exchange (sarf), therefore hand-to-hand delivery of the dinars before separation is required. Or a thousand deferred in exchange for half immediately because the description of being immediate is in exchange for five hundred, and that description is not property. Or a thousand black dirhams in exchange for half white. [1] His saying: because this sulh... etc. Its explanation: the dinars are not owed by the original debt contract; the creditor of dirhams does not deserve dinars, so it is an exchange, which is sarf, so deferment is not allowed. And it prevents considering the deferment as a postponement of the right, because the creditor's right was in dirhams, not dinars. [2] His saying: because the description of being immediate... etc. Its analysis: the accelerated portion is not owed by the original debt contract; what is owed is the deferred portion. The accelerated is better than the deferred, so the sulh occurred on something not owed by the debt, thus it is an exchange. The deferment was a right of the debtor, and he left it in exchange for what was reduced from the debt, so it is a compensation for the deferment, which is forbidden; because deferment is a quality like quality (jooda), and compensation for quality is not allowed, so too for deferment. Do you not see that riba al-nasi'ah is forbidden, though there is only a resemblance of wealth against deferment? So the actual prohibition is more appropriate. The principle: whenever beneficence (ihsan) exists from both sides, it is considered an exchange, as in this case: the creditor forgave five hundred, and the debtor forgave his right of deferment for the remaining five hundred, so it becomes an exchange. Unlike the earlier case of sulh from one thousand to five hundred, which is considered a reduction of some of the right without exchange, because beneficence existed only from the creditor's side. [3] His saying: or a thousand black dirhams... etc. Means: if one has a thousand black dirhams and makes sulh for five hundred white dirhams, this sulh is not valid. Black dirhams mean those in which silver is more than alloy. Declared by al-Jami' al-Mudmarat and others. (1) Explanation: there is no basis for validity except exchange, and selling dirhams for dinars on deferred terms is not allowed; it cannot be considered a postponement because dinars are not owed by the original debt contract. See: Fath Bab al-Inayah (3:191).
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings on invalid sulh (settlement) in debts, followed by a detailed commentary. The first ruling: a creditor of one thousand dirhams deferred (muajjal) settles for five hundred dirhams payable immediately. This is invalid because the creditor was owed a specific debt (deferred dirhams), not dinars or immediate payment. The settlement involves a change in time and possibly currency, making it an exchange (mu'awadah) rather than a reduction of debt. If it were an exchange, it would be a currency exchange (sarf) requiring immediate delivery (qabd) before separation; deferment invalidates it. The second ruling: a creditor of one thousand black dirhams (lower-quality silver coins) settles for five hundred white dirhams (higher quality). This too is invalid because the creditor was owed black dirhams, not white. The settlement includes an increase in quality, which constitutes riba (usury). The commentary clarifies that when both parties give something up (the creditor reduces the amount, the debtor gives up the deferment or quality), it becomes a mutual exchange, and such exchanges are subject to the rules of sarf and riba. The principle is that if the settlement involves anything not originally owed (different time, quality, or currency), it is an exchange subject to strict rules; if it is merely a reduction of the same debt (as in the earlier example of one thousand settled for five hundred of the same type), it is a valid reduction (ibra). The passage emphasizes that the description of time or quality is not property in itself, so exchanging it for a reduction in debt is forbidden riba.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): Settlement or compromise in debt, where the creditor accepts less than what is owed.
معاوضة (mu'awadah): An exchange contract, where both parties give and receive something of value.
صرف (sarf): Exchange of gold for silver or vice versa, or currencies of the same type with different qualities.
الدراهم السوداء (al-darahim al-sawda'): Black dirhams: coins with more silver content than alloy (i.e., lower quality).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the creditor is giving up more than half the debt.
B) Because it involves an exchange of time for money, which is riba.
C) Because the debtor did not agree to the settlement.
D) Because the payment is not made in the same currency.

Key Takeaway
Sulh that changes the time, quality, or currency of the debt is not a reduction but an exchange, and it is invalid under Hanafi law as either riba or deferred sarf.

Conditional Remission of Debt: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Debt Revival
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional remission of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two intra-Hanafi positions on conditional remission of debt and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each.

Core Idea
Conditional remission: failure to pay on time revives the debt (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad) or not (Abu Yusuf).

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a scenario where a creditor offers to forgive part of a debt if the debtor pays half by a specific time. The Hanafi school agrees that the conditional remission is valid, but a split emerges over what happens if the debtor fails to pay on time. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between conditional acts that are binding versus those that are revocable, a key skill in Hanafi muamalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A creditor says to the debtor: 'Pay me half of your debt tomorrow, and you are free from the remainder.' The debtor accepts. What is the legal nature of this statement?
2
Understand the condition
The phrase 'on condition' (على أن) makes the remission contingent on timely payment. Without the word 'tomorrow', it would be an absolute remission.
The text stresses that 'tomorrow' is critical to create a conditional remission.
3
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: if not paid on time, the debt returns in full. Abu Yusuf: the debt does not return; the remission is absolute.
Both positions are recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Determine the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the creditor can reclaim the full original debt after default. Under Abu Hanifa/Muhammad, yes; under Abu Yusuf, no.
Later Hanafi fatwa often follows Abu Yusuf for practicality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

۳۱۹
[۲]
ومن أمر بأداء نصف دين عليه غداً على أنه بريء مما زاد إن قبل ووفى برئ، وإن لم يف عاد دينه لأنه يكون معاوضةً ألف سودِ بخمسمئة ، وزيادة وصف وهو البياض فلا يجوز (ومن أمر بأداء نصف دين عليه غداً" على أنه بريء ما زادَ إِن قَبلَ ووفى برئ، وإن لم يف عاد دينه) : أي إن قال : أد إلى خمسمئة غداً على أنك بري من الباقي فقبل، وأدى برى ، فإن لم يؤد خمسمئة في الغدِ عادَ دينه ، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة هو محمد ه ، وعند أبي يوسف الله لا يعود دينه
11 ] قوله : لأنه يكون ...الخ ؛ توضيحه : أنَّ الدراهم البيضاء غير مستحقة بعقدِ المداينة ؛ لأنّ مَن له الدراهم السوداء لا يستحق الدراهم البيضاء، فقد صالح على ما لا يستحق بعقد المداينة، فكان معاوضة الألف بخمسمئة ، وزيادة وصف الجودة فكان ربا. والأصل فيه : إنه متى كان الذي وقع عليه الصلح أدون من حقه قدراً ووصفاً ووقتاً، أو في أحدها فهو إسقاط للبعض واستيفاء للباقي، وإن كان أزيد منه بمعنى أنه دخل فيه ما لا يستحقه من وصف، أو ما هو بمعنى الوصف، كتعجيل المؤجل أو عن اختلاف جنس فهو معاوضة. كما في «المنح»، وغيره.

Book of Sulh, Chapter of Sulh in Debt. 319 [2] And whoever orders the payment of half of a debt due on him tomorrow on condition that he is free from the remainder — if he accepts and pays, he is free; and if he does not pay, his debt returns. Because it would be an exchange of a thousand black dirhams for five hundred white, with an increase in quality, which is not allowed. (And whoever orders the payment of half of a debt due on him tomorrow on condition that he is free from the remainder — if he accepts and pays, he is free; and if he does not pay, his debt returns). That is: if he says, 'Pay me five hundred tomorrow on condition that you are free from the rest,' and he accepts and pays, he is free. But if he does not pay the five hundred tomorrow, his debt returns. This according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, his debt does not return. [11] His saying: because it would be... etc. Explanation: the white dirhams are not owed by the original debt contract; the one who has black dirhams does not deserve white dirhams. So he made sulh on what is not owed by the debt, thus it is an exchange of a thousand for five hundred, with an increase in quality, making it riba. The principle: whenever the subject of sulh is less than the right in amount, quality, or time, or in any one of them, it is a reduction of some and receipt of the remainder; and if it is more, meaning it includes something not deserved, like a quality or something equivalent to a quality, such as acceleration of a deferred debt or difference in type, then it is an exchange. As in al-Minah and others. (1) [2] His saying: And whoever orders... etc. The picture of the issue: For example, Zayd has a thousand dirhams owed by Amr. Zayd said to Amr, 'Pay me five hundred dirhams from it tomorrow on condition that you are free from the surplus.' Amr accepted and paid, then Zayd becomes free from the surplus by agreement, because remission (ibra) can be restricted by a condition, though it cannot be suspended by a condition. The word 'ala' (on condition) makes it a condition to validate his action, even though it is used for exchange. Declared by al-Ayni and others. And if he does not pay the five hundred tomorrow, the thousand returns against him and he is not free. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. As for Abu Yusuf, the thousand does not return against him. [3] His saying: 'tomorrow' — he restricted it by saying 'tomorrow' because if he did not mention it and said 'Pay me five hundred on condition that you are free from the surplus,' then the remission is valid and the debt does not return, because it is an absolute remission, as will come. (1) Manh al-Ghaffar (2:197). (2) In Ramz al-Haqa'iq (2:168).
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage deals with a conditional remission: a creditor says to the debtor, 'Pay me half of the debt tomorrow, and you are free from the rest.' If the debtor accepts and pays on time, the remainder is remitted. If he fails to pay, the debt returns in full. This ruling from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad treats the condition as a binding promise: the remission is contingent on timely payment. However, Abu Yusuf holds that once the remission is made, it is absolute; failure to pay does not revive the debt because remission is an act of generosity that cannot be undone. The commentary also clarifies that the word 'tomorrow' is crucial; if unspecified, it becomes an absolute remission without condition. The passage also connects to the earlier principle that if the settlement involves a change in quality (e.g., white for black dirhams), it becomes an exchange subject to riba, but here the remission is conditional, not an exchange, so the dispute remains within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra): Remission or forgiveness of debt; a unilateral act by the creditor.
شرط (shart): A condition that restricts an action; here, timely payment is a condition for the remission to take effect.
أبو حنيفة (Abu Hanifa): The founder of the Hanafi school; his position in this case is that the debt returns if condition is unmet.
محمد (Muhammad (al-Shaybani)): A primary disciple of Abu Hanifa; he agrees with Abu Hanifa in this ruling.
أبو يوسف (Abu Yusuf): Another primary disciple of Abu Hanifa; he holds the debt does not return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical difference between the two Hanafi positions on conditional remission?
A) Abu Hanifa allows the condition; Abu Yusuf does not allow any condition.
B) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the debt returns if the condition fails; Abu Yusuf holds the debt does not return.
C) Abu Yusuf holds the remission is void from the start; Abu Hanifa holds it valid.
D) Both agree the debt returns, but differ on the amount.

Key Takeaway
Conditional remission of debt is valid in the Hanafi school, but the consequence of non-fulfillment is disputed: debt revival (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad) vs. absolute remission (Abu Yusuf).

6 min
Explicit Conditional Release of Debt Invalid in Hanafi Law
Ruling and ta'lil on explicit conditional ibra' invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on explicit conditional ibra' and explain why it is invalid based on the dual nature of ibra'.

Core Idea
An explicitly conditional debt release is invalid because ibra' combines tamlik (cannot be suspended) with isqat (can be suspended).

This lesson unpacks a key ruling in Hanafi muamalat: a creditor cannot validly say, 'If you pay me $500, you are free from the remaining debt.' The reason lies in the very nature of ibra' (release). Understanding this distinction between suspension (ta'liq) and restriction (taqyid) is critical for drafting valid debt forgiveness in Islamic finance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
First, locate the explicit ruling: any ibra' that is explicitly suspended (ta'liq sarih) by a future condition is invalid (batil). The condition typically uses words like 'if', 'when', or 'whenever'.
The ruling applies whether or not the condition is later fulfilled.
2
Analyze the dual nature of ibra'
Ibra' has two aspects: isqat (dropping of the right) and tamlik (ownership transfer of the debt to the debtor). Isqat can be suspended, but tamlik cannot. So an explicit condition invalidates the tamlik aspect, voiding the entire ibra'.
This is the core ta'lil (reasoning) provided in the text.
3
Distinguish ta'liq from taqyid
Explicit suspension (ta'liq) means the effect is postponed entirely until the condition occurs. In contrast, restriction (taqyid) makes the ibra' immediate but subject to a condition that, if broken, causes the debt to return. Taqyid is valid in Hanafi law.
The commentary notes that the scholar of al-Hidayah clearly separated the two.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
Statement 'If you pay me $500, you are free' is ta'liq – invalid. Statement 'I release you from debt on condition that you pay $500 now' is taqyid – valid, and the release is immediate with a returning condition.
The footnotes also mention that putting the consequence first (e.g., 'I reduce for you if you pay') may be valid as it implies immediacy.

ولو علق صريحاً كإن أديت إلى كذا، أو إذا ، أو متى لا يصح، لا يصح (ولو علق صريحاً كإن أديت إلي كذا ، أو إذا ، أو متى لا يصح) : أي إن قال : إن أديت إلي كذا، فأنت بريء من الباقي، لا يصح ) ؛ لأنَّ الأبراء" المعلق تعليقاً صريحاً لا يصح، فإنَّ الإبراء فيه معنى التمليك، ومعنى الإسقاط ، فالإسقاط لا يُنافي تعليقه بالشَّرط ، والتمليك يُنافيه، فراعينا المعنيين، وقلنا : إن كان التعليق صريحاً لا يصح، إن لم يكن صريحاً كما في الصورة المذكورة يصح فهذه التعليل عجيب، وكان المناسب أن يذكر هنا ما استدل به على ما ذهب إليه أبو حنيفة ومحمد الله في المسألة الأولى من أنّ هذا إبراء مقيّد بالشرط فيفوتُ بفواته ، فإنَّ كون الإبراء مقيداً بالشرط موجود هنا لا في المسألة الأولى، فانقلب الأمر."

And if he explicitly suspends, like 'if you pay me such and such', or 'when', or 'whenever', it is not valid. It is not valid (and if he explicitly suspends, like 'if you pay me such and such', or 'when', or 'whenever', it is not valid): meaning if he says, 'If you pay me such and such, then you are free from the remainder', it is not valid, because the release (ibra') explicitly suspended is not valid. Indeed, release (ibra') contains the meaning of ownership transfer (tamlik) and the meaning of dropping (isqat). Dropping does not conflict with its suspension by a condition, but ownership transfer conflicts with it. So we considered both meanings and said: if the suspension is explicit, it is not valid; if it is not explicit, as in the mentioned case, it is valid. As for the minor premise, it is obvious. As for the major premise: because release is a dropping, so it is not dependent on acceptance, and it contains the meaning of ownership transfer such that it is rejected by rejection. Suspension of ownership transfer by a condition, like sale and the like, is not permissible, and suspension of dropping by a condition, like divorce and manumission, is permissible. So in release which combines both meanings.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (ta'lil) from the commentary on the matn. The ruling is that if a creditor explicitly suspends the release of a debt on a future condition, such as by saying 'If you pay me such and such, you are free from the remainder', the release is invalid. The commentator explains the reasoning: ibra' has a dual nature — it is both a dropping (isqat) and an ownership transfer (tamlik). Dropping can be suspended, but ownership transfer cannot. Therefore, when the suspension is explicit, the tamlik aspect invalidates the whole act. The text then critiques this reasoning, noting that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's view in a previous case treated the ibra' as restricted (muqayyad) by a condition, not suspended (mu'allaq). The distinction between ta'liq and taqyid is crucial: ta'liq makes the effect contingent on the future condition and does not operate immediately, while taqyid makes the ibra' immediate but subject to a condition that if unfulfilled, the debt returns. The footnotes further illustrate that if the condition is phrased as a taqyid (e.g., 'I reduce for you on condition you pay'), it may be valid. The commentator emphasizes pondering this distinction. This is a pure Hanafi ta'lil based on the nature of ibra', not on direct scriptural evidence.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): A legal act whereby a creditor releases a debtor from a debt, combining elements of isqat (dropping) and tamlik (ownership transfer) in Hanafi law.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the effect of a legal act on a future condition such that the act does not operate in the present.
التقييد (al-taqyid): Restricting a legal act with a condition that does not suspend its immediate effect but makes it contingent for fulfillment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is explicit conditional release of debt (e.g., 'If you pay me, you are free') invalid in Hanafi law?
A) Because it is a form of gambling (qimar).
B) Because ibra' contains a tamlik element that cannot be suspended.
C) Because the condition is not expressed in writing.
D) Because it violates the principle of finality of contracts.

Key Takeaway
An explicitly conditional release of debt (ta'liq sarih) is invalid in Hanafi law because ibra' has a tamlik aspect that cannot be suspended; valid conditional releases must be structured as immediate grants with a revocatory condition (taqyid).

Secret vs. Public Debt Renegotiation: The Taqyid–Ta'liq Distinction
Ruling on validity of debt deferral/reduction with secret conditional statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a debtor's conditional statement to defer or reduce a debt, and distinguish valid secret agreements from invalid public ones using the taqyid–ta'liq framework.

Core Idea
A debtor's secret implicit condition makes deferral/reduction valid; public explicit wording invalidates it.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in debt law: a debtor tells the creditor secretly, 'I will not admit the debt until you defer or reduce it.' If the creditor agrees, is the agreement binding? The Hanafi school says yes—but only if the statement is made secretly and without explicit conditional wording. The same statement made publicly forces immediate payment of the full debt. Understanding why requires grasping the key distinction between taqyid (restriction) and ta'liq (suspension).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the creditor's deferment or reduction of the debt in response to the debtor's conditional statement. Determine the hukm (valid/invalid) for the two scenarios: secret and public.
The secret scenario yields a valid agreement (sahih); the public scenario invalidates it, and the full debt is due immediately.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Contrast the secret statement (sirran) with the public one (a'lana). The secrecy prevents the appearance of coercion (ikrah), making the agreement like a settlement with denial.
Public declaration is treated as coercive pressure, nullifying the consent of the creditor.
3
Apply the taqyid vs. ta'liq distinction
The debtor's words 'until you defer' are a restriction (taqyid), not an explicit condition (ta'liq). Taqyid produces an immediate effect conditioned on future performance; ta'liq suspends the effect altogether. In this case, the absence of explicit conditional wording (like 'if') makes the agreement valid.
The commentary emphasizes that if the debtor had explicitly said 'if you defer, I will admit', it would be ta'liq and invalid.
4
Note the authorities and the neglected condition
Several authorities (Mulla Miskin, authors of Al-Durar, Al-Hidayah, etc.) highlight the secrecy requirement, while others (Al-Kanz) omitted it. The Hanafi position relies on the consensus of the later scholars.
This shows the development of the madhhab over time.

And if he says to the other secretly, 'I will not admit to you until you defer it from me, or reduce it,' and he does so, it is valid upon him. And if he announces it, the debt is taken immediately. [Commentary] And if he says to the other secretly, 'I will not admit to you until you defer it from me, or reduce it,' and he does so, it is valid upon him. And if he announces it, the debt is taken immediately. We said: It is valid if he does not explicitly state the condition, and it is not valid if he explicitly states it, acting on both considerations, unlike the previous issue, because in that one there was no explicit suspension, but rather restriction. The difference between restriction (taqyid) by a condition and suspension (ta'liq) by it is established in wording and meaning. As for wording: restriction does not use the wording of the condition explicitly, while in suspension it is used. As for meaning: restricting release by a condition produces immediate release, on condition that what is restricted by it exists, while in suspension it does not occur immediately, because what is suspended by a condition is non-existent before it, so suspension is like addition to the time of the condition. [Footnote 1] His statement: 'And if he says to the other...' etc. Meaning: if the debtor says to the creditor secretly, 'I will not admit to you any property or debt that is due from me until you defer that debt from me, or reduce from me some of it,' and the creditor performs the deferment or reduction, then that deferment or reduction is permissible and binding upon him, such that after the deferment he cannot demand payment immediately, and after the reduction he cannot demand payment for what was reduced, because he is not coerced. So it becomes like a settlement with denial. If he announces what he said secretly, the money is taken from the one who admitted without deferment or reduction. What is meant by announcement is speaking about it first among people; it does not mean that after they have agreed on reduction and deferment, the settlement is not voided. The condition of secrecy was neglected by the author of Al-Kanz, and not pointed out by its commentator Al-Zayla'i, but it was pointed out by Mulla Miskin, the author of Al-Durar, author of Al-Ghurar, author of Al-Ayni, author of Al-Hidayah, and others.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a specific scenario: a debtor tells the creditor secretly, 'I will not admit you have a right until you defer it or reduce it.' If the creditor then defers or reduces, the agreement is valid and binding. However, if the debtor makes the same statement publicly, the creditor can immediately claim the debt. The commentary clarifies that the validity hinges on the absence of an explicit condition (shart) in the wording. When the debtor says 'until you defer', it is a taqyid (restriction) not a ta'liq (suspension). The distinction is crucial: taqyid produces an immediate effect conditioned on future performance, while ta'liq suspends the effect altogether. The secret nature is not a condition of validity per se, but a factor that prevents the appearance of coercion. The commentary notes that several authorities emphasize this nuance, while others like the author of Al-Kanz omitted it. This ruling is specific to Hanafi fiqh and illustrates how implicit taqyid can make a debt renegotiation valid, even when the debtor threatens non-admission.

Key Terms
سراً (sirran): Privately, away from public knowledge. In this context, it distinguishes a statement made confidentially from one made openly.
أعلن (a'lana): To declare publicly. When the debtor's conditional statement is made openly, it is treated as a form of pressure or coercion, invalidating the agreement.
تقييد (taqyid): Restriction: a conditional statement that produces an immediate effect, subject to the condition's future fulfillment. E.g., 'I will admit the debt when you defer it' (implied).
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension: a conditional statement that delays all effect until the condition occurs. E.g., 'If you defer, I will admit'—the admission and its consequences are suspended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes the debtor's secret conditional statement valid?
A) It is made in writing.
B) It is made secretly and uses implicit wording (taqyid).
C) It is made in the presence of two witnesses.
D) It is made after the debt is due.

Key Takeaway
A debtor's secret conditional statement to defer or reduce a debt, when worded as a restriction (taqyid) rather than an explicit condition (ta'liq), creates a valid and binding agreement under Hanafi fiqh.
