The Option of the Co-Creditor (Khiyar al-Sharik)
Explains khiyar for co-creditor when one partner settles debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when one joint creditor settles a joint debt with a different-genus item and explain the option granted to the other creditor.

Core Idea
When one joint creditor settles a debt with a different-genus item, the other creditor may choose between pursuing the debtor or taking half of the settlement item.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling when two people jointly own a debt against a debtor. If one partner makes a settlement (sulh) with the debtor for an item of a different type than the debt, the other partner is not automatically excluded. Instead, he is given an option to protect his interest. Understanding this option requires knowing the nature of joint debts and the principles of sulh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Two creditors jointly own a debt against a single debtor. One creditor makes sulh with the debtor, taking a garment (different genus) in exchange for his share of the debt.
The debt must be shared due to a single cause (e.g., one sale).
2
Understand the default problem
The debt is indivisible before receipt; what one partner receives is not exclusively his. The garment is better than the debt, so strict liability would require the settling partner to compensate the other with a quarter of the debt.
Strict application would harm the settling partner if the garment is worth less than a quarter.
3
Identify the option
The non-settling partner is given a choice (khiyar): either follow the debtor for his half of the debt, or take half of the garment from the settling partner, unless the settling partner guarantees a quarter of the debt.
If the settling partner guarantees a quarter of the debt, the option is blocked.
4
Understand the rationale (sirr)
Sulh is based on reduction (hatt). Forcing the settling partner to always pay a quarter would harm him. The option balances interests and prevents harm to both parties as much as possible.
The option is established by istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent harm.

"
واحدة أو أعياناً بلا تفصيل ثمن أو قيمة عين مشتركة مستهلكة، أو بدل قرض، أو دین موروث صالحه أحدهما عن نصيبه ، فإن كان على غير جنس الدين خير الشريك إن شاءَ اتَّبع المديون بحصته أو شريكه. انتهى.
فهذا جامع واضح، واعلم أنه لا بُدّ في المشاركة أن يتساويا في قدر الثمن وصفته؛ لأنهما لو باعاه صفقة واحدة على أنَّ نصيب فلان منه مئة ونصيب فلان خمسمئة، ثمّ قبض أحدهما منه شيئاً لم يكن للآخر فيه مشاركته ؛ لأن تفرق التسمية في حق البائعين كتفرق الصفقة بدليل أن المشتري له أن يقبل في نصيب أحدهما دون
الآخر.
خمسمئة
وكذا لو اشترط أحدهما أن يكون نصيبه خمسمئة بيضاً، ونصيب الآخر سوداً، لم يكن للآخر أن يشاركه فيما قبضه ؛ لأنّ التسمية تفرقت، وميزت نصيب أحدهما عن الآخر وصفاً، ولعل المصنف الله لم يلتفت إلى هذا القيد، فإنّه شرط الاشتراك، وهو في بيان حقيقته. فتأمل.
(۱) «البحر الرائق» (٧ : ٢٦٠).
Except if he guarantees a quarter of the debt. The secret behind the option: that one of the two partners is not exclusively entitled to what is received of the debt, because its division before receipt is not permissible, and what is received is better than the debt. So the requirement would be that he guarantees a quarter of the debt, and he would have no claim on the garment because he took its substitute. But sulh is based on reduction, so if we were to obligate a quarter of the debt, the person making sulh would be harmed, because the value of the entire garment may not reach a quarter of the debt. So we established for him the option between returning to the debtor for his share and taking half of what the sulh was made upon or a quarter of the debt, to ward off harm from both as much as possible. Then the author said: if he makes sulh, because if one of the two partners buys something with his share, the other does not share in it, as will be clarified. He only said 'one of the two debt owners' and placed the issue in debt, because if the sulh was about a common 'ayn, the one making sulh becomes exclusively entitled to the substitute of the sulh, and his partner has no right to share in it because it is a complete exchange; because the thing for which sulh is made is property in reality, unlike debt whose property status is based on the consequence of receipt as previously mentioned. And he only said 'on a garment' meaning that it is of a different genus than the debt, because if he made sulh with him on the same genus, the other would share in it, or return to the debtor, and the receiver has no option because it is like receipt of part of the debt. There is no difference in this sulh whether it is based on acknowledgment, silence, or denial, because the two claimants confirm that they have a debt against the defendant and what he takes in its place, and their claim is a proof against them. If the receiver wants to exclusively take it and his partner does not return to him regarding what he received, the trick is that the debtor gifts him the amount of his debt, and he discharges him from his debt, or the claimant sells a handful of raisins or similar for the amount of his share of the debt, then discharges him from the debt and takes the price of the raisins, as will be mentioned in detail, God willing. [Footnote: See Al-'Inayah (8:432) and Al-Tabyin (5:46).] His statement: 'Except if he guarantees...' etc. is an exception from his statement: 'He follows his partner's debtor or takes half the garment from his partner': meaning if the partner who made sulh guarantees a quarter of the debt, the other partner has no right to the garment. This is when the debt is shared between them, i.e., it is obligatory due to a single cause like the price of a sale in one transaction, and the price of shared property, or inherited property between them, and the value of what is jointly destroyed. For everything taken by one of the two partners, the other can follow him. The one making sulh has the option because his right is in the debt, and he has no way to the garment as previously mentioned. His statement: 'i.e.,' etc.: This is the form of shared debt, and its summary: shared debt is what is obligatory due to a single cause like the price of a sale if the transaction is one, such that [if] both were two slaves, each having one slave, and they sold in one transaction, then their price is shared, even if each slave is specific to one of them. And like the price of jointly owned property, such as if they sold a slave jointly owned between them in one transaction, and like inherited property between two, such as when their ancestor died and he had a debt against a man so they inherited it, and like the value of what is jointly destroyed. For everything one partner takes in these cases, the other partner can follow him. He said in Al-Bahr: Shared debt due to a single cause is like the price of a sale sold in one transaction, whether a single 'ayn or multiple items without specification of price, or the value of a jointly owned consumed 'ayn, or a loan substitute, or an inherited debt. If one of them makes sulh for his share, and it is of a different genus than the debt, the partner has the option: if he wishes, he follows the debtor for his share, or his partner. End. This is comprehensive and clear. Know that for participation, they must be equal in the amount and description of the price; because if they sell in one transaction on the condition that so-and-so's share is one hundred and so-and-so's share is five hundred, then one of them receives something from it, the other does not participate with him in it, because the differentiation of naming in the right of the sellers is like the differentiation of the transaction, as evidenced by the fact that the buyer may accept in one of their shares without the other. Similarly, if one of them stipulates that his share is five hundred white and the other's share is black, the other cannot participate in what he received, because the naming differentiated and distinguished one's share from the other in description. Perhaps the author did not pay attention to this condition, for he conditioned partnership, and he is in the process of explaining its reality. So contemplate. (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:260)
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of a ruling regarding sulh (settlement) when two people jointly own a debt against a debtor. The base text states that if one of the joint creditors makes a settlement with the debtor for a garment (or any item of a different genus), the other creditor has an option: he may either pursue the debtor for his share of the debt, or take half of the garment from the settling creditor, unless the settling creditor guarantees a quarter of the debt. The author explains the wisdom behind this option: since the debt is indivisible before receipt, what one partner receives in settlement is considered better than the debt itself. Strictly speaking, the settling partner should be liable for a quarter of the debt (since he received half the debt as a garment), but this would harm him if the garment is worth less than a quarter. Therefore, the law grants the option to balance interests. The author clarifies that this option only applies when the settlement is for a different genus (e.g., a garment instead of cash). If the settlement is in the same genus as the debt, the other partner automatically shares it, with no option. The passage also provides a legal trick (hila) for the settling partner to avoid participation: the debtor can gift the amount equal to the partner's share, then the creditor discharges him. Alternatively, the creditor can sell an item like raisins to the debtor for his share and then discharge him. The later part of the passage defines 'shared debt' as a debt owed due to a single cause, such as the price of goods sold in one transaction, the price of jointly owned property sold together, an inherited debt, or compensation for a jointly owned item that was destroyed. The condition for sharing is that the partners must be equal in the amount and description of the price. If the shares are differentiated in amount or quality (e.g., one gets 100, the other 500, or one gets white coins and the other black), then there is no participation. The author notes that the original text may have overlooked this condition, but it is necessary for the full understanding of partnership.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A settlement transaction where a creditor gives up part of his claim in exchange for immediate payment or property
خيار (khiyar): An option granted to the non-settling joint creditor to choose between following the debtor or taking the settlement item
دين مشترك (dayn mushtarak): A debt jointly owed to two or more creditors due to a single cause (e.g., one sale)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a joint creditor who settles a debt with a garment have to guarantee a quarter of the debt?
A) When he wants to keep the garment exclusively
B) When the garment is of the same genus as the debt
C) When the other creditor opts to take the garment
D) Never, because sulh is based on reduction

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants a joint creditor an option when his partner settles the debt with a different-genus item, balancing interests through istihsan.

intermediate
8 min
Conditions for the Option: Same Genus vs Different Genus and the Legal Trick
Clarifies conditions for the option and provides a legal trick

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when the option arises based on the genus of the settlement item and explain the legal trick (hila) for exclusive possession.

Core Idea
The option only arises when the settlement item is of a different genus than the debt; if of the same genus, the other partner shares automatically.

The option described in the previous lesson is not universal. It depends on the nature of the settlement item relative to the debt. If the item is of the same type as the debt, the non-settling partner simply shares in it; no option is needed. Additionally, the text provides a practical legal trick (hila) for the settling partner who wishes to keep the item exclusively. This lesson unpacks these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate by genus (jins)
If the settlement is for an item of a different genus than the debt (e.g., garment for cash), the non-settling partner has the option. If it is of the same genus (e.g., cash for cash), the non-settling partner automatically shares in the item, and the settling partner has no option.
The text illustrates: if the settlement is on the same genus, the receiver has no option because it is like receiving part of the debt.
2
Understand the ruling on acknowledgment, silence, or denial
The option applies regardless of whether the debtor acknowledges the debt, remains silent, or denies it, as long as both creditors confirm the debt.
This is because the creditors' claim is evidence against them.
3
Identify the hila (legal trick) for exclusive possession
If the settling partner wants to keep the settlement item exclusively without the other partner sharing, he can use a hila: the debtor gifts him the amount of his share, and he discharges the debtor. Alternatively, the creditor sells a handful of raisins (or similar) to the debtor for the amount of his share, then discharges him.
The hila avoids the option by making the transaction a gift-sale combination rather than a direct sulh.

لكن الصلح مبني على الحطّ ، فلو ألزمنا ربعَ الدِّينِ لتضرر المصالح ؛ لأنه قد لا يبلغ قيمة الثوب كله ربع الدين، فأثبتنا له الخيار بين أن يرجع على المدين بنصيبه، وبين أن يأخذ نصف ما وقع عليه الصلح أو ربع الدين، دفعاً للضرر عنهما بقدر الإمكان. ثم إن المصنف له إنما قال : لو صالح، فإنّه لو اشترى أحد الشريكين شيئاً بنصيبه لم يشارك الآخر فيه، كما سيصرح به، وإنّما قال أحد ربي دين، ووضع المسألة في الدين ؛ لأنه لو كان الصلح عن عين مشتركة يختص المصالح ببدل الصلح، وليس لشريكه أن يشاركه فيه لكونه معاوضةً من كل وجه؛ لأن المصالح عنه مال حقيقة ، بخلاف الدين فإنَّ ماليته باعتبار عاقبة القبض كما مرّ.

Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of a ruling regarding sulh (settlement) when two people jointly own a debt against a debtor. The base text states that if one of the joint creditors makes a settlement with the debtor for a garment (or any item of a different genus), the other creditor has an option: he may either pursue the debtor for his share of the debt, or take half of the garment from the settling creditor, unless the settling creditor guarantees a quarter of the debt. The author explains the wisdom behind this option: since the debt is indivisible before receipt, what one partner receives in settlement is considered better than the debt itself. Strictly speaking, the settling partner should be liable for a quarter of the debt (since he received half the debt as a garment), but this would harm him if the garment is worth less than a quarter. Therefore, the law grants the option to balance interests. The author clarifies that this option only applies when the settlement is for a different genus (e.g., a garment instead of cash). If the settlement is in the same genus as the debt, the other partner automatically shares it, with no option. The passage also provides a legal trick (hila) for the settling partner to avoid participation: the debtor can gift the amount equal to the partner's share, then the creditor discharges him. Alternatively, the creditor can sell an item like raisins to the debtor for his share and then discharge him. The later part of the passage defines 'shared debt' as a debt owed due to a single cause, such as the price of goods sold in one transaction, the price of jointly owned property sold together, an inherited debt, or compensation for a jointly owned item that was destroyed. The condition for sharing is that the partners must be equal in the amount and description of the price. If the shares are differentiated in amount or quality (e.g., one gets 100, the other 500, or one gets white coins and the other black), then there is no participation. The author notes that the original text may have overlooked this condition, but it is necessary for the full understanding of partnership.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Genus or type of property; the option depends on whether the settlement item is of a different jins than the debt.
حيلة (hila): A legal trick or stratagem to achieve a permissible result without violating the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the non-settling partner NOT have the option to take the settlement item?
A) When the settlement is on the same genus as the debt
B) When the debtor denies the debt
C) When the settling partner sells a handful of raisins
D) When the non-settling partner already received part of the debt

Key Takeaway
The option for the non-settling partner only arises when the settlement item is of a different genus; otherwise, automatic sharing applies, and a legal trick can be used for exclusive possession.
